    , Accepted 30th March 2015First published on 9th April 2015AbstractCarbothermal reduction could be employed as a facile technology for the synthesis of various novel materials, especially transition-metal-functionalized nanostructures. In particular, energy materials, such as ZnO, MnO2, and LiFePO4, combined with different carbon nanostructures have been widely synthesized via carbothermal reduction, which could be well established industrially due to its low-cost starting materials. In addition, a variety of carbon sources can be employed as comparatively low-cost carbon precursors for the synthesis of carbonaceous functional materials, such as porous carbon-coated magnetic nanoparticles (e.g., Co3O4@C, Fe3O4@C, and FeC3–C). These functional materials have great potential for use in energy and environmental applications. Carbothermal reduction methods possess some incomparable advantages, such as convenience, relatively low cost, and good repeatability, for commercial applications. However, they normally require a relatively high temperature for sustaining carbothermic reaction. Consequently, novel structures can also be derived from renewable, abundant carbon precursors. Examples include lignocellulosic biomass or other biological products derived from food or agricultural wastes (carbohydrates, cellulose, hemicellulose, lignin, chitin, inorganics, and proteins). Thermochemical conversion of biomass, including pyrolysis in an inert environment, has been developed for the production of energy and carbon materials. Furthermore, it is still a potential challenge to simultaneously produce high-quality biofuel products and synthesize value-added functionalized materials via in situ carbothermal reduction. This review will be a powerful resource for stimulating the development of sustainable metal-functionalized nanostructured materials by carbothermal reduction integrated with other advanced technologies, particularly for strengthening efforts towards novel materials for clean energy and environmental applications in the future sustainable society.Yafei ShenYafei Shen received his MSc degree (2012) from Shanghai Jiaotong University (SJTU), PR China. In this period, his research interests include energy & chemical engineering. He was awarded the excellent master dissertation in Shanghai, PR China. He received his PhD (2015) at Tokyo Institute of Technology (TokyoTech), Japan, supported by China Scholarship Council (CSC). After that, he worked as a lecturer in SJTU. Currently, he is hired as a professor in Nanjing University of Information Science & Technology (NUIST), PR China. His research interests mainly include tar and fuel gas cleaning, thermochemical conversion of wastes, and carbonaceous materials for energy and environmental application. He has authored more than 30 academic publications in the fields of energy and environment.
1. Introduction
1.1. General introduction
Carbothermic reactions involve the reduction of substances (e.g., metal oxides) using carbon sources as reducing agents. These chemical reactions are commonly conducted at temperatures of several hundred degrees celsius. Such processes are often applied for production of the elemental forms of many elements. Carbothermic reactions are not useful for some metal oxides such as those of sodium and potassium. The ability of metals to participate in carbothermic reactions can be predicted from an Ellingham diagram as shown in Fig. 1,1 which plots standard free energy changes (ΔG) per mole O2 as a function of the absolute temperature. Carbothermal reactions produce carbon oxides (i.e., CO and CO2). The facility of these conversions is ascribed to the entropy of the reaction: two solids (a metal oxide and carbon) are converted into a new solid (i.e., a metal) and a gas (i.e., CO), which has a high entropy. Heat is required for carbothermic reactions because diffusion of the reacting solids is slow otherwise. Oxidation of C to CO results in an increase in the number of gas molecules; it is accompanied by a large increase in entropy and is favored at high temperature. Conversely, oxidation of CO leaves the number of gaseous molecules unchanged; there is a small change in entropy [ΔS0 = 2.93 J (K−1 mol−1)] and the free energy almost depends on temperature. The two dotted lines intersect at 983 K, indicating that ΔG for the disproportionation reaction is zero. In addition, this diagram includes plots of ΔG for the oxidation of several representative metals. Such a diagram indicates that below 710 °C (983 K) carbon is a stronger reducing agent when it is converted into CO2 rather than CO, whereas above 710 °C the reverse is true. As reduction of metal oxides with C will occur when ΔG is negative, such a reduction becomes progressively more feasible at higher temperatures. For instance, ZnO is reduced at a relatively low temperature, whereas MgO is reduced at temperatures approaching 2000 K. However, in predicting the outcome of such reactions, a number of otherwise reasonable reductions cannot be used, as the metal forms a carbide such as Cr and Ti.
 Fig. 1  Ellingham diagram for the free energy of the formation of metallic oxides (reproduced with permission from ref. 1). 
Carbothermal reduction of iron oxides is one of the oldest technologies; the history of humans in the last 4000–5000 years is defined as originating from the beginning of the Iron Age.2 Nowadays, interest in the direct reduction of solid iron oxides is supported by its increased application for sponge iron production. It is no wonder that this process has been widely studied, similarly to the carbothermal reduction of Co, Ni and Cu oxides. Several mechanisms have been proposed to explain the interaction of two solid reactants (i.e., oxide and carbon) at temperatures below 800–1000 °C, along with the formation of a solid metal. The most widespread is the oxide reduction mechanism via gaseous intermediates including CO and CO2 in accordance with the following reactions (R1) and (R2):3 MO (s) + CO (g) → M (s) + CO2 (g)(R1) CO2 (g) + C (s) → 2CO (g)(R2)
However, there are some obstacles impeding its application. The most significant is slow (thermodynamically limited) restoration of CO at low temperatures. The others are connected with the interpretation of an autocatalytic effect observed for all these oxides and the high rate of their reduction in vacuum conditions. In this case, some alternative mechanisms have been proposed. For example, metal oxides reduction via gaseous metal carbides can occur via the interaction of metal vapor with carbon (R3) and (R4). 2MO (s) + MC2 (g) → 3M (s) + 2CO (g)(R3) M (s) + 2C (s) → MC2 (g)(R4)
This mechanism has mainly been supported by the direct observation of metal and gaseous carbide vapors by mass spectrometry. It was used for the interpretation of temporal oscillations in the kinetics of the interactions of carbon with the oxides of different elements.4–6 However, this mechanism cannot normally be applied to the reduction of iron, cobalt, nickel and copper oxides due to their low saturated pressures at reduction temperatures and the impossibility of transportation of metal vapor to carbon in line with reaction (R4). Subsequently, thermal reduction via the gaseous intermediates H2 and H2O has been proposed as follows in (R5)–(R7). MO (s) + H2 (g) → M (s) + H2O (g)(R5) C (s) + H2O (g) → H2 (g) + CO (g)(R6) MO (s) + C (s) → M (s) + CO (g)(R7)
Hydrogen in the reaction mixture derived from water vapor could be adsorbed on the carbon surface. However, some doubts have been cast on the presence of H2 in quantities which are necessary for efficient reduction, especially in a vacuum. Actually, the process of carbothermal reduction of iron, cobalt, nickel and copper oxides is a decomposition process, especially for solid-state decomposition reactions. Moreover, thermal dissociation based on dissociative evaporation of the reactant with simultaneous condensation of the low-volatility product has been invoked to interpret the kinetics of FeO, CoO, NiO and Cu2O reduction by carbon. The characteristic property of the carbothermal reduction of Fe, Co, Ni and Cu oxides is an autocatalytic development, indicating the rate of isothermal reduction accelerates as the reduction progresses, and passes through a maximum. The function of carbon in this process is to react with the oxygen liberated from the oxide decomposition, thus maintaining a low partial pressure of oxygen in the system. In other words, carbon can play a key role as a buffer in this process. Significantly, this mechanism does not work if the metal remains in the gaseous phase at the temperature of oxide reduction. As for the oxides of metal elements like Al, Cr, Mg, Mn, Sn, Zn, etc., carbothermal reduction most likely occurs via the gaseous carbide mechanism.5,6
1.2. Carbothermal synthesis of materials
Carbothermal reduction can be used as a facile technology for the synthesis of various materials, especially transition-metal-functionalized nanostructures. In particular, energy materials such as ZnO, MnO2, and LiFePO4 combined with different carbon nanostructures have been widely synthesized via carbothermal reduction, which could be well established industrially due to its low-cost starting materials. In addition, a variety of carbon sources can be used as comparatively low-cost carbon precursors for the synthesis of carbonaceous functional materials, such as porous carbon magnetic nanoparticles (e.g., Co3O4@C, Fe3O4@C, and FeC3–C). These functional materials have great potential for use in energy and environmental applications. Carbothermal reduction approaches possess some incomparable advantages such as convenience, relatively low cost, and good repeatability for industrial applications. Moreover, the development of more flexible routes to new carbonaceous materials derived from renewable resources has only recently become a topic of significant interest. It is appropriate to note that materials researchers can draw inspiration from a variety of natural processes and structures to produce various complex nanostructured functional materials, which are required to address the energy and chemical challenges of a future sustainable society. Significantly, new structures could also be derived from renewable, abundant carbon precursors. Examples include lignocellulosic biomass or other products contained in food/agricultural wastes (i.e., carbohydrates, cellulose, hemicellulose, lignin, chitin, inorganics, proteins, etc.). In recent years, thermochemical conversion of biomass (e.g., pyrolysis) has been developed rapidly for energy and carbon materials production. It has still been a major challenge to simultaneously produce high-quality energy and synthesize value-added functional materials. Considering the lack of a relevant comprehensive report, this review aims to survey recent progresses in the carbothermal synthesis of commonly used metal nanostructures (especially for the non-precious transition metals in black characters in Fig. 2). Besides, other metal nanostructures (e.g., Sn and Sb) are included for energy and environmental applications.
 Fig. 2  Transition metals in the periodic table of elements. 
2. Carbothermal reduction
2.1. Direct carbothermic reduction
The carbothermic reduction process is a mature technology using carbon to directly reduce metal oxides (ores) to produce metals such as iron in blast furnaces, as well as metallurgical grade silicon. The same process can be used to reduce minerals containing various metallic oxides in the lunar regolith to produce oxygen. Ostrovski et al. and Kononov et al.7,8 used carbothermic reduction to extract oxygen from the regolith by high-temperature reaction of oxides with a carbon source and recover oxygen in a multi-step process including three basic steps. The first step is carbothermic reduction of metal oxides in minerals (e.g., ferrous, silicon, titanium oxides, etc.) which are mostly reduced by a carbon source (e.g., methane) to form carbon monoxide and hydrogen. Subsequently, carbon monoxide is reduced by reacting with hydrogen to form water and methane, which is recycled. Finally, the generated water could be electrolyzed to form oxygen and hydrogen. Therefore, this closed cyclic process does not depend on the presence of water precursors in the lunar materials. It produces oxygen from ilmenite and silicate minerals in the lunar regolith regardless of their precise composition and fine structure. Fig. 3 shows integrated carbothermic reduction of the regolith, where carbothermal tests were conducted using a 440 W CO2 laser. The equivalent oxygen yields are up to 28%. Moreover, the carbon loss in the processed regolith with these oxygen yields is typically very low (<15%).
 Fig. 3  Integrated system for the carbothermic reduction of regolith for field tests (taken with permission from ref. 7). 
Sun et al.9 reported a simple carbothermal reduction route for the production of SnO2 nanobelts and nanowires. Fig. 4a–d show SEM images of the synthesized SnO2 nanostructures on substrates at different deposition temperatures. Fig. 4e shows the formation temperatures. SnO2 nanobelts grew mainly in the temperature range 1000–850 °C. The typical widths of the nanobelts formed in region I (1000–950 °C) are in the range 1–3 μm, with lengths ranging from tens to hundreds of micrometers, even up to millimeters (Fig. 4a). SnO2 nanowires grew mainly in the temperature range 850–600 °C. The diameters of the nanowires formed in region III (850–750 °C) normally range from 100 nm to 300 nm, with lengths of several tens of micrometers (Fig. 4c). The substrate temperature and the oxide vapor concentration are two significant parameters for forming different morphologies and sizes of SnO2 nanostructures via the reduction of SnO2 powders. The SnO2 nanostructures were synthesized by carbothermal evaporation. Based on the vapor–solid mechanism,10 the growth mechanism can be explained by (R8)–(R10). 2SnO2 (s) + C (s) → 2SnO (v) + CO2 (v)(R8) 2SnO (v) → SnO2 (s) + Sn (l)(R9) 2Sn (l) + O2 (v) → 2SnO (v)(R10)
 Fig. 4  SEM images of SnO2 nanostructures: (a) wide nanobelts; (b) narrow nanobelts, inset showing a closer view of one curved SnO2 nanobelt; (c) thick nanowires; (d) thin nanowires. (e) Schematic of the formation temperatures (reproduced with permission from ref. 9). 
This direct carbothermal reduction method requires neither complex apparatus nor metal catalysts11 or templates, whereas it does need a high reaction temperature or other relatively strict conditions. SnO2 nanowires could also be synthesized by a carbothermal reduction process at a lower temperature of 800–850 °C from a SnO precursor at normal atmospheric pressure employing CuO as a catalyst.11 Firstly, CuO powder was reduced by active carbon powder at 700 °C to yield Cu2O and CO2(R11). Commonly, the phase transformation from SnO to SnO2 in an ambient atmosphere may occur at temperatures as low as 370 °C,12 but in a powder form not a nanoform if heated alone. Thus, Cu2O acted as a catalyst for transforming SnO into SnO2 in a nanoform at 850 °C by the reaction between Cu2O and SnO (R12). The SnO2 nanoparticles acted as nucleation sites and initiated the growth of SnO2 nanowires by means of the vapor–solid mechanism.13,14 CuO could be regenerated via(R13) and (R14) by oxidation. 4CuO (S) + C (S) → 2Cu2O (s) + CO2 (g)(R11) SnO (S) + Cu2O (S) → SnO2 (s) + 2Cu (s)(R12) 2Cu (S) + O2 (g) → 2CuO (s)(R13) 2Cu2O (S) + O2 (g) → 4CuO (s)(R14)
2.2. Carbothermal hydrogen reduction
The temperature and energy required for the conversion of a metal oxide into the pure metal are defined by the reaction thermodynamics and kinetics. A major reductant for metal oxides is carbon in the form of coke or char. Carbothermal reduction of stable metal oxides such as MnO, Cr2O3, SiO2, TiO2 and Al2O3 requires relatively high temperatures. Nevertheless, the efficiency of carbothermal reduction processes could be improved by decreasing the reduction temperature and improving the reaction kinetics. This can be achieved by decreasing the CO partial pressure or increasing the mass transfer in the gas phase by running the process in an inert atmosphere or in hydrogen to aid the reduction reaction. At low temperatures, reduction of oxides takes place in the solid state. It is well known that carbothermal reduction of metal oxides in the solid state occurs via the gas phase. When carbothermal reduction takes place in the presence of hydrogen, methane is formed by means of carbon reacting with hydrogen (R15). This changes the mechanism of reduction, which then proceeds via(R16). In addition, the reduction of the metal oxide to the carbide (i.e., MC) can be represented by (R17). C (s) + 2H2 (g) → CH4 (g)(R15) MO (s) + 2CH4 (g) → MC (s) + CO (g) + 4H2 (g)(R16) MO (s) + 3CO (g) → MC (s) + 2CO2 (g)(R17)
In (R1) and (R2), or (R17) and (R2), carbon and oxygen are transferred between solid phases by CO and CO2, respectively. In the reduction of stable metal oxides similar to manganese or titanium oxides, the CO2 partial pressure is very low, below 10−4 atm (subject to the applicable temperature); therefore, mass transfer could be a limiting step for the reaction rate. In (R6) and (R7), carbon is transferred between a solid carbonaceous material and an oxide by CH4 with the formation of CO and H2, which are transferred to the gas phase. The partial pressure of CH4 is much higher than the partial pressure of CO2, allowing the carbothermal reduction of manganese and titanium oxides in a H2 atmosphere to occur at a much faster rate. The reduction temperature can be decreased by 300–400 °C in the carbothermal reduction of manganese, titanium (ilmenite), and aluminium oxides. Furthermore, control of the gas atmosphere in reactions is an important factor in the development of carbothermal reduction of metal oxides. Gas species are directly involved in carbothermal reactions, so the composition of the gas phase affects the reaction rate. Fig. 5 shows reduction curves for MnO2 obtained in hydrogen, helium and argon. Reduction is much faster in hydrogen than in helium, and faster in helium than in argon.8
 Fig. 5  Carbothermal reduction curves of MnO2 at 1275 °C in H2, He and Ar (taken with permission from ref. 8). 
In general, the carbothermal hydrogen reduction involves two successive steps: (1) reduction of a metal oxide precursor (i.e., MoO3, WO3) by hydrogen and (2) reaction between partially reduced metal oxides (i.e., MoO2, WO2) and surface carbon atoms of carbon materials under a hydrogen atmosphere.15–17 Nanostructured β-Mo2C was formed on carbon materials with an ultra-high surface area by carbothermal hydrogen reduction at 700 °C. The particle size of β-Mo2C increased with an increase in reaction temperature.16 Likewise, tungsten carbides (e.g., W2C) nanostructures were synthesized with a size range 5–12 nm on a carbon material with ultra-high surface area by carbothermal hydrogen reduction at 850 °C and promotion by metal Ni at 650 °C.17 Besides, activated carbon-supported bimetallic CoMo carbides were successfully synthesized by carbothermal hydrogen reduction at around 600 °C. The addition of a second metal such as Co and Ni decreases the formation temperature of a carbide because the second metal promotes the formation of CHx species from reactive carbon atoms or groups on the carbon material and hydrogen, which further carburizes the oxide precursors.18 Furthermore, Li et al.19 fabricated Mo2C catalysts on multi-walled carbon nanotubes by carbothermal hydrogen reduction. As a support and a carbon source, the temperature for Mo2C formation on multi-walled carbon nanotubes is 580 °C, which is much lower than with other conventional carbon supports. It is noteworthy that the metal carbides (i.e., Mo2C, W2C) synthesized by carbothermal hydrogen reduction have extensive characteristics for use as adsorbents, catalysts, electronic materials, etc.20–27
2.3. Carbothermal chemical vapor deposition (CTCVD)
One-dimensional (1D) nanostructures such as nanowires, nanotubes, and nanoribbons have become a subject of intensive research on their synthesis, properties, characterization and applications, due to their potential use as building blocks in making nanoscale electrical or other devices.28–32 Currently, vapor deposition technologies have been employed for fabrication of these nanostructures, such as physical vapor deposition (PVD) and chemical vapor deposition (CVD) with its two typical growth modes, vapor–solid (VS) and vapor–liquid–solid (VLS), respectively.33–35 For instance, ZnO nanowires can provide a direct band gap (3.37 eV) and a large exciton binding energy (60 meV), which make them suitable for a wide range of device applications, e.g., optoelectronic and sensor devices.36–39 ZnO nanowires can be fabricated by vapor deposition techniques. Fig. 6 shows a schematic of a typical experimental setup for CVD ZnO nanowires growth, consisting of a source boat and substrates in a two-zone growth system. A ZnO/C (e.g., graphite) powder mixture in a source crucible is evaporated at high temperatures (e.g., 950 °C). The Zn (g) atoms produced by carbothermal reduction are transported in a CVD chamber to the substrate for deposition.
 Fig. 6  Schematic of a typical experimental setup for CVD ZnO nanowires growth, consisting of a source boat and substrates in a 2-zone growth system. The heating zones can be independently well controlled, enabling a reliable and homogeneous growth to several centimeters (reproduced with permission from ref. 34). 
However, ZnO nanowires grown by thermal CVD typically require a metal catalyst (e.g., Au) on the substrate surface, as it initiates and guides the growth. Here, the main mechanisms of ZnO nanowires growth are the VS and catalyst-assisted growth (i.e., VLS) mechanisms. In the VS process, Zn atoms could condense on a solid catalyst (e.g., Au) at a lower temperature and preferential deposition occurs on energetically favored nucleation sites.40,41 The Zn atoms move due to surface diffusion and the ZnO nanowires grow with a preferential orientation (typically a c-orientation) by self-organization.42 In the case of VS growth, the nanowires have a buried Au dot or Au at their root. In the VLS process, a thermally evaporated growth species (i.e., Zn) is transported to the metal catalyst and is subsequently adsorbed and diffuses to form a liquid alloy. Accordingly, its concentration exceeds its solubility and ZnO nanowires are precipitated. Growth through supersaturation and precipitation occurs only in appropriate growth conditions and until the source of the growth species is exhausted.42–44 In the case of VLS growth, the nanowires typically exhibit an Au dot on their tip. Moreover, the diameter of VLS nanowires can be controlled by the size of the Au dot.45 Control of the diameter in the case of VS nanowires can be tuned via the substrate temperature. At higher substrate temperatures, the nanowires tend to grow with smaller diameters and larger lengths, whereas lower substrate temperatures yield increased diameters and shorter lengths. This effect can mainly be attributed to the growth kinetics.46,47
Menzel et al.48,49 employed one-zone and two-zone heating furnaces for the controlled growth of ZnO nanostructures to distinguish between the VS and VLS growth modes. The transport properties and species distribution in thermal CVD were previously investigated for one-zone and three-zone growth systems. It has been demonstrated that the controlled growth of VS and catalyst-assisted-grown (i.e., VLS) ZnO nanowires can occur in a CVD reactor by the carbothermal reduction method based on either upstream or downstream deposition techniques.49 In addition, proper tuning of the pressure and carrier gas flow has an impact on the nanowire growth position in a growth reactor. Controlling the diffusion and convection of growth species enables growth in either upstream or downstream positions.48,50 Moreover, control of the O2 flux is crucial because it determines how much O2 is available for the reaction of Zn to ZnO in upstream and/or downstream directions. In addition, O2 might be consumed at the source position, where the carbothermal reduction occurs. If the O2 flux is rather low, upstream nanowires growth might occur based on Zn gas diffused upstream, while the remaining O2 is consumed at the powder source, leaving insufficient O2 at the downstream position, where only a Zn-rich film is grown.50 On the other hand, if the O2 flux is too concentrated, the oxidation environment suppresses nanowires formation in the upstream position; nevertheless, after passing through the position of the source powder, the reduced O2 concentration at the downstream position is high enough for ZnO nanowires growth.50 A different approach to control switching between the VS and VLS growth modes was the addition of an extra carbon source consisting of ionic liquids (ILs) inside the growth reactor. The growth area was drastically increased to centimeter ranges, even in a one-zone oven for VLS-grown nanowires, by adding ILs. It is noted that a one-zone furnace typically offers a large temperature gradient and nanowires only grow at a certain position with appropriate conditions; therefore, the growth range is limited. A step involving cleaning the tube by HCl enables complete switching back to the VS growth mode.51

Fig. 7 provides a schematic diagram of the most significant parameters and how they can affect the growth mechanism in nanowires grown by VS and catalyst-assisted growth (i.e., VLS). For thermal CVD growth, the key parameters, e.g., chamber pressure, carrier gas transport, source temperature, substrate temperature, and concentration gradients of the reaction gas, can affect the morphology of the grown nanostructures. For controlled nanowire growth, gas leakage must be prevented since it represents an additional source of oxygen; therefore, initial pumping to a relatively low base pressure in a tightly sealed system is significantly required. Moreover, the pumping system and tube geometry (i.e., tube diameter) play crucial roles in setting suitable growth parameters.
 Fig. 7  Schematic diagram of the key parameters controlling nanowire growth (reproduced with permission from ref. 49). 

Fig. 8 is a shape diagram for variations in the Ar flow and the substrate temperature and shows the morphologies for different parameters at the upstream position in a 1 in. one-zone furnace system. The O2 flow rate is constant at 0.01 sccm. Below a 10 sccm Ar flow, corresponding to a <0.1 vol% O2 concentration, nanostructure formation could be observed due to Zn species diffusion in the upstream direction, and a sufficiently high Zn species concentration forms nanostructures.48 Higher flow rates only cause a Zn/Au catalyst structure, which is ascribed to the low concentration of Zn vapor in the upstream position (gray area). Generally, if the carrier gas flow rate is too high in the upstream configuration (i.e., >10 sccm), the Zn concentration is not sufficient for the nucleation and growth of ZnO structures. By increasing the Ar flow further, a shift in nanostructure formation to the downstream position is caused, due to the high flow. The temperature is lower than 700 °C, and only a ZnO film forms on the substrate surface (Fig. 8b). Increasing the substrate temperature leads to the formation of VS-grown ZnO nanorods (Fig. 8a). A further increase in the substrate temperature leads to creation of an optimum growth area, which contains VS-grown nanowires (Fig. 8d). However, their average length and diameter can be controlled by the substrate temperature and deposition time. The temperature is high enough, so catalyst-assisted growth can be observed due to diffusion of Zn atoms on the Au catalyst and supersaturation (Fig. 8c). Furthermore, there is still an oxidizing environment that rather leads to intermixing of VS and VLS growth of nanowires. Temperatures beyond 880 °C at a 5 sccm carrier gas flow rate lead only to the formation of a thin ZnO film.
 Fig. 8  Shape diagram for a 1 inch one-zone reactor system in the upstream growth direction configuration: (a) ZnO nanorod formation, (b) ZnO film formation, (c) the growth region for VLS ZnO nanowires, and (d) the optimum growth region for VS ZnO nanowires (reproduced with permission from ref. 49). 
In contrast, Fig. 9 shows a shape diagram generated for the downstream position in a 2 in. two-zone system. At a low substrate temperature (up to 650 °C) and a high substrate temperature (900 °C) with a relatively low Ar flow rate (0–5 sccm), shown by A1 (lower substrate temperature) and A2 (higher substrate temperature), only a ZnO film was formed due to the very high Zn vapor concentration inside the reactor. Increasing the flow rate to 50 sccm at low substrate temperatures (<650 °C) can lead to the formation of a thick ZnO film with slight nucleation of ZnO nanorods (B area). Compared to the same temperature with higher flow rates (70 and 100 sccm), the growth of ZnO at lower flow rates is preferably on the surface rather than vertically from the surface, indicating that growth with preferential orientation cannot occur at lower flow rates. This could be the effect of higher wettability of Zn liquid during deposition before oxidation. A higher flow rate might encourage more complete oxidation during deposition. A higher Ar flow rate (>50 sccm) dilutes the O2 and Zn concentrations far enough to produce VS-grown ZnO nanowires (C area). This VS growth area extends over the entire range of Ar flow rates up to 825 °C, showing that the kinetics and Zn concentration are appropriate for forming VS-grown ZnO nanowires at these temperatures. By tuning the substrate temperature and the carrier gas flow, the geometry in terms of length and diameter is controllable over the entire range of the C area. In the range of temperatures 825–950 °C and Ar flow rates 5–35 sccm in the D area, the thin ZnO film contains nuclei with Au on the tip, which is attributed to catalyst-assisted nanostructures starting to form. At higher flow rates (>35 sccm), intermixing of catalyst-assisted (dominant morphology) and VS-grown nanowires (E area) is observed. At such high flow rates, the vapor concentration is low enough to facilitate catalyst-assisted growth.52Fig. 8 (upstream configuration) and Fig. 9 (downstream configuration) indicate that dilution of Zn at a respective substrate temperature forms similar nanostructures. In general, a decrease in the diameter and increase in the length of nanowires when decreasing the temperature in VS conditions is attributed to slow growth kinetics due to temperature-dependent diffusion conditions. In typical growth conditions, the Zn vapor is undersaturated, nucleating at energetically suitable accommodation sites such as Au-coated regions or ZnO buffer layers/seeds. Furthermore, Saunders et al.53 suggested that growth proceeds via condensation of Zn at such sites and subsequent oxidation, which is consistent with the evidence that metal catalysts or pre-deposited ZnO nucleation points were generally required for growing ZnO nanostructures via carbothermal vapour phase transport.
 Fig. 9  Shape diagram of a two-zone reactor system in downstream growth direction configuration: (A1) and (A2) ZnO film formation, (B) a mixture of ZnO film formation with nanowire nucleation points, (C) the optimum growth region for VS-grown ZnO nanowires, (D) intermixing growth of VS and VLS ZnO nanowires, and (E) the growth region for VLS ZnO nanowires (reproduced with permission from ref. 49). 
CVD methods have successfully been employed to fabricate ZnO nanowires, but there still exists some scope to develop methods for ZnO nanowires production in large quantities. Therefore, Zhou et al.54 developed a modified carbothermal reduction method for the rapid mass production of ZnO nanowires. In this study, the synthesis was carried out at atmospheric pressure without using any catalysts and air was employed as an oxygen source. Mixed ZnO and graphite powder were used as source materials and the reaction time was about 5 min. The flask and the prepared ZnO nanowires are shown in Fig. 10A. The yield of the prepared ZnO nanowires was about 85 wt% of the source ZnO powder. It was found that the product was composed of randomly aligned nanowires (Fig. 10B). Moreover, the edges of the nanowires were smooth and no catalyst particles were observed on the edges of the nanowires (Fig. 10C). The ZnO nanowires exhibited a hexagonal wurtzite crystal structure. The average length and diameter of the ZnO nanowires were about 1.8 μm and 35 nm, respectively. Furthermore, the ZnO nanowires displayed a polycrystalline nature with uniform structures (Fig. 10D and E).
 Fig. 10  (A) Digital image of the prepared ZnO nanowires and the flask for collecting the prepared product; typical low (B) and high (C) magnification SEM images of the prepared ZnO nanowires; (D) TEM image of ZnO nanowires; (E) high-resolution TEM image of a single ZnO nanowire (reproduced with permission from ref. 54). 
Cheng et al.55 synthesized hierarchical nanostructures with a SnO2 backbone and ZnO branches by combining carbothermal reduction with hydrothermal growth. The hybrid SnO2–ZnO nanostructures exhibited an enhanced near-band-gap emission compared to pure SnO2 or ZnO nanowires. Furthermore, Jin et al.56 synthesized ZnO-core/SnO2-shell nanorods via a combination of thermal evaporation and atomic layer deposition. These also exhibited a high near-band-edge emission intensity for a shell thickness of 15 nm, which was attributed to the formation of a sub-wavelength optical resonant cavity in the nanorods. Most hybrid ZnO/SnO2 nanostructures have been made by a two-step method at low pressure, where each oxide is synthesized by a carbothermal reduction method sequentially. For instance, Hwang et al.57 synthesized ZnO/SnO2 core–shell nanowires via a two-step carbothermal reduction method with a system pressure around 10−2 Torr, whereas Lan et al.58 produced hierarchical nanostructures at a pressure of 1 Pa. Tharsika et al.59 synthesized ZnO/SnO2 nanowires by a single-step carbothermal reduction based on the catalyst-assisted VLS mechanism. Here, activated carbon powder acted as a reducing agent. In this synthetic process, metal nanoparticles or nanoclusters were used as nucleation seeds, which determined the growth direction, interfacial energy and diameter of the one-dimensional metal oxide nanowires.60 In this facile method, the morphology and properties of nanowires are mainly controlled by growth parameters such as temperature, thickness of the catalyst layer, rate of carrier gas flow, and distance between the source and the substrate.61 Different types of nanostructures are obtained at different locations on an Au/alumina substrate. ZnO/SnO2 nanowires possess a comb-like branched structure in Fig. 11A. In this structure, the diameter of the backbone nanowire is 80 nm and the branched nanowires exhibit a uniform diameter of 30 nm. Fig. 11B shows the morphology of the ZnO/SnO2 hierarchical nanostructure. Importantly, the secondary branches from backbone nanowires are significant. The ends of these secondary branches have Au tips, indicating that the VLS mechanism is operative here. Au can play a significant role as a catalyst for growing branched and hierarchical nanowires.59 Hierarchical nanostructures consist of a trunk and branches, which are composed of mixed ZnO/SnO2 material. However, controlled synthesis of these kinds of nanostructures is still a challenge. It has been suggested that the backbone nanowire grew quickly with an Au catalyst particle at its tip, while smaller alloy droplets were formed on the backbone nanowire surface, leading to hierarchical nanowires in different locations.62 The growth of hierarchical nanowires is triggered as the critical values of temperature and vapor supersaturation ratio are reached. In general, catalyst-assisted growth of nanowires on an alumina substrate constitutes a promising method for growing nanowires over large areas.
 Fig. 11  FESEM images of ZnO/SnO2 nanowires: (A) comb-like and (B) hierarchical nanostructures; (C) FETEM image of SnO2/ZnO nanostructures after 2 h deposition; (D) FESEM and (E) TEM images of SnO2/ZnO core–shell nanowires fabricated for 0.5 h (reproduced with permission from ref. 59 and 60). 
Recently, Tharsika et al.63 employed carbon-assisted thermal evaporation as a vapor-phase growth method for SnO2/ZnO nanostructures on various substrates. In the vapor-phase growth, nanostructures could grow via two common VLS and VS mechanisms. SnO2/ZnO core–shell nanowires and hierarchical nanostructures were fabricated on top of a quartz boat loaded with the precursor by a catalyst-free one-step synthetic route based on the VS mechanism.63 The SnO2/ZnO nanostructures exhibited two types of structures depending on the deposition time. Only regular nanowires were observed at a shorter growth time (e.g., 0.5 h). When the growth time increased, e.g., to 2 h, the nanowires exhibited hierarchical nanostructures (Fig. 11C). The nanowires had a diameter of 50–100 nm and a length of several tens of micrometers. Evidently, no catalyst tips appear at the ends of the nanowires (Fig. 11D). Therefore, the growth of nanostructures is in accordance with the VS mechanism. The regular nanowires observed at a shorter growth time of 0.5 h exhibited a SnO2/ZnO core–shell structure. The diameter of the core was found to be about 50 nm and the thickness of the shell was about 17 nm (Fig. 11E). This special process allows different types of nanostructures to be prepared and collected by selecting the deposition time.
3. Carbon sources
In the widest sense, any object which is, at least in one dimension, less than 1 μm long can be considered a nanostructure.64 One-dimensional (1-D) nanostructures such as rods, wires, belts and tubes have attracted much attention due to their unique properties and the possibility that they may be used as building blocks for electronics and photonics, as well as life-science applications.65–69 As a special example, the synthesis of ZnO nanowires by carbothermal reduction with growth methods has been widely studied and discussed. However, particles <100 nm in at least two dimensions are commonly referred to as nanoparticles. It is noted that metal nanoparticles (e.g., metal carbides, metal oxides, metal borides, and zero-valent metals) could be synthesized by carbothermal reduction. In general, the carbon sources used for carbothermal synthesis include carbon black, activated carbon, graphite, biochar, charcoal, organic polymers (e.g., sol–gel), biopolymers (e.g., cellulose), sugars (e.g., glucose), and light hydrocarbons (e.g., CH4, C2H2, C3H6).70–72 The properties of carbon sources, for instance, their surface characteristics and impurities, might affect the synthesis of nanostructures by carbothermal reduction.
Carbothermal reduction is popular for production of ZnO nanostructures, as it allows control of the Zn vapor pressure via the reaction temperature.73 Carbothermal reduction-vapor phase transport growth of ZnO nanostructures is usually carried out at temperatures above 900 °C. It has been found that other carbon sources, such as graphite, carbon black, charcoal, single-walled carbon nanotubes, multi-walled carbon nanotubes, and activated carbon can be used to grow ZnO nanostructures at lower temperatures.74–79 Lower purity in the carbon source, as well as its surface area, have an effect on factors such as differences in the free energy of carbon sources and effects on the reaction energetics.79 In addition, the surface activity of non-graphite carbon sources (originating in edges and defects of the carbon layers and the presence of heteroatoms such as oxygen, hydrogen, sulfur, and nitrogen, which introduce active sites onto the carbon surface) is known to play a considerable role in the surface chemistry of these materials.80 The surface activity is expected to vary with the surface area for otherwise identical materials, but is not correlated solely with changes in the surface area. Moreover, the density of active sites is strongly dependent on material preparation methods. Here, different surface activities depend on differences in preparation.80 Finally, the purity levels available in non-graphite carbon sources are often, or even generally, inferior to the purity levels available in graphite, and impurities affect nanostructures growth. For instance, an indium impurity contaminating the growth system could alter the growth morphology and alignment of nanostructures in carbothermal reduction-vapor phase transport growth.81 The requirement to ensure reproducibility and control of morphology is an important consideration with respect to unexpected high-temperature effects. Therefore, appropriate selection of the carbon source is a key factor. Nanostructures can be grown at significantly lower temperatures using carbon black and activated carbon, even with different morphologies compared to graphite. The surface areas of carbon black and activated carbon are higher than that of graphite; this is used to explain the origin of such differences in growth and morphology.
Biswas et al.78 therefore used carbothermal reduction-vapor phase transport to grow ZnO nanostructures at different temperatures using carbon black, graphite, and activated carbon powders as carbon sources. Fig. 12 shows SEM images of the synthesized ZnO nanostructures in the Au-deposited region. Differences in growth and morphology are observed for the different carbon sources, which are especially visible for growth at 750 and 800 °C, although the morphology for growth at 850 °C is quite different, with substantially longer, generally narrower, higher-density, and less well-aligned nanostructures grown using carbon black compared to graphite. Generally, differences in ZnO nanostructures prepared with different carbon sources cannot only be explained by differences in the surface areas, thermodynamic effects, or purity of the source; in addition, differing surface activities of the carbon powders are responsible. Using different carbon powders, growth cannot occur at much lower temperatures and different morphologies, including smaller nanowire diameters, are obtained in certain temperature regions. However, the negative side of achieving this diversity is that reproducible deposit yields and morphologies are more difficult to achieve with carbon powders whose chemical properties vary, especially in terms of more variable surface activities. Obviously, there is a trade-off between the relative advantages of lower-temperature growth using non-graphite powders, which is able to provide a diversity of nanostructures and a range of available substrates, and driving to develop industrial applications of the ZnO nanostructures, for which reproducibility and scalability are key considerations. For the latter, the use of graphite powder may be preferred.
 Fig. 12  FESEM images of ZnO nanostructures grown using carbon black, graphite and activated carbon at 750 °C, 800 °C, and 850 °C, respectively (reproduced with permission from ref. 78). 
Relatively high-purity carbon sources such as carbon black, graphite, and activated carbon can be used for the synthesis of zinc electronic nanostructures (e.g., ZnO nanowires). Interestingly, at certain temperatures, no growth at all was found using graphite with a lower total surface area, whereas nanostructures were grown using the other carbon sources. The reason is that the higher total surface area of non-graphite carbon sources can lead to an increased release of Zn vapor which is responsible for nanostructure growth, but the lower purity of the carbon source affects the growth as well. It is noted that the involvement of heteroatoms in surface activity could be described as an impurity effect; however, the key point is that the presence of impurities alone is not the origin of the effects, but rather the specific binding to the carbon layers of such heteroatoms during synthesis of the carbon, creating active carbon sites (in addition to intrinsic effects such as edges and defects of the carbon layers), thereby enabling carbothermal reduction. The presence of heteroatoms in the condensing vapor stream could result in changes in the nanostructure morphology.
Hitherto, a variety of one- or two-dimensional ZnO nanostructures have been synthesized, in the various forms of nanodots, nanorods, nanowires, nanobelts, nanotubes, nanobridges, nanonails, nanowalls, nanohelices, seamless nanorings, mesoporous single-crystal nanowires, and polyhedral cages (Fig. 13).82 Also, intensive researches have focused on fabricating one-dimensional ZnO nanostructures and on correlating their morphologies with their size-related optical and electrical properties.78 However, transition-metal nanostructures are not limited to the aforementioned applications. For instance, some nanostructures, especially metal carbide or oxide nanoparticles with two-dimensional structures, have been extensively fabricated via carbothermal reduction for catalysts and environmental functionalized materials. These applications have relatively low requirements for the carbon sources in carbothermal synthesis. Consequently, sustainable carbon sources, such as biopolymers (e.g., cellulose), sugars (e.g., glucose and sucrose) and biomass have been extensively used.83–87
 Fig. 13  Typical ZnO nanostructures synthesized by the carbothermal evaporation of solid powders under controlled conditions (taken with permission from ref. 82). 
Wang et al.85,86 successfully synthesized pure Fe3O4 powder from Fe2O3 powder mixed with glucose as a carbon source using the carbothermal reduction method. Glucose was the reducing agent. Carbon, a by-product of glucose pyrolysis, could reduce Fe2O3 to Fe3O4 at 650 °C. Then, the generated CO could react with Fe2O3 to produce Fe3O4. Consequently, the carbon content and the concentration of CO determine the reaction rate of carbothermal reduction.85 Wu et al.87 recently synthesized magnetite (Fe3O4) dendrites/sugar carbon (MDs/SC) nanocomposites by a one-step carbothermal reduction method using α-Fe2O3 dendrites (HDs) as precursor and sucrose as carbon source. Fig. 14 schematically illustrates the synthesis process of MDs/SC nanocomposites with a TEM image shown below. In this work, the α-Fe2O3 phase of HDs was completely transformed into Fe3O4 during the complicated carbonization-reduction process. Carbon monoxide (i.e., CO) as one of the sucrose pyrolysis products can play a dominant role during the phase transformation process and the possible redox reaction could be roughly expressed as 3Fe2O3 + CO → 2Fe3O4 + CO2. Furthermore, the dendritic morphology of the embedded MDs could make them a promising candidate for lightweight electromagnetic absorption materials.87
 Fig. 14  Synthesis process of magnetic MDs/SC nanocomposites (reproduced with permission from ref. 87). 
4. Commonly used metal nanostructures
4.1. Iron (Fe) nanostructures

4.1.1. Iron carbides (Fe3C). 
Magnetic nanoparticles have been used for numerous applications, particularly as recoverable catalysts88 and in the biomedical sciences.89 By far the most widely used magnetic materials are iron oxides, especially in the form of magnetite (Fe3O4) and maghemite (γ-Fe2O3). However, iron oxide nanoparticles exhibit relatively low magnetic saturation, a crucial factor for many applications. Metallic iron (Fe0) has much higher saturation magnetization, but is unsuitable for medical applications due to its toxicity. Therefore, iron carbide (Fe3C) as an attractive alternative provides both high magnetic saturation and chemical stability. The synthesis of phase-pure Fe3C is very difficult, because the relatively high-temperature reducing conditions characteristic of carbide synthesis often lead to the formation of metallic iron or mixed Fe/Fe3C products.90,91 However, some routes, such as flame spray pyrolysis, have been employed to synthesize phase-pure Fe3C, involving multiple-step or high-energy procedures using hazardous or costly chemical precursors.92–95 Recently, organic gelators have been widely applied for the synthesis of carbide materials. In these cases, small molecules (e.g., urea) or polymers (e.g., chitosan) have also been employed to disperse soluble metal precursors within a homogeneous gel network.96–98 Once heated in an inert atmosphere, the gel decomposes and acts as a special carbon source for carbide formation. Biopolymers are particularly useful for this method due to their diverse functionality, meaning that they can be used to strongly bind and disperse metal precursors. Furthermore, many biopolymers (e.g., chitosan) display remarkable thermal stability.99 In the typically high-temperature synthesis of metal carbides, this means that the polymeric template is retained for a large portion of the calcination step. As a result, the complex tertiary structure of the biopolymer could control the nucleation and growth of crystalline intermediates and thus influence the size and morphology of the product. Similar methods are well established in the synthesis of metal oxide nanostructures.100
In addition, Fe3C can be synthesized by carbothermal reduction processes using biopolymers as carbon sources.83,84 Schnepp et al.83 used gelatin (a readily available biopolymer) for the synthesis of phase-pure Fe3C nanoparticles. Moreover, further insight into the mechanism of iron oxide/carbide formation was obtained using TGA coupled with mass spectrometry (TGA-MS). As shown in Fig. 15, the initial mass loss from 20 to 200 °C is attributed to vaporization of free and bound water, possibly coupled with loss of acetic acid released during binding of iron to carboxylate groups of the gelatin. The steeper section of the mass loss curve (190–350 °C) relates to gelatin decomposition in an inert atmosphere. TGA-MS in this decomposition region showed peaks for CO2 (293 °C), HCN (280 °C), H2O (205 °C, 235 °C), and NH3 (290 °C), which are consistent with partial decomposition of the gelatin. Porcine gelatin is mainly composed of multiple amino acids, so it is impossible to define an exact source for each of these emission products. Besides, iron acetate decomposition (190–200 °C) accounts for part of the mass loss. Crucially, this decomposition step occurs during the same temperature range as that of the nucleation of magnetite nanoparticles. In this way, dispersed oxide nanoparticles as well as carbon- and nitrogen-rich amorphous matrices are formed simultaneously.83
 Fig. 15  The key crystalline phases, elemental composition, mass loss, and evolved species during the synthesis of Fe3C from gelatin (reproduced with permission from ref. 83). 
The occurrence of iron oxide nanoparticles as an intermediate phase in this mechanism can be rationalized by considering the microstructure and composition of the biopolymer starting material. Indeed, there are multiple reports of the synthesis of metal oxide nanoparticles using biopolymer templates. The metal-binding capacity of biopolymers plays a crucial role. Gelatin is composed of a mixture of amino acids, which have multiple functionalities providing sites for metal ion binding. By dispersing the precursor iron cations within a functional aqueous gel, a stabilized homogeneous mixture of the metal precursor is formed. Consequently, the initial nucleation of the iron oxide phase is restricted to nanoparticles, whilst the polymer decomposes around the nanoparticles, forming a carbon- and nitrogen-rich template that prevents sintering of the oxide phase. Upon further heating, this template can react with the iron oxide, generating Fe3C nanoparticles.
Carbothermal reduction of metal oxides to carbides occurs at different temperatures depending on the stability of the oxides. Consequently, it is possible to employ sol–gel chemistry to prepare an oxide/carbide/carbon mixture, provided that the stabilities of the two intermediate oxides are sufficiently different. One or both of the phases could be etched to introduce pores with distinct sizes and chemical features. Schnepp et al.101 synthesized MgO/Fe3C/C nanocomposites from gelatin, an abundant polypeptide. Mild etching of MgO and Fe3C nanoparticles can result in a network of micropores and graphitic mesoporous “capsules”. Remarkably, this same simple system also introduces macropores. It has been demonstrated that mixed Mg and Fe nitrates interact synergistically with gelatin to produce foams with tunable “bubble” sizes. By combining these new advances, carbons with three types of pores have been prepared (Fig. 16A). Iron(III) can bind strongly to gelatin,22 forming a gel that expands into a sponge on drying, with bubbles 10–100 mm in diameter. Mg(NO3)2 can induce foaming, whereas mixed Mg(II) and Fe(III) nitrates exhibit a synergistic effect to form a foam with much smaller bubbles (Fig. 16B), having bubble sizes ranging from 100 to 1000 nm (Fig. 16C and D). Synchrotron X-ray diffraction (SXRD) reveals two crystalline phases (Fig. 16E), indexed to MgO and Fe3C. These two phases with highly contrasted Fe3C crystallites were embedded in a matrix alongside smaller MgO particles (Fig. 16F). Energy-dispersive X-ray analysis (EDXA) line scans and high-resolution TEM images showing discrete MgO and Fe3C particles (Fig. 16G and H) support these phase assignments. The average Fe3C particle diameter is clearly smaller in MF25 (5–10 nm) compared to MF50 (10–20 nm), indicating that the MgO suppresses sintering of the Fe3C phase during selective carbothermal reduction of iron oxide intermediates. Significantly, the particle size is considerably smaller than that of Fe3C/C synthesized from gelatin.83 The Fe3C particles are covered in multiple graphitic carbon layers (Fig. 16I).
 Fig. 16  (A) Schematic of the one-pot route to C/Fe3C/MgO foams and etching to generate carbon with three types of pores; (B) gelatin sponge; (C) SEM and (D) TEM images of the calcined foam; (E) SXRD of samples prepared using 25 and 50 mol% Fe in the starting material, denoted as MF25 and MF50, respectively; (F) dark-field STEM image of MF50; (G) STEM image and overlaid EDXA line scans of Mg and Fe for MF50; (H) high-resolution TEM image of MF25; (I) TEM of Fe3C/graphite (reproduced with permission from ref. 101). 
There are many synthetic polymers and indeed small molecules that can enable the sol–gel-type synthesis of carbides or nitrides. However, the interesting physical properties of many biopolymers can be exploited to introduce structural complexity into the product. For example, gelatin/metal nitrate hydrogels often foam on drying to produce expanded sponge-like structures, which are exploited to produce a metal carbide/nitride with high accessible porosity. Biopolymers have been applied to produce hybrid oxide/nitride or oxide/carbide nanocomposites (e.g., TiO2/Fe3C) in a one-step sol–gel-type process,102 which depends on different stability of the oxide intermediates to enable selective carbothermal reduction or nitridation. The homogeneous biopolymer precursor ensures that the two metal oxide intermediates are highly interdispersed, thus leading to a composite with very small particle size (<10 nm). In addition, biomass could be used both as a template and a carbon source for metal carbides production, typically achieved by first carbonizing the biomaterial (e.g., wood) and then infiltrating the char template with a liquid or gaseous metal reagent to form a metal carbide replica. Alternatively, the metal carbide can be produced in one step by soaking the biomaterial in an aqueous metal salt solution before pyrolysis. In this case, the metal precursor initially decomposes to give an oxide coating over a soft/hard template before carbothermal reduction to give a carbide replica with the biological features still intact. By heating to a lower temperature (avoiding carbothermal reduction), biomaterials could be used to template carbon/metal oxide composites as well.103
Glatzel et al.104,105 employed a biopolymer (i.e., cellulose) as a carbon source for the synthesis of iron-based carbides. As shown in Fig. 17, it can be seen that iron etches micrometer-long tubular carbons, running through the entire fiber (Fig. 17A) and lacing the whole structure with iron carbide nanoparticles. Furthermore, HRTEM micrographs of samples prepared at 800 °C confirm the high degree of crystallinity of the iron carbide, as well as the carbon in graphite (Fig. 17B). Comparing samples with and without iron shows that the presence of iron is required to graphitize cellulose at ≥800 °C, which was expected, as cellulose is considered a non-graphitizable carbon.106 Temperature-dependent XRD patterns revealed two interesting results. One of these is that the only crystalline phase this system passes through is magnetite, iron(II,III) oxide, which starts to crystallize at ∼500 °C. This is then directly converted into iron carbide, which forms at 800 °C. Furthermore, it can be seen that decomposition of iron carbide to elemental iron sets in between 800 and 900 °C and progresses further at 1000 °C (Fig. 17D). In this case, folded crane shapes were submerged in an iron precursor solution and calcined under an inert atmosphere (Fig. 17E–G). The resulting cranes were not only very well preserved in shape; they were also highly magnetic and stable enough to be picked up by the ‘wings’ or the ‘beak’ (Fig. 17G, inset).
 Fig. 17  (A) Cellulose calcined with added iron salt yields structures that are vastly different to that of the amorphous carbon obtained from cellulose alone. (B) HRTEM micrograph with labeled lattice fringes for iron carbide and graphite. (C) EDX line scan for iron (white curve, taken along orange line) and elemental mapping for iron (orange). (D) XRD patterns of samples. Green: Fe3O4, red: Fe3C, blue: Fe0 and the gray circle at 26° is attributed to graphitic carbon. Steps leading to the magnetic crane shapes: folding (E), soaking with the precursor solution (F) and after calcination (G) (reproduced with permission from ref. 104). 
The whole mechanism of the iron/carbon system can be summarized as follows. First, iron salts are converted into iron oxide, which, at elevated temperatures exceeding 600 °C, is then carbothermally reduced into metallic iron nanoparticles.107 Subsequently, these dissolve the amorphous carbon derived from cellulose and recrystallize it in the form of well-ordered graphene nanostructures, because the reaction proceeds via a liquid eutectic metal droplet (iron:carbon = 50:50) morphologically observed from some of the Fe3C nanostructures. During crystallization of the iron carbide, occurring along with cooling of the sample, several layers of ordered carbon are formed around the particles. These arise from sequestration of surplus carbon from the eutectic mixture, with the aim of moving from an iron-to-carbon atom ratio of approximately 1:1 in the eutectic mixture to a ratio of 3:1 in the cementite.
The precursors employed thus far have already been based on renewable resources, but both iron salts and cellulose still undergo some degree of processing before they are used in the syntheses. Consequently, there is certain room for further simplification. For instance, coffee filter paper is composed of unbleached and free cellulose. Using unprocessed dry wood worked just as well.104 In our recent work, rice husks in the presence of lignocellulose were utilized as a carbon source for metal nanoparticles (i.e., FeC3, Fe3O4, and Ni0) synthesis by one-step carbothermal pyrolysis at 750 °C. The XRD patterns are shown in Fig. 18. FeC3 and metallic nickel (Ni0) could initially be generated in situ in the carbon matrix of rice husk char by carbothermal pyrolysis at 750 °C in an inert atmosphere (e.g., N2). In addition, FeC3 can be converted into Fe3O4 after calcination at 600 °C, whereas the crystalline phase of Ni0 became stronger after further pyrolysis in an inert atmosphere. This indicates that nickel precursors in the carbon matrix were much more easily converted into the metallic state at a relatively low temperature compared to iron precursors.
 Fig. 18  XRD patterns of (A) iron carbide or oxides and (B) nickel (Ni0) nanoparticles embedded in a carbon matrix. 


4.1.2. Iron ternary carbides (MxFeyC). 
Iron carbide has been demonstrated to be a versatile catalyst, especially for the Fischer–Tropsch process.108–110 More interestingly, although single metal carbides are of high significance, iron-containing ternary carbides (i.e., MxFeyC) have received little attention outside the steel/metallurgy community. This is more so when one considers that their combination could potentially lead to synergistic effects between materials, resulting in unprecedented catalytic performance or even new catalytic properties. Glatzel et al.104 demonstrated a very easy synthetic route to defined iron-based ternary carbides, such as Fe3W3C, W2Fe21C6 and Fe2.25Mn0.75C in the form of nanoparticles, thereby broadening their prospective applicability.
Tungsten–iron system. 
For tungsten and cellulose alone, XRD analysis can be used to follow the gradual reduction of the initially formed tungsten oxides (WO3) via tungsten(IV) to elemental tungsten. With increasing temperature, each of the oxides is present in the different crystal structures (Fig. 19). It can be seen that the carbon matrix is able to stabilize and maintain these intermediate stages while cooling the samples down to room temperature. When calcining the tungsten precursor and cellulose alone, initially polyoxotungstates (W18O49) are still present, but they gradually decompose into WO3 and WO3−δ (present up to 900 °C). At lower temperatures, the various oxygen-deficient (1 > δ ≥ 0) tungsten oxides could not be distinguished via the XRD patterns due to their insufficient crystallinity.111–113 To maintain clarity and comprehensibility, only selected tungsten oxide patterns are depicted in the summary figure below. This means that tungsten(VI) oxides are subsequently reduced to different tungsten(IV) oxides between 800 and 900 °C. These are then further reduced to W0. In the last stage, elemental tungsten is present with tungsten carbides and tungsten oxycarbide. Tungsten does not catalyze graphitization in the investigated temperature region,114 which has no visible trace of ordered carbon structures.
 Fig. 19  XRD patterns of the temperature- and ratio-dependent phases of the tungsten–iron system. Green: W18O49; red: WO3, triclinic; gray: WO3, hexagonal; blue: WO2, orthorhombic; cyan: WO2, monoclinic; magenta: W0; yellow: WC; dark yellow: W2C; navy: W2 (C, O); purple: FeWO4; wine: Fe0; olive: Fe3W3C; dark cyan: Fe0.94W0.06; royal blue: W2Fe21C6 and the gray circle: graphitic carbon (reproduced with permission from ref. 104). 
The addition of iron has a substantial impact on the phase development, as the presence of iron causes phase coalescence at 700 °C. Here, only ferberite (FeWO4) is formed. When stoichiometric amounts of Fe and W are used, FeWO4 is formed phase-pure; in the case of samples with a 3:1 ratio, excess iron is present in the form of Fe0. Decomposing ferberite is transformed into several tungsten and iron–tungsten carbides above 700 °C, but, in contrast to tungsten alone, no traces of any tungsten oxides are visible. Furthermore, with 3:1 ratio samples, the excess iron can cause graphitization from this temperature on; for 1:1 ratio samples, graphitization does not set in until 1000 °C, where iron is released from the decomposing ternary carbides.104
Generally, the combination of iron and tungsten salts with cellulose and subsequent heat treatment under an inert atmosphere will, under the reaction conditions that were investigated, only yield FeWO4 as a pure phase. Various mixed-phase iron–tungsten carbides have been synthesized by this route. Nevertheless, utilizing alginate and gelatin as biopolymers and otherwise the same precursors has shown that this system is somewhat difficult to control. However, with the right temperature treatment, pure Fe3W3C and nitride phases can be obtained from biopolymer precursors115. This strongly indicates that it would only be a matter of finding the correct reaction conditions to yield pure phases with cellulose. In contrast to the aforementioned work, using cellulose, rather than dissolved biopolymer precursors, causes considerable porosity to be formed with overall surface areas exceeding 250 m2 g−1. Neither alginate nor gelatin yielded graphitized and hence porous products. The presumable reason is the interaction between cellulose and iron. This allows the formation of iron oxide nanoparticles on the surface of fibers, rather than the binding of individual ions to chelating moieties of the matrix. This seems to be essential for graphitization to occur and thus the formation of pores. This is supported by the consumption of the fibers derived from the outside in when comparing samples prepared at different temperatures in the presence of iron. Even though the phase purity leaves room for improvement, the present synthesis is a generally easy one-pot route to ternary oxides and carbides and it is only a matter of varying the reaction conditions to achieve the desired single-phase systems.
Manganese–iron system. 
Elemental manganese (Mn), which is structurally closer to iron (Fe), was chosen, as tungsten proved to be a somewhat difficult partner for iron in terms of the phase purity and size homogeneity of the resulting product. However, combination with Mn promises to yield equally interesting ternary compounds, as Mn is also known to be a versatile catalyst.116–118 The formation of phase-pure ternary carbides turned out to be a smaller challenge, and the synthesis of pure Mn0.75Fe2.25C could be achieved, which corresponds to the ratio of the Fe and Mn precursors. Manganese is hardly reduced by carbon alone (Fig. 20). The first reduction products were visible at 1000 °C, namely the two manganese carbides Mn5C2 and Mn7C3, along with substantial amounts of graphitic carbon. Manganese and cellulose alone form manganese(II) oxide with very small particle sizes (<4 nm). The first crystalline MnO nanoparticles emerge at 600 °C. Moreover, the manganese oxide nanoparticles formed at 600 °C do not grow significantly up to 900 °C. The first major change in the phase composition takes place at 1000 °C. A steep increase in the MnO particle size from 5 to 21 nm is followed by the emergence of a pronounced peak arising from graphitized carbon and crystalline Mn7C3 and Mn0 as minor phases. As opposed to tungsten, manganese could catalyze the graphitization of cellulose in the absence of iron. Graphitized carbon appears with the manganese carbides by a similar graphitization mechanism as observed with iron.104 The addition of iron can cause a significant drop in the graphitization temperature from 1000 to 800 °C and give rise to a multitude of Fe/Mn phases. At 600 °C, Fe3O4 and Fe0.5Mn0.5O could be formed. In this case, the formation of iron oxide and the mixed metal oxide Fe0.5Mn0.5O precedes the formation of crystalline MnO. At ≥800 °C, MnO becomes visible with an increase in Fe0 and Mn0.75Fe2.25C as additional phases. Furthermore, traces of a nitrogen-depleted iron nitride phase (FeN0.0589) were formed from 900 °C (Fig. 20). This is most likely due to the use of nitrate precursors. By replacing these with the acetates Fe(CH3CO2)2 and Mn(CH3CO2)2, formation of the nitride is suppressed without any other change in composition.104
 Fig. 20  XRD patterns of the phase developments in the manganese–iron system. Blue: Mn0.75Fe2.25C; red: MnO; magenta: Mn0.5Fe0.5O; gray: Mn7C3; wine: Mn5C2; green: Fe3O4; dark yellow: FeN0.0589; cyan: Fe0 and gray circle: graphitic carbon (reproduced with permission from ref. 104). 
Manganese and cellulose alone can yield homogeneous MnO nanoparticles dispersed in a carbon matrix at temperatures as low as 600 °C. Significantly, all other manganese oxides can be easily obtained by means of controlled further oxidation of MnO. Furthermore, the synthesis of Mn0.75Fe2.25C is demonstrated in nanoparticulate form, which is produced by simply combining aqueous solutions of manganese and iron acetates, in a ratio of 1:3, with cellulose and calcining the slurry under an inert atmosphere at 1000 °C. The possibility of producing this ternary carbide by an easy large-scale one-pot synthesis allows the material to be used for catalytic applications.


4.1.3. Fe3O4/C. 
Magnetite (Fe3O4), with its cubic inverse spinel structure, has unique electric and magnetic properties based on electron transfer between Fe2+ and Fe3+ in octahedral sites.119 Magnetite, as an important magnetic material, has been widely used in different applications, such as magnetic sensors, high-density magnetic recording media, printing ink, catalysts and especially in the biomedical field. Zhang et al.119,120 successfully synthesized Fe3O4 powders from a Fe2O3 and glucose mixture by a carbothermal reduction method. In this case, glucose was the reducing agent. Only mixed Fe3O4 and Fe2O3 phases were obtained with a glucose mass fraction less than 1/24. However, a pure Fe3O4 phase was obtained when the glucose mass fraction was above 1/24, up to 1/2.119 Besides, pure Fe3O4 can be synthesized at a higher heating temperature (650 °C) for a shorter holding time (even 0.5 h), or at a lower temperature (450 °C) for a longer holding time (>6 h), which is attributed to adequate decomposition of the carbon source (Fig. 21). Furthermore, Fe3O4 heated at 650 °C for 2 h possessed the highest saturation magnetization (Ms = 97.99 emu g−1) compared with using other methods, due to its better crystallinity and expanded crystalline cell.120 Carbon, as the by-product of glucose pyrolysis, can react with Fe2O3 at an appropriately high temperature.121 Possible reactions, along with the corresponding free energy, are listed in Table 1. Firstly, according to thermodynamics, carbon cannot react with water at 650 °C, because ΔG is positive for the corresponding reaction (18). This means that reaction (18) cannot occur and there is no hydrogen in the system. Therefore, neither of the reactions (19) and (20) will occur due to the absence of hydrogen, although their ΔG values are both negative. Secondly, ΔG is negative for reaction (21); therefore, carbon would reduce Fe2O3 to Fe3O4 at 650 °C (ΔG < 0). The CO produced in reaction (21) would react with Fe2O3 further via reaction (22). Because ΔG for reaction (21) is lower than that of reaction (23), reaction (21) is more likely to happen. Similarly, reaction (22) most likely occurs due to the ΔG for reaction (22) being lower than that of reaction (24). When the CO partial pressure reaches equilibrium with atmospheric pressure for reaction (21), reaction (23) cannot occur. The same applies to the relationship between reactions (22) and (24). When the CO and CO2 partial pressures reach equilibrium for reaction (22), reaction (24) cannot occur either. Therefore, no FeO phase would appear in the products.119
 Fig. 21  XRD patterns of samples with different mole fractions of glucose and Fe2O3 (reproduced with permission from ref. 119).

4.1.4. Zero-valent iron (Fe0). 
Zero-valent metal nanoparticles have been widely studied due to the diverse applicability of these structures for some important applications, especially in catalysis,122 medicine, and spectroscopy and sensors.123 In addition to zero-valent metal nanoparticles, bimetallic alloy nanoparticles have received much attention due to their enhanced properties and additional tunability.124,125 The main concern with the preparation of metallic nanoparticles relates to the issue of stabilization. This typically represents a twofold issue: one is stabilization of the particles against aggregation or coalescence, the other is their stabilization against chemical changes, especially oxidation. A common solution is the use of stabilizing agents such as surfactants.126 MnO nanoparticles can disperse homogeneously in the carbon matrix. MnO nanoparticles are prone to further oxidation, which suggests that the carbon matrix is a potent stabilizing agent. Moreover, no coalescence of the particles was observed over a wide temperature range, suggesting that the carbon structure is a good anticaking agent as well. Non-noble zero-valent metal particles are especially prone to undergo chemical changes along with the usual ripening processes and consequently are harder to stabilize; however, they are important catalysts.126–128 In this respect, cellulose is valuable for dispersing and stabilizing them. Several transition metals of recent interest have been applied such as iron, cobalt, nickel, palladium and copper. Furthermore, Glatzel et al.104 synthesized zero-valent metal and metal alloy particles, including Cu, Ni, NiFe, Ni0.32Fe0.68, NiPd, CoFe and Co3Fe7, using celluloses (e.g., cellulose filter paper and microcrystalline cellulose) as carbon sources and bivalent metal nitrates as metal precursors.
Copper–iron system. 
The combination of iron and copper can produce well-dispersed stable Cu0 nanoparticles. It turns out that, despite their small size of around 9 nm, these particles are found to be aggregates of very small crystalline copper clusters of only a few atoms. Moreover, copper can direct the graphitizing power of iron. On the one hand, copper directs the formed tubular carbons to align themselves and to grow in straight lines. On the other hand, the fcc structure of copper promotes the formation of diamond-like carbons at very low temperatures. However, none of the ratios or temperatures yielded any combination of the metals, neither ternary oxides nor ternary carbides, nor is any form of alloy seen in Fig. 22. Copper alone can form crystalline copper nanoparticles at a temperature as low as 500 °C. At relatively low temperatures, those particles are accompanied by Cu2O particles, which disappear above 600 °C. The crystallinity of copper particles appears virtually invariant with temperature, exhibiting a crystallite size of ∼20 nm. The addition of iron initially does not produce any unexpected alterations. Upon heating, Fe3O4 is observed as an additional phase. Moderate amounts of iron will be transformed directly into Fe0 and larger amounts will additionally produce small amounts of Fe3C. No form of combination of the two metals is observed.104 Copper alone could yield well-controlled nanoparticles with an average diameter of 9 nm. In general, copper nanoparticles have been produced and stabilized in a carbon matrix. The particles were actually copper clusters of sizes below 1 nm with an approximate atom count of less than 200 atoms per copper cluster. The combination of copper and iron enabled the formation of a diamond-like carbon phase that was catalytically produced by the iron and stabilized by the fcc lattice of the copper.
 Fig. 22  XRD patterns of the phase developments in the copper–iron system. Gray: Cu2O; red: Cu0; green: Fe3O4; blue: Fe0; magenta: Fe3C; cyan: diamond-like carbon and the gray circle: graphitic carbon (reproduced with permission from ref. 104). 
Nickel–iron system. 
Nickel yielded, much like copper, well-dispersed and highly stable zero-valent metal nanoparticles. These were of well-controlled size with diameters of 5.5 ± 0.5 nm and crystallized at temperatures as low as 600 °C. An increase in the treatment temperature at and above 900 °C can cause catalytic graphitization to occur, similar to the processes with iron. Nevertheless, the graphitized structures were of somewhat different morphology. The use of nickel precursors furthermore gave rise to micrometer-sized pores in addition to the pores caused by graphitization. As opposed to copper, nickel will form various alloys in the presence of iron due to their similar size and chemical nature. In particular, the alloys NiFe and Ni0.32Fe0.68 were obtained. Iron and nickel formed at different locations within the cellulose framework and only upon graphitization. In addition, the iron present throughout the carbon matrix was in the form of pure alloys.
The XRD patterns of the crystalline phases at the various temperatures and atomic ratios are summarized in Fig. 23. From the point of crystallization at 700 °C and up to the onset of graphitization at 900 °C the size of the Ni0 nanoparticles is virtually invariant with temperature. For samples prepared at 800 °C, the Scherrer equation yields particle sizes of 4.7 ± 0.3 nm. Upon graphitization, the particle sizes increase dramatically; this mechanism is similar to that for iron. The addition of iron first causes the formation of a Fe3O4 phase at temperatures of 500 and 600 °C; in addition, above 600 °C the alloy NiFe is formed alongside Ni0 (4.4 nm). With graphitization at 900 °C, the Ni0 phase disappears completely and only graphitic carbon and the alloy remain. Adding a threefold excess of iron causes a similar reaction path, only now Fe0 is the excess phase and the alloy is the 3:1 alloy Ni0.32Fe0.68.
 Fig. 23  Summary of the temperature- and ratio-dependent XRD patterns for the nickel–iron system. Gray: Ni0, red: Ni0.5Fe0.5, green: Fe3O4, blue: Fe0, cyan: Ni0.32Fe0.68 and the gray circle: graphitic carbon (reproduced with permission from ref. 104). 
Interestingly, nickel could cause the formation of micrometer-sized pores akin to those seen with manganese. However, the addition of iron immediately suppresses those larger pores, as observed with manganese. The large pores are attributed to nickel over the entire temperature range. With the onset of graphitization, the walls of large pores become porous, effectively creating well-defined hierarchical pore structures ideal for applications in catalysis.129 Samples prepared with iron and nickel do not exhibit porosity until graphitization sets in. However, the presence of iron causes the formation of a layer of nanoparticles on the surface of the fibers. Also, at 1000 °C, it becomes apparent that the graphitized carbon structures derived from nickel are of a more layered nature, meaning that they form flat aligned bundles as opposed to the rather tubular structures derived from iron. The transition from one structure to the other can be followed by increasing the iron-to-nickel ratio.
The sample prepared at 800 °C with an iron-to-nickel ratio of 3:1 showed that iron still forms on the surface of the fibers and only upon etching into the fibers does it come into contact with nickel. This explains why the alloy does not become phase-pure until graphitization completely sets in. Iron behaves differently from all other metals, in the respect that it forms oxides solely on the surface of the fibers and does not enter into the carbon matrix, until it forms an iron–carbon eutectic mixture. The most probable reason is a coordination effect where iron does not bind strongly enough to the cellulose matrix. Recently, Thompson et al.130 reported the direct transformation of lignocellulosic biomass into nanostructured graphitic carbon by means of a single step. Iron carbide nanoparticles are generated in situ by thermal decomposition of absorbed iron nitrate followed by carbothermal reduction. The Fe3C particles subsequently etch through the biomass to generate intertwined graphitic tubules by means of catalytic graphitization. These materials are mesoporous structures with the pore size dependent on the iron content. Conversion of biomass into stable graphitic carbon at relatively low temperatures (e.g., 800 °C) offers a promising route to the sustainable synthesis of carbons for electrode or filtration applications.
