Moreover, this facile method was applied to produce nanocomposites of Fe3C/graphite combined with nanoparticles of metal oxides such as CaO or MgO, providing the potential for a wide range of applications.130


4.1.5. LiFePO4/C. 
Lithium iron phosphate (LiFePO4) has been intensively used because of its low cost, low toxicity, abundant raw materials, good cycling stability, high theoretical capacity, and good structural stability.131–135 Olivine-type LiFePO4 has a hexagonally close-packed oxygen array, in which the octahedra share both edges and faces (Fig. 24). The cation arrangement in LiFePO4 is significantly different from that in layered and spinel structures. There is no continuous network of FeO6 edge-shared octahedra that might contribute to electronic conductivity; instead, divalent Fe2+ ions occupy corner-shared octahedra. The phosphorus ions are located in tetrahedral sites, and the lithium ions reside in chains of edge-shared octahedra. LiFePO4 initially includes one Li+ ion per formula unit that could be extracted and transferred to the anode in the first charging process, compensating for the oxidation of Fe2+ to Fe3+. The theoretical capacity based on this one-electron reaction is 170 mA h g−1, but the first attempts to de-insert Li from this material were limited to about 0.6 e−, owing to transport limitations of electrons and ions.131 The main obstacle to reaching the theoretical capacity is low intrinsic electronic conductivity. Furthermore, motion of lithium in the olivine crystal structure takes place through one-dimensional channels, with little possibility of crossing between channels.136 These 1-D paths are particularly susceptible to blockage by defects and impurities.136,137 Several approaches were developed to solve the conductivity problem.138 These approaches include reduction of particle size and homogeneous particle size distribution;139 coating the particles with carbon140,141 or co-synthesizing the compounds with carbon to surround each particle with a good electronic conductor;142 tailoring the morphology and texture of the particles via low-temperature synthetic routes;143 and selective doping with supervalent cations in order to increase the intrinsic conductivity.144
 Fig. 24  The olivine structure of LiFePO4 (taken with permission from ref. 135). 
The technology used to synthesize LiFePO4 is important for reducing particle size, which directly affects its performance, cost, and potential applications. Various methods, such as solid-state reaction,145,146 carbothermal reduction,147–149 hydrothermal processing,146,150,151 sol–gel processing,146,152 and precipitation153 have been employed to synthesize LiFePO4 for cathode materials. Carbothermal reduction possesses some distinct advantages over other methods, such as cheap precursors, a simple synthetic process, and easy scale-up.148 Besides, residual carbon introduced by reducing agents in the carbothermal reduction process not only offers better electron transport in a composite electrode, but also impedes the growth of LiFePO4 particles, which can enhance the electrochemical performance of an electrode.147
Carbothermal reduction is essentially a solid-state reaction process that uses higher temperatures and longer reaction times to enhance the diffusion process and generate a well-ordered structure.154 Owing to the high temperatures and long reaction times used, products synthesized by this method have some disadvantages, such as broader particle size distributions, undesirable impurities, and larger particles.154,155 Mechanical milling, including dry milling156,157 and wet milling,158,159 has been used to improve the uniformity and decrease the average particle size of the synthesized materials. Wet-milled samples have higher charge–discharge capacities and longer stable life cycles than dry-milled samples.155 This improved performance originates from the finer particles generated (nano/sub-micron size) and their homogeneous surface morphologies. Therefore, wet-milling-assisted carbothermal reduction is an appropriate route to produce nano/sub-micron LiFePO4.155 The electrochemical performance of LiFePO4/C is closely associated with a variety of properties, including its surface area, particle size and distribution, particle morphology, carbon content, carbon morphology, and phase purity.160 These properties depend on the wet-milling parameters, including ball-to-powder ratio, stirrer speed, ball milling time, ball diameter, solids concentration, and the amounts and types of organic additives. Lv et al.161 synthesized LiFePO4/C using wet-milling-assisted carbothermal reduction, and studied how the ball-to-powder ratio affected its structure, morphology, and electrochemical performance. The particle sizes of LiFePO4/C could be adjusted by changing the ball-to-powder weight ratio. An SEM micrograph shows that the particles have become slightly agglomerated from nanosized primary particles (Fig. 25A), and a TEM micrograph shows that the LiFePO4 particles are surrounded by a carbon network, which prevents the LiFePO4 particles from growing during carbothermal reduction (Fig. 25B). When the ball-to-powder weight ratio reached 25:1, the sample exhibited the highest discharge capacity (155 mA h g−1 at 0.5 C and 125 mA h g−1 at 10 C) and excellent capacity retention after 100 cycles at room temperature, which is attributed to a reduction of particle size to nanometer scale, narrower particle size distribution, the network of carbon coating the active material, and reduced electrochemical polarization resistance.161 Then, they successfully synthesized bowl-like mesoporous LiFePO4/C composites by a wet-milling/spray-drying carbothermal reduction method that used FePO4·2H2O as the iron source and starch as the carbon source and reducing agent with a bowl-like mesoporous structure.162 Moreover, the resulting bowl-like LiFePO4/C composites consist of carbon-coated nanoparticles along with an interconnected open mesoporous microstructure, which provides more channels for the rapid diffusion of lithium (Li) ions and prevents aggregation of LiFePO4 particles during charging and discharging processes. Therefore, these LiFePO4/C composites exhibit excellent high rate performance.162 In addition, the solvent is an important parameter in spray-drying to prepare LiFePO4 particles. As shown in Fig. 25(C) and (D), particles prepared with alcohol have nearly spherical structures, but particles prepared with water exhibit a bowl-like morphology. This can be attributed to hydrodynamic effects (internal and external with respect to the droplet), which also have an important effect on particle morphology.162
 Fig. 25  (A) SEM and (B) TEM micrographs of LiFePO4/C with ball-to-powder ratio of 25:1; SEM micrographs of precursor using different solvents: (C) alcohol and (D) water (reproduced with permission from ref. 161 and 162). 
Coating with carbon derived from pyrolysis of organics has been a very efficient strategy for enhancing electrical conductivity and improving rate capacity. Besides, due to the assistance of in situ carbon coatings in restricting the crystal growth and suppressing the agglomeration of LiFePO4, its primary particle size is decreased on the nanoscale, leading to a shorter distance for lithium ion diffusion in crystals and thereby an enhancement of the rate capability. Nevertheless, Delacourt et al.163 reported carbon-free LiFePO4 particles in the range 100–200 nm which still exhibit excellent electrochemical performance in terms of specific capacity and capacity retention upon cycling. Moreover, to achieve high rate capability of LiFePO4 electrodes, more emphasis should be focused on minimization of particle size, because the ionic conductivity is much lower than the electronic conductivity.164 Their results imply that decreasing the particle size can have a beneficial effect on power performance. Recently, carbon-coated LiFePO4 composites were modified with carbon nanotubes (CNT) or graphene, which exhibit enhanced electrochemical performance.165–170 Most reported studies have confirmed that the addition of superior conductive materials is effective in enhancing the dispersion of LiFePO4 and reducing its primary particle size. Although it is difficult to determine whether the major role in power performance is played by enhanced electronic conductivity or reduced particle size, this trend of combining the two merits of conductive coatings and particle size minimization is certainly the future direction for improving the rate capacity of LiFePO4. Nevertheless, expensive carbon materials (e.g., CNT and graphene) can cause an increase in the cost of LiFePO4-based composites, which does not promote their widespread application. In addition to the cost, the excessive carbon content can lower the tapped density of LiFePO4-based composites.171 Therefore, it remains a major challenge to establish a balance among these aspects for preparing economical carbon-coated LiFePO4-based composites (C-LFP). Therefore, Liu et al.172 developed a high-energy ball mill (HEBM) technique to treat bulk precursors and achieve particle size minimization. The precursor was prepared by means of a rheological phase method to ensure uniform mixing of materials. Carbon-coated single-crystalline LiFePO4 nanocomposites (nano-CS-LFP) can be synthesized by a rheological phase method, combined with the HEBM process and carbothermal reduction (Fig. 26). A large amount of the nanosized precursor is obtained within a narrow particle size distribution of ca. 95 nm. Recently, Rong et al.173 synthesized a LiFePO4/C nanocomposite by a simple carbothermal process within a short sintering time using nano-Fe2O3, LiH2PO4 and sucrose. Nanosized Fe2O3 was adopted to reduce the diffusion distances of ions in the solid-state reaction and reduce the sintering time. In addition, nano-Fe2O3 can effectively reduce the LiFePO4 grain size (<100 nm) and make it uniform, as well as accelerating the solid-state reaction kinetics, which is a convenient procedure for industrialization.
 Fig. 26  Schematic procedures used to prepare nano-CS-LFP composites; embedded SEM images of the bulk precursor of C-LFP (a) and the nanosized precursor of nano-CS-LFP (b) (reproduced with permission from ref. 163). 
Mesoporous LiFePO4 electrode materials could exhibit good electrochemical performance, but they are always prepared using surfactants or templates. Therefore, it is still a major challenge to discover a facile, cost-effective, repeatable and high-yield way to synthesize mesoporous LiFePO4 nanoparticles that exhibit excellent electrochemical performance without using a template or surfactant. Du et al.174 reported the synthesis of mesoporous LiFePO4/C nanocomposites by a carbothermal reduction method. The starting materials were FeCl3·6H2O, NH4H2PO4, and Li2CO3. Fig. 27A shows the XRD patterns of the LiFePO4/C (LFP/C) and porous LFP/C nanocomposites. All the major diffraction lines of the two samples could be indexed to the orthorhombic olivine-type structure without any detectable impurity phases. Fig. 27B shows that most of the particles are well-dispersed spherical particles, with an average particle size of 80 nm, indicating that polyaniline can effectively prevent agglomeration of the particles and restrict the growth of LFP. However, the morphology of LFP/C is distorted and agglomerated and the size of the particles is about 110 nm (Fig. 27E). In addition, a mesoporous structure can be observed for each nanoparticle in Fig. 27C, whereas hardly any pores can be seen in LFP/C (Fig. 27F). Furthermore, the LiFePO4 crystallites in contact with a carbon layer (Fig. 27D) can ensure electrical continuity around the crystallites. The carbon layer was proven to be effective in achieving optimized rate capabilities.
 Fig. 27  (A) XRD patterns of (a) porous-LFP/C and (b) LFP/C nanocomposites; (B) SEM, (C) and (D) TEM images of porous-LFP/C; (E) SEM, (F) and (G) TEM images of LFP/C nanocomposites (reproduced with permission from ref. 174). 
In addition, carbothermal reduction can be used for the synthesis of porous LiFePO4/C in combination with other technologies. Chen et al.175 used a microwave-assisted hydrothermal process combined with carbothermal reduction to synthesize monodisperse porous LiFePO4/C microspheres. Ferric citrate and lithium dihydrogen phosphate were used as starting materials. After the precursor was initially prepared via a microwave-assisted hydrothermal approach, it was reground, together with sucrose as an additional carbon source, for carbothermal reduction. The carbothermal reduction was conducted at 700 °C under an Ar/2 vol% H2 atmosphere for 2 h at a heating rate of 5 °C min−1. Fig. 28A(a) shows a typical XRD pattern of the as-synthesized precursor, which is highly consistent with the standard Fe3(PO4)2(OH)2. Fig. 28A(b) is indexed to the standard orthorhombic LiFePO4. The diffraction peaks are strong and narrow with no detectable impurity phases, indicating the high crystallinity of the LiFePO4/C sample. SEM images of LiFePO4/C microspheres are shown in Fig. 28B and C. It can be observed that the meatball-like morphology was retained after carbothermal reduction and LiFePO4/C microspheres are mainly composed of carbon-coated nanoparticles (50–150 nm). Furthermore, the microspheres exhibit a porous morphology aggregated by nanoparticles, indicating the nanoparticles in LiFePO4/C microspheres are single crystals (Fig. 28D).
 Fig. 28  (A) XRD patterns of (a) Fe3(PO4)2(OH)2 microspheres and (b) LiFePO4/C microspheres (reproduced with permission from ref. 175). 
The proper amount of carbon coating can effectively enhance the electronic and ionic conductivities of LiFePO4/C microspheres, thereby improving its rate capability and cycling stability as a cathode material. Due to their high tapped density, LiFePO4/C microspheres can provide high volumetric energy density as well as excellent electrochemical performance as cathode materials, which is promising for high-power lithium-ion batteries.175 Although surface coating and particle size reduction have been shown to increase the power density, these are usually associated with a lower tapped density of the cathode material as the particle size decreases.176 As an alternative method, metal ions have been used as doping species to improve the electrochemical properties of pure LiFePO4.177 In this respect, much attention has been paid to structural modification by substitution of Fe, P, or Li, which leads to enhancement of Li+ ion or electron mobility.178 The electronic conductivity of LiFePO4 can be increased by cation doping, which generates defects in the interior of the crystallite. Thus, Örnek et al.179 reported different combinations of Ni-doped LiFePO4/C nanocomposite synthesized via sol–gel-assisted carbothermal reduction. Tannic acid was added to form a conductive carbon network between the LiFePO4 particles and restrict the growth of grains. Lin et al.180 enhanced the electrochemical performance of LiFePO4/C by surface modification with Sn nanoparticles. More recently, Fan et al.181 synthesized LiFe0.98Mg0.02PO4/C, LiFe0.97Cr0.02PO4/C and LiFe0.96Ti0.02PO4/C by a carbothermal reduction method using FePO4·2H2O as the iron source and phenol–formaldehyde resin as the reducing agent and carbon source. Mg2+, Cr3+ and Ti4+ with various valences in the ratio of 0.02 were used as dopants in order to improve the electrochemical performance of LiFePO4/C.

4.2. Nickel (Ni) nanostructures
Porous carbon materials are of great interest due to their high surface area, thermal stability, chemical inertness, and biocompatibility, which contribute to their high performance in various applications, such as adsorption of dye molecules.182–186 However, their widespread application is restricted due to the size of micropores, which limits the transfer of bulky dye molecules from the surface of carbon materials into the pores.187,188 Moreover, porous carbon-based metal or metal oxide materials have been widely used for catalytic applications. These are fabricated by incorporating metals (oxides) within pores by impregnation or by modifying metal (oxide) nanoparticles within carbon mesopore walls.189–199 The latter case is superior to the incorporation of particles in pores, in that open pores are retained and pore blockages are avoided, especially at high loadings.193 On the other hand, nanoparticle size has a significant effect on catalytic performance. Small-sized particles (normally 3–15 nm) are active and stable in catalytic reactions.193,200–203 The technique of confined growth of nanoparticles within the pores of a matrix is normally adopted to control particle sizes.193,200–204
Ordered mesoporous carbons with uniform, large, accessible mesopores and designed pore structures at least partially overcome the disadvantages associated with traditional porous carbons and are outstanding candidates for supporting metal nanoparticles.205–217 Highly ordered mesoporous polymer–silica and carbon–silica nanocomposites with interpenetrating networks could be synthesized by the evaporation-induced triconstituent co-assembly method. Thus, soluble resol polymer was used as an organic precursor, prehydrolyzed TEOS was used as an inorganic precursor, and the triblock copolymer F127 was employed as a template. Ordered mesoporous nanocomposites have “reinforced concrete”-structured frameworks. By adjusting the initial mass ratios of TEOS to resol, the obtained nanocomposites possess continuous composition gradients, with the ratios ranging from zero to infinity for the two constituents that are “homogeneously” dispersed in the pore walls. The presence of silicates in nanocomposites dramatically inhibits framework shrinkage during calcination, resulting in ordered mesoporous carbon–silica nanocomposites. Combustion in air or etching in HF solution can remove carbon or silica from carbon–silica nanocomposites and generate ordered mesoporous pure silica or carbon frameworks.216 Wang et al.218 reported self-assembling metal or metal oxide nanoparticles, which were embedded in ordered mesoporous polymers or carbon frameworks. The synthetic scheme is illustrated in Fig. 29. TEM images show oriented and hexagonally arranged pores with an estimated domain size of 11.8 nm for the NiO catalyst, confirming the highly ordered 2D hexagonal mesostructure (Fig. 30). Interestingly, well-dispersed nanoparticles with sizes of about 3.0 nm are clearly observed in the pore walls. Metal or metal oxide nanoparticles with sizes below 12 nm controlled by the metal content and pyrolysis temperature are well dispersed throughout the entire polymer or carbon matrix. These features facilitate their application as adsorbents or catalysts with an easy separation process. This method is also applicable to other mesoporous transition metal oxide-containing polymer or carbon catalysts, such as those containing Co and Fe. Moreover, Zhai et al.219 developed a soft-template route to synthesize highly ordered mesoporous carbon/nickel nanoparticle composites, which possess high surface areas, large mesopores and magnetic frameworks. Resol, nickel nitrate and TEOS were used as precursors, and Pluronic F127 as a template. Ordered mesoporous silica/carbon/nickel nanoparticle composites were obtained by co-assembling the precursors with F127. The presence of rigid silicates in nanocomposites can reduce structural shrinkage during carbonization. The ordered mesoporous carbon/nickel nanoparticle composites were fabricated by etching the silicate component with NaOH solution.
 Fig. 29  Schematic diagram for the synthesis of ordered mesoporous Ni–C catalyst with metal embedded in carbon pore walls (taken with permission from ref. 218). 
 Fig. 30  TEM images of mesoporous Ni- (a and b) or NiO-containing (c and d) polymer catalysts (taken with permission from ref. 218). 
Integrating large magnetic transition metal nanocrystals onto mesoporous carbons has attracted more attention because of its low cost, easy separation and great potential in various applications. Subsequently, Yang et al.220 reported large nickel nanocrystals (30–75 nm) in situ integrated onto mesoporous carbons (MC), synthesized via block copolymer-directed self-assembly of nickel acetate and 8-quinolinol-modified chitosan under neutral conditions assisted by tetraethyl orthosilicate before pyrolysis and removal of silica (Fig. 31). This silica-assisted strategy strengthens the interaction between chitosan and P123 via the polymerization of chitosan with silica, yielding “homogeneous” interpenetrating silica–chitosan frameworks with inserted evenly dispersed nickel complexes, leading to homogeneously distributed Ni nanocrystals on the silica–carbon frameworks in the pyrolyzed samples. Nickel ions are reduced in situ to metallic nickel (Ni0) via carbothermal reduction using reducing carbon species, like CO and C, in a single step. The presence of silicates in nanocomposites could dramatically inhibit framework shrinkage during pyrolysis and removing the silica yields a replicated intertwined carbonaceous mesostructure, during which the Ni nanocrystals are exposed. Significantly, the removal of silica can create more exposed and magnetically recoverable Ni nanocrystals. Furthermore, their size, microstructure and morphology of the carbonaceous support can be easily tuned by varying the pyrolysis temperature.
 Fig. 31  Schematic diagram of Ni–MC-t synthesis (taken with permission from ref. 220). 
In addition, Kido et al.221 applied a facile sol–gel reaction accompanied by phase separation for the fabrication of rigid monolithic nickel hydroxide xerogels and nickel/carbon composites with hierarchical porosity. In the synthetic route, nickel chloride (i.e., NiCl2) acts as a nickel precursor and trimethylene oxide acts as a gelation initiator to increase the pH in the reaction solution. Moreover, polyacrylic acid plays a double role as a phase separation inducer and as a co-constituent with nickel hydroxide to form continuous gel skeletons on the micrometer scale. As a result, the obtained xerogels possess well-defined macropores. Subsequent heat-treatment in air can result in the crystallization of NiO at 300 °C, whereas carbothermal reduction under an Ar flow leads to the formation of nickel/carbon (Ni/C) composites with hierarchical pores and large specific surface areas at temperatures higher than 300 °C (as shown in Fig. 32). This facile and versatile synthetic route has the potential to be extended to various monolithic inorganic materials, which include oxides, carbides, and nitrides as well as metallic materials, along with well-controlled pores based on divalent metal hydroxides.
 Fig. 32  Schematic of a facile sol–gel reaction for the fabrication of rigid monolithic nickel hydroxide-based xerogels and nickel/carbon composites (taken with permission from ref. 221). 
In recent years, graphene-based nanocomposites have undergone rapid development based on their promising applications and the steady accumulation of technology in the carbon nanotube (CNT) field. Compared with one-dimensional CNT, graphene is more suitable for immobilizing guest nanoparticles due to the large contact area provided by its planar structure. Moreover, when graphene oxide (GO) is used as a precursor, its oxygen-containing functional groups would facilitate immobilization of nanoparticles.222 It is easy to anchor metal nanoparticles onto graphene sheets by simultaneously reducing GO and metal ions via a suitable reduction method. Significantly, when decorated with metal nanoparticles, reduced GO could efficiently prevent aggregation arising from stacking and interaction among the sheets after drying.223 The two-dimensional morphology of graphene-like materials can offer opportunities as scaffolds in composites, combining the properties of graphene with those of an inorganic support.224,225 One type of material that is attracting more attention is metal and metal oxide nanoparticles deposited on graphene-like sheets.226 Metal nanoparticle/graphene hybrid composites have been widely applied in catalysis,227–229 supercapacitors,230,231 sensors232,233 and hydrogen storage,234 due to the synergistic effect between the inherent properties of metal nanoparticles and the merits of graphene, e.g., large surface area and high electrical conductivity. Zhang et al.235 synthesized Ni nanoparticles supported on graphene (Ni/G) using GO and Ni2+ ions as starting materials. GO was mixed with NiCl2 solution and treated with hydrazine. Hydrazine both reduced GO and formed a complex with nickel(II). At 500 °C, highly dispersed Ni nanoparticles with diameters of about 10 nm and surface oxygen species comprising Ni2+/Ni3+ could be formed on the graphene sheets after decomposition of the Ni complex. Moreover, further “reduction’’ of the graphene sheets by removing the oxygen functionalities remaining after reduction with hydrazine could be achieved via carbothermal reduction. By increasing the annealing temperature to 700 °C, the Ni nanoparticles migrated and aggregated into large particles and their surface oxygen species were mainly reduced by the graphene support. Taking into account the fact that transition metal ions such as Fe2+, Co2+ and Mn2+ can react with hydrazine to form a complex, the synthetic route to Ni/G is applicable to other metal nanoparticle/graphene composites.
In addition, Abellán et al.236 proved that thermal treatment of the hybrid material formed by the spontaneous precipitation of GO and Ni,Mn layered double hydroxide (NiMn-LDH) can possibly lead to the segregation of nickel nanoparticles and the decomposition of graphene into CO2. The GO material would provide carbon atoms to convert Ni2+ ions into Ni metal via carbothermal reduction. Mixing solutions of LDH and GO leads to the instantaneous precipitation of a solid in which, due to the relative dimensions, nanometer-scale LDH flakes are supported on larger micrometer-scale GO sheets (Fig. 33A). Fig. 33B–G show images obtained from NiMn-LDH–GO after calcination for 24 h at 450 °C and 800 °C, respectively. Fig. 33E shows the morphology of the material obtained at 450 °C, in which nanoparticles of <20 nm can be observed to be deposited on graphene layers. Moreover, the lamellar morphology of the hybrid material displays a series of curved graphene layers thinner than 3 nm covered with nanoparticles of 17 nm (Fig. 33B and C). Importantly, for the material calcined at 450 °C, the presence of carbonaceous materials arising from the transformation of GO and, in particular, CNTs is observed. Fig. 33G shows an interlayer spacing of 0.34 nm, which is in agreement with the value for a (002) plane in graphite. In contrast, the key feature after calcination at 800 °C is the complete disappearance of any residual carbon material. The changes in GO are accompanied by the appearance of small Ni nanoparticles (Fig. 33B and C) for calcination at 450 °C or large Ni spheres for calcination at 800 °C. The key feature is that thermal treatment at 800 °C can lead to complete segregation of Ni from the original NiMn-LDH in the form of large micrometer-scale Ni spheres, accompanied by the disappearance of any carbonaceous material (carbon content 0.1%), as well as the residual presence of manganese oxides.
 Fig. 33  (A) Structure of the NiMn-LDH–GO hybrid. (B) FESEM-EDS elemental mapping performed over the area indicated in the inset, showing phase segregation of Ni and Mn. The scale bar represents 20 μm. (C) Larger magnification of a well-defined Ni sphere present in (B) of ca. 20 μm in size. (D) FESEM, (E) TEM, (F) HRTEM, and (G) larger-magnification images of NiMn-LDH–GO calcined at 450 °C (reproduced with permission from ref. 236). 
Most processes concentrate on native biomass, including food or agricultural wastes, since potential applications often require cheap and sustainable materials.237 The simplest approach is pyrolysis of these biomaterials under closed conditions or an inert atmosphere.238 Alternatively, a two-step heating process can be employed to initially strengthen the biopolymer network by crosslinking; for example, the synthesis of microporous N-doped carbon materials from chicken feather fibers (composed of keratin, a fibrous structural protein also found in wool, hair, and some animal shells).239 Another two-step strategy is to activate the carbon materials after pyrolysis, either chemically (e.g., with KOH) or by heating in air to increase their porosity.240,241 Metals can be used to crosslink and stabilize the polymer precursor,242 to increase porosity, or as catalysts (e.g., Fe or Ni) for graphitization of the carbon materials at relatively low temperatures.243 The metal precursor can be introduced by infiltration, but many native biomaterials also contain metals (e.g., potassium in seaweeds) that have been exploited for in situ chemical activation.244 It has been widely reported that the natural variability of biopolymers (e.g., seaweed species) can be used to tune the exact properties of the carbon product. However, for some special applications, such as supercapacitors, this lack of uniformity in the biomass can cause some problems with consistency of performance compared to carbon materials originating from synthetic polymers.245 Some researchers synthesized nickel (Ni0) nanoparticles via carbothermal reduction.246–248 Biomass can be employed as a carbon source with a natural biotemplate and the nickel precursor (Ni2+) is inserted into the lignocellulosic matrix by impregnation (e.g., atmospheric pressure or vacuum). Vacuum impregnation contributes to the formation of inner-sphere surface complexes, whereas atmospheric pressure impregnation promotes electrostatic interactions with the outer hydration sphere of the Ni2+ cations (i.e., outer-sphere complex) or the formation of hydrogen bonds (as illustrated in Fig. 34). After one-step pyrolysis at a higher temperature (e.g., 700 °C), nickel (Ni0) nanoparticles were successfully embedded in situ in the carbon matrix. However, the initial dispersion of the preformed Ni0 nanoparticles is very poor, due to the difficulty of inserting them into the lignocellulosic matrix,248 which can be solved by optimizing the surface properties, such as surface area and pore structure. For example, it can be converted into activated carbon. Yu et al.249–251 synthesized Ni-loaded activated carbon (Ni/AC) by carbothermal reduction, which exhibited remarkable hydrogenolysis activity compared with reduced Ni/AC, KBH4, or H2.249
 Fig. 34  Schematic of the mechanism of nickel ion (Ni2+) adsorption in a lignocellulosic matrix (i.e., rice husk fiber) via impregnation under atmospheric pressure or vacuum (reproduced with permission from ref. 246 and 247). 
Nature has evolved structures that are optimized for specific functions, for example, the porous tubular arrays in wood that confer strength and flexibility while simultaneously providing a route for water and nutrient transport through the tree. Many of these natural structures contain complex ordered biopolymers, such as the microfibrillar composite of cellulose and lignin in the cell walls of biomass. By templating the biomaterial, we can harness this structural complexity, either to mimic a natural function or for an entirely new purpose (e.g., catalysis). In this case, the templating focuses on hard templating of native biomass to produce negative (inverse) and positive (hollow) copies or direct replicas (Fig. 35A). A wide range of biomaterials, including wood, paper, cotton, and fungi, have been used to template ceramic materials.252–254 Commonly, this templating can be achieved by infiltrating the biomaterial with inorganic precursors and then precipitating an amorphous, polycrystalline, or, more rarely, single-crystal ceramic product. The template could essentially be considered as a cast, which provides a confined space for mineralization. However, the choice of conditions and the inorganic precursor can have a dramatic effect on the resulting material. For example, templating wood with sol–gel silica precursors by slow hydrolysis and condensation produced a fine-structured replica, since the precursors were able to leach into the cell walls (Fig. 35B).255 Fast hydrolysis, on the other hand, formed a simple inverse structure (Fig. 35C). An advantage of many organic biomaterials for templating is that they often exhibit functional surfaces owing to the biopolymer content. These can be exploited to achieve accurate templating via surface-specific chemisorption of, for example, metal alkoxides onto the hydroxyl-rich surface of cellulose fibers (Fig. 35D).256–259 Careful sequential adsorption and hydrolysis enables deposition of a thin metal oxide layer and maintains nanoscale structural features. If the biotemplate does not readily adsorb metals, it can also be modified by chemical functionalization, enzyme treatment, or mild hydrolysis.260–262 Another route is decorating the biomass surface with noble-metal nanoparticles for the surface-specific electroless deposition of metals.263
 Fig. 35  (A) Schematic of the different methods of templating to produce (1) negative and (2) positive copies and (3) direct replicas. Examples of (B) a replica and (C) an inverse copy of poplar wood via the slow and fast hydrolysis of silica precursors; (D) schematic of sequential chemisorption and hydrolysis to build atomic-sized layers of metal oxides on a template surface (reproduced with permission from ref. 255 and 257). 
Biomass itself can be used both as a template and as a carbon source for metal carbides production. This is typically achieved by initially carbonizing the biomaterial and then infiltrating the resulting charcoal template with a liquid or gaseous metal reagent to form a metal carbide replica such as TiC or ZrC.264,265 Alternatively, the metal carbide can be produced in one step by soaking the biomaterial in an aqueous metal salt before pyrolysis.266,267 In this case, the metal precursor first decomposes to give an oxide coating over the template before carbothermal reduction to give a carbide replica with the biological features still intact. By heating to a lower temperature (avoiding carbothermal reduction), biomaterials can also be widely used to template carbon/metal oxide composites.268,269 Another example that exploits carbon-rich biomaterial as both template and reducing agent is the formation of metal sulfides from cellulose fibers.270
The synthesis of bimetallic alloys often requires sophisticated chemical and physical methods, including non-aqueous organometallic routes,271 ultrasonic spray pyrolysis272 or reducing agents such as borohydrides that yield FeNi nanoparticles with boron inclusions.273 Furthermore, incipient wetness and co-impregnation methods were employed.274 Recently, Chieffi et al.275 used a low-cost rapid approach for the synthesis of carbon-supported catalysts, consisting in the impregnation of cellulose filter paper (CFP) with the metal precursors Ni(NO3)2 and Fe(NO3)3 in optimal mixing ratios. Afterwards, carbothermal reduction of iron and nickel salts was performed leading to isolated nanoparticles on the CFP that substantially retain the morphology of the paper on the microscale after thermal treatment. On the other hand, the metals trigger the reorganization of the carbon matrix on the nanoscale, resulting in the formation of a graphene shell which encapsulates and stabilizes the nanoparticles (as illustrated in Fig. 36). Consequently, reducing agents and multi-step and time-consuming syntheses have been completely avoided, thus affording a catalyst based entirely on sustainable precursors.
 Fig. 36  (A) Schematic diagram of the synthesis of CFP FeNi catalyst used for hydrogenation; (B) high-magnification SEM image of the CFP FeNi catalyst; (C) low-magnification TEM image and (D) high-resolution TEM image with labeled lattice fringes of CFP-supported FeNi alloy nanoparticles. The SAED pattern shown in the inset of (C) is indexed to the fcc lattice of FeNi alloy nanoparticles (reproduced with permission from ref. 275). 
In addition, carbon nanofibers (CNFs) are excellent support materials that can act as scaffolds to direct the growth of metallic nanoparticles with uniform shape and tunable size.276,277 Mahata et al.278 reported that Ni0 nanoparticles stabilized with CNFs exhibited good catalytic performance in the hydrogenation of nitrobenzene, producing clean aniline in 99% yield. The main challenge is cost-effective and environmentally friendly synthesis of the Ni–CNF catalyst. The conventional method, i.e. carbon vapor deposition and subsequent reduction of nickel salts to synthesize the Ni–CNFs,279,280 usually involves complex synthetic procedures, complicated equipment,280 and extra reducing agents (e.g., NaBH4, NH2–NH2, and H2).281 Fast pyrolysis of lignocellulosic biomass can not only produce bio-oil, but also produces low-boiling-point reducing species (e.g., CxHy, H2, and CO).282,283 Abundant reductants and anoxic conditions could offer an ideal environment for the reduction of nickel salts. Recently, Liu et al.284,285 found that CNFs can be formed via the pyrolysis of lignocellulosic biomass along with FeCl3 catalysis. Then, they developed a facile process to obtain Ni nanoparticles embedded in CNFs by pyrolyzing Ni,Fe-containing lignocellulosic biomass.286 The Ni2+/Fe3+-preloaded lignocellulosic biomass was rapidly pyrolyzed at 873 K,287,288 during which a Ni–NiFe2O4/CNF catalyst, as well as renewable bio-oil, were harvested.289,290 During the fast-pyrolysis process, the lignin, celluloses, and hemicelluloses in biomass quickly decomposed into volatiles and char. NiCl2 and FeCl3 are converted into Ni0 nanoparticles and magnetic NiFe2O4, respectively, by reducing agents (e.g., CO, H2, and C) formed during biomass decomposition. A similar feature was demonstrated for the formation of Cu–CuFe2O4/graphene composites under hydrothermal conditions.291 Moreover, FeCl3 catalyzed the decomposition of volatiles into small-molecule hydrocarbons, such as CH4, C2H4, and C2H2,292 which can be deposited on the surface of char to form CNFs under the catalysis of newly formed Ni and Fe compounds (e.g., NiFe2O4 and Ni) in a way similar to that in the CVD process.282,293 In the XRD pattern, only peaks of NiFe2O4 (2θ = 29.8°, 35.3°, 37.2°, 43.5°, 53.5°, 56.7°, 62.4°, and 74.1°) and metallic Ni (2θ = 44.7°, 51.9°, and 76.4°) are found, and no other crystalline Fe and Ni species are observed (Fig. 37A). It is confirmed that the catalytic activity and selectivity of the catalyst should be ascribed to Ni0 species, whereas its separability is ascribed to NiFe2O4. Fig. 37B shows SEM and TEM images of Ni–NiFe2O4/CNFs, which possess a rough morphology, comprising a nanofiber structure with lots of particles embedded in it. The TEM image indicates that the particles are evenly distributed in the CNFs with an average size below 50 nm. As a support, the CNFs provide a relatively high surface area, which promotes dispersion of the Ni nanoparticles, increasing the catalytic activity of materials.294 It is noteworthy that Ni–NiFe2O4/CNFs can be quickly separated by an external magnetic field from the reaction mixture. NiFe2O4 endows the Ni–NiFe2O4/CNF composite with high separability due to its magnetic properties (Fig. 37C). In addition, a mechanism for the hydrogenation of nitrobenzene by Ni–NiFe2O4/CNFs has been proposed. As illustrated in Fig. 37D, H2 was initially activated by the evenly distributed Ni nanoparticles to form active H atoms295,296 and then it transferred electrons to another acceptor (the nitro group of nitrobenzene) due to the high graphitization degree of CNFs.297,298 Finally, protonated H combined with the acceptor to form the hydrogenation product.299 On the other hand, due to the relatively low hydrogen activation ability, the H atoms activated by the Ni nanoparticles cannot react further with other reducible functional groups (e.g., CO, C–OH, C–Cl or C–Br) at low H2 pressure, endowing the catalyst with high selectivity.
 Fig. 37  (A) XRD pattern of Ni–NiFe2O4/CNFs, (B) SEM and TEM images of Ni–NiFe2O4/CNFs, (C) magnetic hysteresis loop of Ni–NiFe2O4/CNFs recorded at 300 K. The photographs (inset) show that Ni–NiFe2O4/CNFs in methanol solution can be quickly separated by a magnet, and (D) catalytic mechanism of Ni–NiFe2O4/CNFs for the hydrogenation of nitrobenzene (reproduced with permission from ref. 286). 
4.3. Copper (Cu) nanostructures

4.3.1. Copper oxide (Cu2O). 
The interaction of carbon monoxide (i.e., CO) with CuO has been investigated with the primary aim of using CuO to catalyze the water gas shift reaction: CO + H2O → CO2 + H2. CO is able to reduce bulk CuO powders between 236 and 500 °C.300,301 Under high concentrations of CO, CuO can be directly converted into Cu. However, when the CO concentration is low, the reduction process occurs as CuO to Cu2O to Cu. The removal of oxygen and the formation of a non-stoichiometric and disordered Cu2O1+δ were also reported.300 Wu et al.302 synthesized vertically aligned dense Cu2O nanowire arrays by carbothermal reduction of CuO nanowires. Periodic nodule formation is observed in the Cu2O nanowires, which is found to be driven by Rayleigh instability during the phase transformation process. This approach can lead to the formation of a diversity of hierarchical one-dimensional Cu2O nanostructures (as shown in Fig. 38A). Besides, an “antenna effect” was found in mass transport where CuO nanowire arrays with a large specific surface area lead to faster phase transformation compared to planar films. Continuous oxygen out-diffusion could prevent the Cu2O nanowire from fragmenting and collapsing; therefore, it results in dense arrays of vertically aligned nodular Cu2O nanowires. Furthermore, a possible mechanism of the transformation of CuO nanowires into Cu2O nanowires under carbothermal reduction conditions is proposed (as illustrated in Fig. 38B). Carbothermal reduction can occur at temperatures as low as 350 °C and is described by the reaction 2CuO + CO → Cu2O + CO2. It can be found that: (1) the reduction front initiates at the nanowire surface and proceeds through the wire and into the underlying film; (2) oxygen out-diffusion is the primary process by which reduction of CuO to Cu2O takes place, thus creating an intermediate non-stoichiometric phase given as Cu2O1+δ; and (3) the presence of nanowires, i.e., extra surface area, enhances the rate of reduction. This reduction scheme leads to Rayleigh instability and the growth of periodic nodules on the nanowire surfaces. Fragmentation is avoided and vertically aligned viable Cu2O nanowires are formed. Alternatively, under a Cu chemical potential gradient and Ar atmosphere, Cu can be made to diffuse into CuO nanowires with the formation of a Cu2O phase and nodules, represented as CuO + Cu → Cu2O.
 Fig. 38  (A) Schematic of Cu2O nanowires synthesis via the carbothermal reduction of CuO nanowires; (B) mechanism of the transformation of CuO nanowires into Cu2O nanowires under carbothermal reduction conditions: (a) starting foil, (b) incipient stage of the transformation process, (c) full conversion of the nanowires and partial conversion of the underlying oxide film, and (d) complete conversion of the CuO nanowires and underlying oxide film (reproduced with permission from ref. 302). 


4.3.2. Cu–C nanocomposites. 
A number of methods have been developed to synthesize porous metal–carbon nanocomposites. Many are based on conventional sol–gel synthesis of carbon aerogels by pyrolysis of polymeric gels, with metal nanoparticles incorporated into the aerogel matrix.303–315 Nevertheless, such metal–carbon nanocomposites have been limited to a relatively low metal loading of <20 wt% and large monolith densities of ≥100 mg cm−3, as scaling of sol–gel methods to a regime of ultra-low densities and high metal loadings has proven to be non-trivial. Charnvanichborikarn et al.316 have found that trapping Cu nanoparticles highly dispersed in a solvent while carbon aerogel precursors undergo gelation causes gel shrinkage and results in porous monoliths with relatively high densities of ≥65 mg cm−3 for Cu loadings of ≥10 wt%. To realize lower-density Cu–C composites, they also developed an approach that involves filling the pores of robust carbon nanotube (CNT)-based aerogels317 with a CuSO4 solution, followed by freeze-drying to remove water and thermal decomposition of CuSO4, which has yielded nanoporous Cu–C composites with a minimum C scaffold density of 25 mg cm−3 and Cu loadings up to 70 wt%.316 Then, they proposed a novel route to obtain Cu–C nanocomposites with an unprecedented minimum C scaffold density of 5 mg cm−3 and controllable Cu loading up to 65 wt%.318 They described the fabrication of porous Cu–C nanocomposites by water-ice templating in aqueous CuSO4 using a suspension of graphene oxide (GO) and CNTs (as illustrated in Fig. 39). Upon crystallization of water, the salt and carbon constituents segregate at grain boundaries of water crystallites. Freeze-drying (vacuum desiccation) is used to remove water for the creation of pores. The final Cu–C composites are obtained by thermally induced reduction of GO and decomposition of CuSO4. In such nanocomposites, mechanical integrity was ensured by a random network of interconnected reduced GO (rGO) sheets and CNTs, while the Cu constituent was in the form of Cu nanoparticles decorating the rGO sheets and CNTs. They explored the flexibility of this method for controlling pore and nanoparticle sizes, Cu loading, and the final monolith density. Furthermore, they demonstrated its applicability to the synthesis of other porous metal–carbon nanocomposites, e.g., Ag–C.318
 Fig. 39  Schematic showing the fabrication process of Cu–C composites (reproduced with permission from ref. 318). 
Flame spray synthesis or ultrasonic spray pyrolysis is known to be a promising process that can be used to produce functional metal particles. In flame spray synthesis, particles are formed via a gas-to-particle conversion mechanism.319–323 Conventional flame spray synthesis has been applied to synthesize oxide nanoparticles in an ambient air environment. To synthesize metal particles, the gas mixture from flame reactions could be altered from CO2/H2O (oxidizing conditions, traditional set-up) to CO/H2/H2O (reducing conditions).319 Combustion of metal–organic precursors in the flame process at a high ratio of fuel to oxygen makes it possible to obtain metallic nanoparticles, since H2 and CO gases formed by decomposition of metal precursors are involved in the complete consumption of the available oxygen and the reduction of oxides to metals. Athanassiou et al.320 synthesized metallic copper nanoparticles covered with thin carbon layers via reducing flame spray synthesis, in which copper(II) 2-ethylhexanoate was completely decomposed in highly reducing conditions. However, for the reducing flame, a special system of metal–organic precursor and solvent is required. Ultrasonic spray pyrolysis, as an aerosol process, can be used to prepare functional particles from cost-effective metal salt precursors. In conventional spray pyrolysis, formation of CuO or Cu2O is inevitable, although N2 gas is used as a carrier gas, as most Cu precursors containing oxygen (e.g., nitrate or acetate) are used as aqueous solutions. Therefore, addition of H2 to the carrier gas (e.g., N2) is needed for the reduction of CuO to Cu.324 Kim et al.325 developed an approach to produce pure Cu particles via spray pyrolysis without the use of hydrogen or other reducing gases. A low-carbon-number alcohol such as ethanol and methanol was used as a reducing agent. In ethanol-assisted spray pyrolysis, reduction of CuO or Cu2O to metallic Cu is basically achieved by H2 or CO generated from decomposition of ethanol. Zhong et al.326 studied the effect of ethanol and ethylene glycol (EG) as co-solvents on generation of micrometer-sized copper particles from a copper nitrate precursor, which made it possible to obtain oxide-free copper particles at temperatures higher than 400 °C; however, hollow particles were obtained. The mechanism of particle generation was proposed as follows: (1) Cu(NO3)2 is initially hydrolyzed to Cu2(OH)3NO3 after the evaporation of solvents; (2) Cu2(OH)3NO3 decomposes to CuO and Cu2O; and (3) reduction of these oxides is achieved under a reducing atmosphere, which is created by decomposition of ethanol or EG. Firmansyah et al.327 studied the effect of ethanol concentration on the phase transformation of copper oxides in ethanol-assisted spray pyrolysis. Two pathways for the generation of metallic Cu particles were proposed. On the one hand, the Cu2O phase formed initially was further oxidized to CuO, followed by reduction to Cu at low temperatures (450 ≤ T ≤ 525 °C); on the other hand, Cu2O was directly reduced to Cu at a high temperature (T ≥ 525 °C). Subsequently, Cho et al.328 developed a new approach to produce core–shell structured carbon-coated Cu particles via hydrogen-free ultrasonic spray pyrolysis (as illustrated in Fig. 40). In order to obtain pure metallic Cu particles having a thin carbon layer, citric acid and sucrose were used as the reducing agent and carbon source, respectively. Initially, Cu(NO3)2/CA/sucrose precipitates were formed after the complete drying of droplets. Thereafter, C/CuO composites were formed by decomposition of copper nitrate and carbonization of organic additives, producing reducing gases (CO and H2) under a nitrogen atmosphere. In the core region of the particles, reduction of CuO to Cu can be achieved by carbothermal reduction. As a result, the carbon inside the particles seems to be removed. On the contrary, in the surface region of the particles, reducing gases such as CO and H2 could be involved in the reduction of CuO to Cu, generating the C/Cu composite region.
 Fig. 40  Formation mechanism of core–shell structured carbon-coated copper by spray pyrolysis (reproduced with permission from ref. 328). 
Recently, Liu et al.329 synthesized a magnetic carbon material anchored to Cu nanoparticles (Cu&Fe3O4-mC) by fast pyrolysis of biomass polluted by heavy metals. The synthesis of Cu&Fe3O4-mC includes two main stages, i.e., metal loading and fast pyrolysis. In the metal-loading stage, Cu and Fe were adsorbed onto the biomass in the form of Cu2+ and Fe3+, respectively. In this stage, heavy metals in water can be removed by adsorption; therefore, the wastewater was decontaminated. Since interaction between metal ions and biomass occurs on the surface of biomass, and adsorption of metal ions by biomass is a single-layer adsorption process,330 Cu2+ and Fe3+ hydrolyzed forms were dispersed on the surface of the biomass after drying. In the fast-pyrolysis stage, biomass was thermally decomposed and the volatile components produced were swept out by a carrier gas to form bio-oil and pyrolysis gas at 773 K. Meanwhile, the adsorbed Cu2+ and Fe3+ species were reduced by the reducing components (e.g., H2, CO, and biochar) derived from the pyrolysis process (eqn 25–28).331–333Fig. 41A shows typical XRD patterns of the metal-preloaded biomass and Cu&Fe3O4-mC. It can be seen that before pyrolysis, both Fe and Cu in the biomass are amorphous, and the diffraction peak at a 2θ value of 22.9° is attributed to the cellulose crystal plane.334 After pyrolysis, the Fe and Cu form crystals, while the cellulose crystal plane disappears, confirming the decomposition of the cellulose structure during pyrolysis. The diffraction peaks at 2θ = 43.5°, 50.7°, and 74.3° are assigned to Cu crystal planes, respectively, indicating that Cu in the char is in a metallic form with face-centered cubic crystals (27.3 nm). The diffraction peaks at 2θ = 36.4°, 42.2°, and 61.2° indicate the formation of Fe3O4 crystallites, which is the main reason for the magnetic properties of the produced materials. As shown in Fig. 41B, the hysteresis loop of Cu&Fe3O4-mC is symmetrical in the magnetic field intensity (H) range of −20 to 20 kOe, and its saturation magnetization (M) reaches 20.6 emu g−1 at 10 kOe, indicating favorable ferromagnetic properties of the material. Such properties are mainly significant for convenient recycling of the catalyst in liquid-phase reactions. There are two types of particles on the carbon support: the particles of micron size are attributed to Fe3O4 particles, whereas the nanosized particles are attributed to Cu particles (Fig. 41C and D). More than 90% of the total Cu nanoparticles possess a particle size of 15–25 nm, confirming that the Cu nanoparticles are highly monodisperse on the carbon support.335 In addition, a possible reaction mechanism for the reduction of 4-nitrophenol by NaBH4 under the catalysis of Cu&Fe3O4-mC is schematically proposed in Fig. 41E. BH4− ions were initially adsorbed on the surface of Cu nanoparticles to form a Cu hydride complex. Meanwhile, 4-nitrophenol was reversibly adsorbed on Cu nanoparticles (i.e., adsorption accompanied by desorption). Consequently, hydrogen and electrons are transferred from the Cu hydride complex to the nitro group of 4-nitrophenol, which goes through hydrodeoxygenation reactions to form the final product, 4-aminophenol (eqn (29)).336 2Fe(NO3)3 + 6H2O → 2Fe(OH)3 + 6HNO3↑ → 2FeO(OH) → Fe2O3(25) Cu(NO3)2 + 2H2O → Cu(OH)2 + 2HNO3↑ → CuO(26) 3Fe2O3 + 3(H2, CO, C) → 2Fe3O4 + 3(H2O, CO2, CO)↑(27) CuO + 3(H2, CO, C) → Cu + (H2O, CO2, CO)↑(28) (29)
 Fig. 41  (A) XRD patterns of metal-preloaded biomass and magnetic carbon anchored to Fe and Cu nanoparticles (Cu&Fe3O4-mC); (B) magnetic hysteresis curves of Cu&Fe3O4-mC and (inset) the photographs show Cu&Fe3O4-mC in the reaction solution attracted and arranged vertically using a magnet; (C) SEM image of Cu&Fe3O4-mC; (D) TEM image of Cu nanoparticles in Cu&Fe3O4-mC, (inset) high-resolution TEM image of Cu nanoparticles; (E) reaction mechanism for the reduction of 4-nitrophenol by NaBH4 with the catalysis of Cu&Fe3O4-mC (reproduced with permission from ref. 329). 

4.4. Cobalt (co) nanostructures

4.4.1. Cobalt carbide. 
Cobalt carbide (e.g., Co2C) has been widely studied as a permanent magnet337 and heterogeneous catalyst,338 because of its high coercivity337 and high surface area339 combined with high activity,340 respectively. Combination of metals or metal oxides with a carbon source is a common method for the synthesis of metal carbides (i.e., carbothermal reduction).341 Graphite or synthetic pitch is usually employed as a carbon source.342,343 For this solid-state reaction, mass transport to the reaction interface is the rate-limiting step. A high reaction temperature (typically >1000 °C) is therefore necessary to facilitate the diffusion of ions/atoms through the solid phases. Therefore, Hasin344 reported the utilization of graphene oxide (GO) as a carbon source for the synthesis of Co2C. In comparison with other carbon sources, GO contains only a few layers of carbon345 and it could facilitate the diffusion of carbon atoms.346 Another merit is that the oxygen-containing functional groups (–COOH, –OH, and –CO groups)347–349 in GO can facilitate direct nucleation of metal oxide nanocrystals on its surface, thus resulting in intimate contact between the two phases.350–352 Coupled with its high monodispersity,353 these properties make GO an attractive carbon source for the synthesis of Co2C. A simple ammonia-evaporation-induced method is proposed for the synthesis of a GO/Co3O4 nanocomposite, which is then converted to Co2C by thermal reduction in a H2/N2 atmosphere. Compared to conventional high-temperature synthesis of metal carbides, utilization of GO as a carbon source enables metal carbide synthesis at the lowest reported temperature. The end product is mesoporous Co2C with a large surface area. Moreover, the product exhibits superparamagnetic behavior. Fig. 42 illustrates the formation of mesoporous Co2C. Firstly, positively charged cobalt ions are attracted to the surface of a GO sheet due to electrostatic attraction from the negatively charged functional groups (e.g., epoxide, hydroxyl, and carboxylic groups) present on the surface of the GO sheet.348,354,355 This intimate contact facilitates nucleation of metal oxide during ammonia evaporation at 80 °C, and the resulting Co3O4 nanoparticles are more thoroughly wrapped in GO sheets. At a low annealing temperature (200 °C) under a reducing atmosphere, graphene is formed and simultaneously reacts with Co3O4 to form Co2C. The single carbon atomic layer is believed to facilitate the diffusion of carbon atoms, resulting in such a low synthesis temperature. It is suggested that the synthesis of Co2C using GO as a carbon source can be extended to other metal carbides (e.g., WC).354
 Fig. 42  Scheme showing a proposed formation route to prepare Co2C (taken with permission from ref. 344). 


4.4.2. Cobalt (Co0). 
The rational design of non-noble metal catalysts with an electrocatalytic activity comparable or even superior to that of Pt is extremely important for future fuel cell-based renewable energy devices. Xia et al.356 demonstrated a new concept: that a metal–organic framework (MOF) can be used as a precursor to encapsulate Co@Co3O4@C core@bishell nanoparticles in situ into a highly ordered porous carbon matrix (CM) (denoted as Co@Co3O4@C–CM), as illustrated in Fig. 43. Firstly, CM with hexagonally ordered mesopores was synthesized357 and functionalized with carboxyl groups,358 which were allowed to coordinate with metal ions and initiate the growth of MOF crystals after adding MOF precursors.359,360 The highly ordered CM is robust and cannot be destroyed even after a high-temperature carbonization process. Subsequently, an MOF constructed from a cobalt center and a benzimidazole ligand361 was introduced into the confined space of the highly ordered CM. The as-prepared MOF–CM composite was pyrolyzed in an inert atmosphere to obtain Co@C–CM. Notably, the Co complex could be easily decomposed and Co2+ ions segregated from the MOF during the initial pyrolyzing stage and then agglomerated to form ultra-fine cobalt nanoparticles362 on carbon rods in the highly ordered porous CM. As the heat treatment proceeded, these nanoparticles could aggregate to form larger Co@C nanoparticles to decrease their surface energy. These Co nanoparticles supported on the CM were highly active and their surface layers were then oxidized to Co3O4 by controlled heating in air, thus forming core@bishell Co@Co3O4@C nanoparticles on the porous CM. The as-formed oxidized outer layers could protect the Co core from further oxidation.363 From the Co–O phase diagram, the stable phase is Co3O4 at elevated temperatures.364,365 From this point of view, the Co@Co3O4 nanoparticles were also completely oxidized to Co3O4 by heating the composite at 150 °C in air for 96 h, resulting in Co3O4@C–CM. Moreover, Co@Co3O4@C could be synthesized by heating a pure MOF crystal under an Ar flow and further oxidizing the obtained Co@C in air.
 Fig. 43  Schematic illustration of the synthesis of Co@Co3O4@C–CM: (a) functionalization of CM with carboxylate groups, (b) growth of MOF on CM, (c) heat treatment under Ar flow and (d) oxidation in air at 90 °C (reproduced with permission from ref. 356). 
In summary, there is a series of reactions for the carbothermal reduction of Co3O4. Co3O4 can be reduced by carbon directly to metallic Co (30) and (31), or to CoO first and then to Co (32)–(35).366 For each route, the gas product could be CO or CO2. The standard Gibbs free energies (ΔG0) of reactions (30)–(35) as a function of temperature are plotted in Fig. 44. From the thermodynamics, reactions (31) and (33) are more feasible compared to reactions (30) and (32) below 707.9 °C, i.e., CO2 will be produced as the gas product. With respect to reactions (31) and (33), the latter occurs more easily even at low temperature. According to Fig. 44B, CoO can be reduced by carbon to metallic Co via reactions (34) and (35) at temperatures below or above 707.9 °C, respectively. Co3O4 + 4C → 3Co + 4CO (g)(30) Co3O4 + 2C → 3Co + 2CO2 (g)(31) Co3O4 + C → 3CoO + CO (g)(32) Co3O4 + 1/2C → 3CoO + 1/2CO2 (g)(33) CoO + C → Co + CO (g)(34) CoO + 1/2C → Co + 1/2CO2 (g)(35)
 Fig. 44  Gibbs free energies (ΔG0) of reactions (A: Co3O4 to Co, CoO) and (B: CoO to Co) via carbothermal reduction (reproduced with permission from ref. 366). 

4.5. Titanium (Ti) nanostructures
Titanium carbide (TiC) has received much attention for catalytic and electrochemical applications, apart from its application in sintered composite materials such as Al2O3–TiC composites. Carbothermal reduction of TiO2 by solid carbon materials in an inert atmosphere or vacuum is the most effective method to produce TiC for applications in composite materials.367,368 According to Weimer,369 synthesis of TiC by this method requires high temperature (1700–2100 °C) and a long reaction time (10–24 h)370 in an argon environment. Therefore, it presents the problems of grain growth, particle agglomeration, non-uniform particle shape and large amounts of unreacted TiO2 and carbon in the product.367 Woo et al.371 produced homogeneous ultra-fine TiC powders via carbothermal reduction. Carbothermal reduction of a mixture of TiO2 and carbon resin at 1500 °C proceeded as follows. Firstly, partially reduced TiO2 particles were agglomerated in the initial stage of carbothermal reduction with sizes from 500 to 1000 nm. In the intermediate stage, the particles were broken down into fine particles and the many holes in the middle of the particles were attributed to the release of gaseous reduction products such as CO. Finally, homogeneous fine TiC particles with a size of 80 nm were formed at 1500 °C for 15 min. In addition, TiC powders can be synthesized by ball milling with carbothermal reduction of titania/charcoal in vacuum conditions. Xu et al.372,373 investigated the preparation of TiC by carbothermal reduction of TiO2 in vacuum conditions. Fine TiC powders (2.05 μm) with low impurity were obtained by ball milling and heating a titania/charcoal mixture (molar ratio 1:4) to 1450 °C for 8 h at 1–60 Pa.
In the development of convenient syntheses of solid-solution carbides, microwave-assisted carbothermal reduction (MWCR) has received attention. There have been some studies on the successful sintering of metal powders via microwave heat, such as copper, iron, tungsten and magnesium powders.374–378 The use of microwaves can increase the heating rate and substantially reduce the sintering time in comparison with conventional sintering. Zhang et al.379 prepared ultra-fine (Ti,W,Nb)C solid-solution powders via MWCR after high-energy ball milling oxide–graphite mixtures. Compared with carbothermal powder techniques, the hybrid polymer route is promising for synthesizing TiC by in situ carbothermal reduction, due to convenient control of composition, homogeneity, and morphology.380,381 Yu et al.382 synthesized highly ordered mesoporous nanocrystalline TiC/carbon composites by a solvent-evaporation-induced self-assembly approach combined with in situ carbothermal reduction. As illustrated in Fig. 45, chelated titanium citrate clusters can themselves interact independently with triblock copolymer F127 templates, forming ordered mesostructures, similar to the behavior of metal aqua complexes383 and titanium trifluoroacetate.384 Furthermore, metal citrate complexes can crosslink with polyols (resols) upon curing to form polymer composites, whereas titanium citrate clusters and resols interact with triblock copolymer templates, thereby resulting in the cooperative assembly and subsequent curing of ordered mesostructures with a high titanium content and dispersion. The situation is different with tri-component assembly of mesostructured hybrid silicate–polymer nanocomposites,216 which leads to silicate–polymer phase separation in the latter case. Therefore, phase separation may not occur in hybrid polymers derived from resols, titanium chelate complexes, and triblock copolymers. By thermal curing and pyrolysis, resols will undergo thermopolymerization to form phenolic polymer frameworks and ordered mesopores are opened up with a high dispersion of amorphous titanium oxide species in the carbon matrix. In situ carbothermal reduction is carried out to obtain TiC nanocrystals in carbon matrices.
 Fig. 45  Schematic of the synthesis of ordered mesoporous titanium carbide/carbon composites by a solvent-evaporation-induced self-assembly approach and subsequent in situ carbothermal reduction (reproduced with permission from ref. 382). 
Metal–organic frameworks (MOFs), like carbon-supported metals, are a class of organic/inorganic hybrid materials that have attracted more attention due to their various practical applications.385–392 MOFs have been used as catalysts, in particular due to their high dispersion of active sites, well-defined structures, microporosity, and tunable physicochemical properties (alteration of metal atoms/ligands).393–397 Furthermore, post-synthetic modification of MOFs bearing certain functional groups (e.g., –OH,398,399 –NH2,400 and pyridine401) with organic or inorganic compounds has led to an increased promise of practical applications.402 This is partially caused by the high dispersion of organic/inorganic compounds attainable via coordination chemistry.398 In catalysis, however, the chemical and thermal stability of post-synthetically modified MOFs limits their recyclability, frequently resulting in structural degradation and significant leaching from their active metal sites.396,398,399,403,404 To overcome this obstacle and provide high stability, MOFs can be used as templates for the formation of various carbon-supported materials.405–408 Using their ability to provide strong coordination between active metal precursors and the unique and diverse chemical functionalities incorporated in MOFs, a method was envisioned to include carbonization and carbothermal reduction (CCR) in one step. This resulted in a material that retained the high surface area and high dispersion of active metal sites inherent in a post-synthetically modified MOF, while imparting the high chemical and thermal stability of carbon-supported materials. Kim et al.409 reported a novel method for the preparation of titanium oxide nanoparticles supported on nanoporous amorphous carbon (Ti/NC) via post-synthetic modification of a Zn-based MOF bearing an amine functionality (i.e., IRMOF-3) with titanium isopropoxide followed by carbothermal pyrolysis. This material exhibited high purity, high surface area (>1000 m2 g−1), and high dispersion of metal oxide nanoparticles, while retaining a small particle size (around 4 nm). As illustrated in Fig. 46, carbonization of IRMOF-3 is initiated by cleavage of the bond between the zinc oxide octahedral cluster and the organic linker. This carbonization step was immediately followed by the formation of ZnO in the temperature range between 200 and 550 °C. When the IRMOF-3/Ti material was subjected to CCR at 550 °C, the resulting material contained crystalline ZnO. As the temperature was increased above 800 °C, ZnO was reduced to Zn via carbothermal reduction, leading to continuous consumption of the carbon source until evaporation of Zn occurred at >910 °C.406 Concurrently, titanium oxide nanoparticles were produced via thermal decomposition initiated by cleavage of the coordinate bond between the titanium precursor [Ti(O–iPr)3] and the amine functionality (–NH2) of the organic linker in IRMOF-3 above 300 °C.410–412 The cleaved fragments of the titanium precursor were subsequently decomposed via pyrolysis and carbothermal reduction413,414 to form titanium oxide nanoparticles, which then underwent further carbothermal reduction, resulting in carbon consumption. This type of carbon consumption could presumably give rise to the formation of concave holes for stronger immobilization of titanium oxide nanoparticles to prevent their aggregation at high temperature (i.e. 1000 °C).415 As a result of this synthetic process, the CCR method can produce unique materials that are highly stable and active for oxidation reactions.
 Fig. 46  Proposed mechanism for the formation of Ti/NC (taken with permission from ref. 409). 
It has been proved that carbides and nitrides of transition metals have excellent properties such as a high melting temperature, high hardness and high abrasion resistance. They have also been widely applied in the mechanical, electronics, and chemical industries.416 Titanium carbonitride (Ti(C,N)), with its good mechanical properties, is a major material for Ti(C,N)-based cermets when mixed with nickel/cobalt, molybdenum carbide, and other materials.417 Cermets using a solid-solution powder of (Ti,Mo)(C,N) as a hard phase have been studied to improve the properties of cermets.418,419 Several methods are available to synthesize titanium nanostructures such as titanium carbide, titanium nitride and titanium carbonitride. The first method is direct carbonization/nitridation of titanium metal or titanium hydride, which is well established as an industrial production method for carbides and nitrides. However, the produced powders require extensive milling in order to yield fine particles. The second method is carbothermal reduction of titanium dioxide with carbon sources in a controlled atmosphere at a relatively high temperature. Consequently, Ti(C,N) powder can be synthesized by a solid-solution reaction between TiC and TiN at high temperatures. The produced powders usually have a wide particle size distribution and tight agglomeration; therefore, they need subsequent milling. Carbothermal reduction offers the possibility of an economically attractive synthetic route for Ti(C,N) powders, which possess a particle size larger than 0.5 μm due to grain growth. Moreover, they exhibit tight agglomeration between individual particulates. Although ultra-fine TiO2 powder has been used as a starting material to synthesize a fine carbide powder,367,420–422 the particle size of the resulting powder is larger than that of the original TiO2 particles. Recently, Matsuda et al.423 investigated particle size control during carbothermal reduction of TiO2 for the industrial production of Ti(C,N) powder. The effects of oxide additives including MoO3, WO3, ZrO2, Nb2O5 and Ta2O5 on the carbothermal reduction of fine TiO2 were studied for obtaining nanopowders. A mixed powder of TiO2, C and additives was thermally treated in flowing Ar up to 1500 °C. It was confirmed that MoO3 and WO3 were effective in yielding fine particles with sizes of 0.1–0.2 μm. The addition of MoO3 can result in restriction of grain growth in the TinO2n−1 phase. Furthermore, the vapor pressure of MoO3 was highest among the oxides investigated under an inert atmosphere, showing that Mo compounds were easily diffused via the gas phase and were homogeneously distributed in the powder.423
4.6. Zinc (Zn) nanostructures

4.6.1. Zinc oxide (ZnO). 
Previously, it has been discussed that one-dimensional ZnO nanostructures including nanowires, nanorods, and nanosheets could be synthesized via carbothermal reduction. For instance, as the synthesis of nanowires or nanorods is basically a process of growing a single crystal from vapor, the source vapors for constructing ZnO nanowires should be derived from the source materials. As shown in Fig. 47a, the source vapors could be Zn (g) + O2 and/or ZnO (g), depending on the process. In the case of a carbothermal reduction process, it has been reported that ZnO nanowires and various nanostructures can be fabricated via a vapor–liquid–solid (VLS) crystallization process on an Au-coated silicon substrate by heating a mixture of ZnO and graphite in an argon flow, as shown in Fig. 47(b).424–426 Hitherto, most ZnO/SnO2 mixed nanostructures have been produced by a two-step route at low pressure, where each oxide is synthesized by a carbothermal reduction method sequentially. For example, Hwang et al.427 synthesized ZnO/SnO2 core–shell nanowires via a two-step carbothermal reduction method with the system pressure around 10−2 Torr, whereas Lan et al.428 produced hierarchical nanostructures at a pressure of 1 Pa. Tharsika et al.429 synthesized ZnO/SnO2 nanowires via a single-step carbothermal reduction according to the catalyst-assisted VLS mechanism. Metal nanoparticles or nanoclusters are used as nucleation seeds, which determine the growth direction, interfacial energy and diameter of one-dimensional metal oxide nanowires.430 In this method, the morphology and properties of ZnO/SnO2 nanowires are generally controlled by growth parameters such as temperature, thickness of the catalyst layer, rate of carrier gas flow, and distance between the source and the substrate.431Fig. 48A indicates the presence of a crystalline wurtzite structure for ZnO and a tetragonal cassiterite structure for SnO2. In addition, some peaks from a ternary compound, i.e. the face-centered cubic spinel Zn2SnO4, are detected, indicating that part of the SnO2 and ZnO react to form Zn2SnO4. Different types of nanostructures are formed at different locations on the Au/alumina substrate. Fig. 48B illustrates a comb-like branched structure of ZnO/SnO2 nanowires. The diameter of the backbone nanowire is about 80 nm and the branched nanowires exhibit a highly uniform diameter of 30 nm. Furthermore, Fig. 48C shows the morphology of a ZnO/SnO2 hierarchical nanostructure. Importantly, the secondary branches from the backbone nanowires are significant. Hierarchical nanostructures consist of a trunk and branches, which are composed of hybrid ZnO/SnO2, but controlled synthesis of these nanostructures is still a challenge. It has been suggested that the backbone nanowire grew quickly with the Au catalyst at its tip, whereas smaller alloy droplets can be formed on the surface of the backbone nanowire, leading to hierarchical nanowires in different locations. The growth of hierarchical nanowires can be triggered when the critical temperature and vapor supersaturation ratio are achieved.
 Fig. 47  (a) Types of source vapor for the fabrication of ZnO nanowires; (b) schematic illustration of a typical carbothermal reduction process (taken with permission from ref. 426). 
 Fig. 48  ZnO/SnO2 mixed nanowires: (A) XRD patterns, FESEM micrographs of (B) comb-like and (C) hierarchical nanostructures (reproduced with permission from ref. 429). 
In recent years, a variety of MOFs have acted as semiconductors in metal oxide quantum dot (QD) configurations.432 Such MOFs exhibit photocatalytic activities and photoluminescence that result from their hybrid structures comprising periodically dispersed metal oxide clusters, which are separated by organic spacers. The properties of these hybrid structures have greatly motivated the development of facile syntheses of well-dispersed metal oxide QDs embedded in porous carbon matrices comprising MOFs (Fig. 49). Yang et al.433 developed a novel and facile approach to the synthesis of ZnO QDs (3.5 nm) without intrinsic defects or agglomeration and embedded in a hierarchically porous carbon matrix derived from isoreticular MOF-1 (IRMOF-1). The tailored microstructures enabled the attainment of exceptionally high Li storage performances. Therefore, the proposed method provides new effective routes to synthesize high-performance anode materials based on a metal oxide@porous carbon matrix. The selected-area electron diffraction pattern displayed a hexagonal wurtzite crystal structure of ZnO, indicating that the amorphous carbon lacked long-range order. Furthermore, the highly crystalline ZnO QDs were well dispersed, not agglomerated, and monodisperse, with a mean particle size of 3 ± 1 nm in the amorphous carbon matrix.
 Fig. 49  Schematic diagrams of IRMOF-1 before and after controlled pyrolysis to synthesize carbon-coated ZnO QDs without agglomeration (reproduced with permission from ref. 433). 


4.6.2. Metal zinc (Zn0). 
Cellulose is an important component of lignocellulosic biomass and is a structural component of plant cell walls. Its primary structure is a homopolysaccharide polymer that is composed of β-D-glucopyranose units that are linked together by (1–4) glycosidic bonds. Earlier, it has been proved that cellulose can be used as a carbon source. During pyrolysis of cellulose, tar, char and gases that contain H2, CO2, CO, light hydrocarbons and water vapor are generated.434 In addition, tar and char gasification can occur with the release of gases such as CO and H2. Maciel et al.435 reported that hydrogen generated in situ during thermal degradation of cellulose led to the reduction of ZnO to Zn, that solidified on a quartz tube in a low-temperature zone. Zn nanowires and Zn nanoflowers were obtained at heating rates of 3 °C min−1 and 10 °C min−1, respectively. These results confirmed that Zn morphologies are markedly dependent on the heating rate, which determines the release of Zn vapor and its partial pressure in the furnace. When graphite was used, no ZnO reduction occurred. This process is different from the carbothermal reduction route using carbon sources (e.g., graphite or CNTs), high temperatures, vacuum, catalysts and templates. A mixture of cellulose and ZnO was pyrolyzed combined with catalytic steam reforming, resulting in the generation of H2, which reduced ZnO to Zn. H2 was the main reducing agent to promote ZnO reduction, unlike CO in classical carbothermal reduction. Moreover, a process was presented for the conversion of black liquor into gases to reduce ZnO and promote the synthesis of Zn nanosheets.436 During pyrolysis and gasification of black liquor, gases evolve, especially CO, and promote ZnO reduction with the release of Zn via carbothermal reduction. The metal is condensed yielding Zn nanosheets, with partial surface reoxidation in the presence of carbon dioxide.

4.7. Tin (Sn) nanostructures
The carbothermal reduction method has been adapted to synthesize tin-based oxide compounds and alloys. Hassoun et al.437 synthesized a SnSb/C nanocomposite using triphenylantimony and triphenylbutyltin as metal precursors. Zhao et al.438 synthesized SnxSb alloy powders via carbothermal reduction from SnO2 and Sb2O3 in an argon atmosphere above 850 °C. Wang et al.439 also synthesized Cu6Sn5-encapsulated carbon microsphere anodes by means of a carbothermal reduction method. However, the alloy materials reported merely dispersed the metal in a carbon matrix or obtained materials on the micron scale, rather than creating a core–shell nanostructure. It is noteworthy for the process of carbothermal reduction that the Sn melting point is much lower than the SnO2 reduction temperature (650 °C), suggesting that once Sn is reduced from tin oxide it exists as a liquid, which makes the alloying process easier. On the other hand, owing to its low melting point (232 °C), liquid Sn is poured off in the outflow in high-temperature treatment. Therefore, preparation of core–shell-structured carbon-coated Sn-based alloy nanoparticles by carbothermal reduction with the aim of improving the cycling stability of the alloy is important but very challenging. Furthermore, Cui et al.440 proposed a modified carbothermal route for the synthesis of core–shell carbon-coated nanosized tin-based materials using a mixture of the oxides CuO and SnO2 as precursors. Upon thermal treatment, the mixed oxides were converted into Cu6Sn5 alloy by carbothermal reduction. Simultaneously, the remnant carbon was coated on the surface of the Cu6Sn5 particles to form a core–shell structure.
