Fig. 50 shows Gibbs free energy diagrams of the thermodynamic relationship between the free energy of the oxide reduction reaction and temperature. Theoretically, carbothermal reduction will take place at the temperature where ΔG equals zero.441,442 The reduction temperatures of CuO, SnO2, Co3O4, Bi2O3 and Sb2O5 can be found in the diagram. For example, 190 °C, 320 °C and 650 °C are the reduction temperatures for Sb2O5, Co3O4 and SnO2, respectively. However, in most situations, the practical reduction temperature is affected by both kinetics and carbon resources.
 Fig. 50  Relationship between the Gibbs free energy of the carbothermal reaction and temperature (reproduced with permission from ref. 440). 
Sn has a very large theoretical capacity (990 mA h g−1, corresponding to the formation of the lithium-rich alloy Li22Sn5 (ref. 443)). Sn-based materials could be considered a promising alternative to graphite-based electrode materials.444 However, their large volume expansion during lithiation and delithiation causes structural instability, resulting in poor cycling performances.445 This can be solved by dispersing small tin metal particles onto an electrochemically inactive matrix able to limit the volume expansion, maintaining the electrical conductivity of the material while cycling.446 Dispersion of tin on a BPO4 matrix can noticeably improve its reversible cycling behavior compared to that of pure tin.447,448 Further improvements can be obtained by optimizing the method of dispersing Sn in the matrix. Also, Mouyane et al.449 prepared a Sn–BPO4/xC composite by carbothermal synthesis, leading to highly dispersed metallic tin particles on a BPO4 buffer matrix. The carbothermal reaction can be expressed by eqn (36): SnO2 + (2 − x) C → Sn + (2 − 2x) CO + xCO2,(36)where x varies between 0 and 1, depending on the reaction conditions (i.e., reaction atmosphere). Unreacted carbon, corresponding to a molar fraction of x per mole of tin, is an important constituent of the composite, improving its electrical conductivity and cohesion. The optimal synthesis conditions were obtained under an argon flow. These conditions were therefore used for the synthesis in the presence of BPO4 to give the final Sn–BPO4/xC composite, allowing intimate mixing of the metal and the matrix.
In general, the introduction of nanoparticulate tin in terms of safety, ease of handling and processing, as well as relatively high tapped densities, provides increased volumetric energy densities for the final lithium-ion cells using tin anodes. In addition, tin–carbon composites have exhibited promising electrochemical performance when applied as sodium-ion anode materials, providing specific capacities in the range of 130 to 180 mA h g−1 and a substantially higher tapped density compared to hard carbons,450,451 which have so far been the anode material of choice for lab-scale sodium-ion batteries.452,453 Bresser et al.454 proposed a simple, cost-efficient, and environmentally friendly one-pot synthesis approach, using only tin(IV) acetate and sucrose as precursors, for embedding tin nanoparticles in micron-sized carbon particles, thus combining the great advantages of nanoparticulate tin with the high tapped density of micron-sized active particles. Such morphology can not only increase volumetric energy densities on the electrode level but also allows safer handling during sample processing and electrode preparation. In addition, electrodes prepared with this Sn–C nanocomposite can provide stable cycling performance and high rate capability for lithium- and sodium-ion anode applications. In this study, the utilization of an organic tin salt provides the great advantage that no washing steps are required in order to remove inorganic residues. In Fig. 51A, a comparison of the XRD patterns of the two samples prior to any thermal treatment (i.e., SnOx/C) and after 3 h at 450 °C under air (i.e., SnO2) is presented. SnOx/C displays very poor crystallinity, whereas the reflections for the latter are significantly sharper, indicating increased crystallinity of the phase-pure SnO2 with a particle size of 15–30 nm (Fig. 51C). Accordingly, subsequent carbothermal reduction resulted in air-stable metallic tin with a highly crystalline structure (Fig. 51B). Moreover, homogeneously distributed metallic nanoparticles which possess an average diameter of 50–200 nm are present within relatively large carbon particles (Fig. 51D).
 Fig. 51  (A) XRD patterns of SnOx/C composite and SnO2 nanopowder; (B) XRD pattern of Sn–C nanocomposite; SEM images of (C) SnO2 nanopowder and (D) Sn–C nanocomposite (reproduced with permission from ref. 454). 
Although metallic tin (Sn0) is one of the alternative materials to silicon that have been identified as taking the place of graphite anodes to achieve higher energy densities,455 this system undergoes large volume changes upon lithium alloying and de-alloying.456 This phenomenon causes pulverization of the active material and loss of contact with the current collector, eventually resulting in rapid capacity fade upon cycling. To address this challenge, some approaches involving the generation of porous nanostructured Sn, carbon coatings, Sn/graphite and Sn/graphene composites have been explored.457–472 Consequently, graphene (G), due to its superior in-plane electronic conducting properties, has attracted wide interest in applications for energy storage, photovoltaics and supercapacitors.473–477 Beck et al.478 synthesized tin nanoparticles embedded in graphene sheets by microwave reduction of SnCl2 and graphite oxide (GO) followed by annealing in Ar. The microwave reaction can result in the formation of SnO2, which was completely embedded in G sheets (Fig. 52A). Annealing in Ar at elevated temperatures initiated carbothermal reduction, culminating in the formation of a tin-decorated graphene sheet composite (Fig. 52B). In addition, a large-sized tin droplet may have formed due to solidification and there are many small particles in the size range of ≤20 nm. The size distribution of these particles is more uniform and the particles appear to adhere well onto the G sheets (Fig. 52C).
 Fig. 52  SEM images of (A) Sn prior to annealing, (B) Sn after annealing at 900 °C in Ar. Both show graphene layers; however, droplets and beads of tin are visible only after annealing. (C) HRTEM image of Sn after annealing at 900 °C in Ar (reproduced with permission from ref. 478). 
Significantly, uniform insertion of Sn metal between graphene layers can reduce aggregation of Sn, limit the overlap of graphene layers during the synthesis process and reduce the change in volume of Sn during charging/discharging cycling. Furthermore, Zhou et al.479 reported a facile synthesis route to Sn/G composites via a hydrothermal technique and subsequent calcination in argon (i.e., carbothermal reduction), as illustrated in Fig. 53A. Typically, a GO dispersion can be obtained from graphite using strong oxidizing agents and ultrasonication. Tin oxide (SnO2) and colloidal silica (SiO2) nanoparticles are sandwiched between GO layers by a hydrothermal process, during which the interval between GO sheets increases. SiO2 acts as a dispersant to separate the SnO2 nanoparticles, increases the distance between two neighboring GO layers and prevents the overlap of GO nanosheets. Then, colloidal silica is removed from the G/SnO2/SiO2 hybrid by 20% HF. Finally, G/SnO2 is heated under an Ar flow at 1000 °C for carbothermal reduction. The diffraction peak of GO around 2θ = 11.4° cannot be found, but a very broad peak at 2θ = 24° is observed for the G sample, indicating that most oxygen functional groups have been removed from the carbon framework (Fig. 53B). The peak at 2θ = 24° for graphene is rather broad and conspicuously distinct from that for graphite.480 The greatly broadened diffraction peaks for GO/SnO2 are indicative of the formation of SnO2 nanocrystals with very small size. After heat treatment, as shown for the G/Sn sample, all intense peaks are well indexed to Sn metal and the sharpness of the diffraction peaks indicates complete crystallinity of the tin phase. The crystallite size of G/Sn is estimated to be 5.9 nm according to the Debye–Scherrer formula. The absence of a SnO2 crystalline phase implies that nearly all the SnO2 nanoparticles have been reduced. No obvious peaks relative to graphene are observed in G/Sn samples, which might be attributed to an effect of disordered stacking of graphene sheets in the composites. A Raman spectrum of the G sample displays a strong G band at 1590 cm−1 and a weak D band at 1359.28 cm−1. The value of I(D)/I(G) for G is 0.81 (less than 1), indicating that the G sample still retained a graphite structure, except for a limited amount of disordered structure. When Sn was added and then GO was hydrothermally reduced, the D peak intensity became greater and the intensity ratio I(D)/I(G) for G/SnO2 exceeded 1 (1.01). This can be attributed to defects introduced into the G/SnO2 sample during preparation. After thermal treatment, the value of I(D)/I(G) for the G/Sn sample is 0.94, suggesting that the quality of the G/Sn sample is higher than that of the G/SnO2 sample.481,482 The preparation of G/Sn periodic structures is meaningful for fundamental study of Sn-based composites and for industrial applications.
 Fig. 53  (A) Schematic of the synthesis of G/Sn; (1) SnO2 and SiO2 nanoparticles were decorated on GO nanosheets via a hydrolysis process; (2) SiO2 was removed from G/SnO2/SiO2; (3) G/SnO2 was transformed into G/Sn nanosheets through carbothermal reduction. (B) XRD patterns of G (a); GO/SnO2 (b); G/Sn (c). (C) Raman spectra of G; GO/SnO2; G/Sn (reproduced with permission from ref. 479). 
As mentioned above, tin-based alloys could be synthesized via carbothermal reduction using tin oxides and carbon sources. Also, the hydrothermal technique improves the metal distribution in the carbon matrix. Hydrothermal treatment can also be used after carbothermal reduction. Wu et al.483 synthesized hierarchical worm-like SnO2@C aggregates via glucose-assisted hydrothermal treatment of Co–Sn alloy nanoparticles, which were initially formed by carbothermal reduction of mixed SnO2 and Co3O4 nanoparticles, as illustrated in Fig. 54A. The Co–Sn alloy nanoparticles are spheroidal particles with smooth surfaces and a diameter range of 200–500 nm (Fig. 54B). SnO2 nanoparticles have a diameter of 60 nm (Fig. 54C). Furthermore, hierarchical worm-like SnO2@C aggregates (Fig. 54D) were obtained, with the diameters of the aggregates similar to those of their precursor, Co–Sn alloy nanoparticles. The worm-like SnO2@C compound has a length of about 130 nm with a diameter about 40 nm.
 Fig. 54  (A) Schematic illustration of the synthetic strategy of hierarchical worm-like SnO2@C aggregates; SEM images of Co–Sn alloy nanoparticles (B), SnO2 nanoparticles (C) and hierarchical worm-like SnO2@C aggregates (D) (reproduced with permission from ref. 483). 
4.8. Manganese (Mn) nanostructures
Transition metal oxides have been considered as anode materials for lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), due to their high capacities.484 However, the large volume change of metal oxides during the Li insertion/extraction process causes mechanical degradation, resulting in rapid loss of capacity. Moreover, the low conductivity of metal oxides hastens the degradation process. To solve these problems, carbonaceous materials with high electrical conductivity and good stability could be employed as matrices for metal oxides.485–488 Among various metal oxides, manganese monoxide (MnO) is extremely attractive because of its relatively low electromotive force (1.032 V vs. Li/Li+), low overpotential, low cost, and environmental friendliness.489–492 MnO/carbon composite materials, such as coaxial MnO/carbon nanotubes,493 MnO/carbon core–shell nanorods,494 and MnO cubic particles/carbon composites,495 have been applied as anode materials for LIBs. Thus, the combination of MnO and carbon could improve electrochemical performance. Qiu et al.496 synthesized a MnO nanoparticle/graphene composite via in situ carbothermal reduction of Mn3O4 on the surface of graphene nanosheets. MnO nanoparticles with diameters ranging from 20 to 250 nm were integrated tightly with the graphene nanosheets. It has been proved that thermal reduction of graphene oxide can release CO and CO2 and the CO/CO2 ratios were dependent on the thermal conditions.497,498 Zhao et al.499 used the reducing agent CO released from GO for in situ carbothermal reduction of MnO2 in MnO2/GO composites to obtain MnO/rGO composites without using any external reducing gases, such as H2 and CO, which provided a synthesis process with less cost and more safety. As illustrated in Fig. 55, the preparation first included synthesis of the precursors, i.e. P-MNP, P-MGC1 and P-MGC2, via a controllable redox reaction between GO and KMnO4. After that, the final materials were obtained by calcination of the precursors under Ar at 500 °C for 90 min.
 Fig. 55  Schematic of the formation of manganese dioxide nanoparticles (MNP) and manganese monoxide/rGO composites (MGC1 and MGC2) (reproduced with permission from ref. 499). 
With respect to carbon-coated MnOx powders, Yang et al.500 synthesized a nano-MnO/C composite based on an alcoholysis step to convert manganese acetate into Mn3O4 followed by carbothermal reduction to reduce Mn3O4 to MnO, while a carbon layer was simultaneously coated on the surface of MnO nanospheres. As illustrated in Fig. 56, natural wood has a 3D porous structure with multiple layers. The xylem of China fir (CF) is composed of small fibres. CF xylem fibres were initially blended with KMnO4 in a neutral Na2SO4 solution to form a MnO2/CF precursor.501 The prepared precursor in DMF solution was calcined in N2 to obtain the MnO/C composite material.502 In the SEM image (Fig. 56B), it is clearly shown that a natural wood fibre without any chemical treatment possesses a curving multi-layered sheet structure. After being soaked with KMnO4 in the Na2SO4 solution, the CF fibre surface with an undamaged appearance (Fig. 56C) was inlaid with MnO2. After further carbonization (Fig. 56D), it is clearly shown that the carbonized CF perfectly retained the original fibres' morphology with multi-layered walls and a 3D twisted structure. It has been proved that this unique structure and the strong synergistic effect between the nanosized MnO particles and the porous carbon matrix result in high discharge capacity, high cycling stability and excellent rate performance. Therefore, this nanocomposite structure can provide a considerable range of structural variability, considering the extensive options of transition metal oxides and carbon-based materials, which can be extended to other promising high-performance anode materials for the future development of novel energy storage materials.
 Fig. 56  (A) Fabrication of MnO/C sample. SEM images of CF xylem fibres (B), MnO2/CF (C) and MnO/C (D), and HRTEM image of MnO/C (E). Well-crystallized MnO nanoparticles with diameters ranging from 3 to 7 nm are evenly dispersed on the carbon matrix (reproduced with permission from ref. 500). 
Nature has already provided us with elaborate one-, two-, and three-dimensional architectures with multiple sizes ranging from nanoscale to macroscale.503 Importantly, these biological resources are inexpensive, abundant, and renewable, so researchers have been inspired to develop biotemplating techniques. So far, many classical examples have been well established in oxides,504–510 carbides,511–517 metals,518–522 chalcogenides,523–525 and even ternary metal oxides526–528 and phosphates529–532 with various biotemplates. Furthermore, Xia et al.533 synthesized a MnO/C composite with hollow porous microstructures by means of a facile biotemplating technique and carbothermal reduction. Compared to other artificial/biological templates, a natural biological material from Nannochloropsis oculata (i.e., N. oculata) microalgae used in this study has three distinguishing features: (i) easy availability. N. oculata cells have a fast growth rate. They can double their biomass in less than 1 day. In addition, they are easy to cultivate and harvest; (ii) biological activity. The cell wall of N. oculata is mainly composed of polysaccharides. Therefore, N. oculata cells could adsorb and take up metal ions via electrostatic interactions, which are different from those with artificial templates;504,534 and (iii) carbon source. N. oculata cells are able to accumulate a large quantity of lipids inside them, and they contain lots of carbohydrates.535 As a result, they could act as a natural carbon source for fabricating a hollow porous carbon matrix. The main process of preparing hollow porous MnO/C microspheres is illustrated in Fig. 57. Firstly, N. oculata cells were concentrated and purged of unwanted materials before use. Then, an N. oculata cell solution was added to the KMnO4/Na2SO4 precursor solution. After the immersion process, N. oculata/MnO2 composites could be easily synthesized via biosorption and spontaneous redox deposition of metal ions on the N. oculata surface. To ensure that MnO nanoparticles could be strongly anchored onto composite microspheres, a polystyrene (PS) film was coated on the precursor. During thermal treatment, carbonaceous organic compounds arising from N. oculata and PS could decompose to carbon, as well as forming a reducing atmosphere to transform MnO2 into MnO via carbothermal reduction. Finally, the MnO nanocrystals can be successfully embedded in porous carbonaceous matrices to constitute hollow MnO/C microspheres.
 Fig. 57  Schematic illustration of the fabrication process of hollow porous MnO/C microspheres (reproduced with permission from ref. 533). 
4.9. Other metal nanostructures

4.9.1. Molybdenum (Mo). 
Molybdenum dioxide (MoO2) is an attractive host material for lithium storage because of its rich chemistry associated with multiple valence states, low electrical resistivity, high electrochemical activity toward lithium, and affordable cost.536–538 MoO2, with a disordered rutile structure, has metallic conductivity and high theoretical capacity (838 mA h g−1). However, bulk MoO2 often delivers a low charge–discharge capacity at room temperature because of sluggish kinetics.539 The incorporation of carbon in electrode materials can improve lithium ion intercalation or extraction kinetics.540–543 So far, various carbon/MoO2 composite materials have been prepared via reaction between Mo-based precursors and carbon in a reducing atmosphere.544–547 However, the amount of carbon in the composite is hard to control. Due to their high carbon content, the lithium ion intercalation/extraction behavior of carbon/MoO2 composite materials is usually similar to that of hard carbon materials. Moreover, no distinct charge/discharge plateaus of MoO2 are observed. The introduction of a hierarchical porous structure is an efficient way to improve the performance of electrode materials, as it can decrease the diffusion length of lithium ions and provide a sufficient contact surface between the electrode and electrolyte.548–551 Therefore, it is feasible to improve the lithium ion intercalation/extraction kinetics of MoO2 by the introduction of a hierarchical porous structure instead of the incorporation of carbon. Zhang et al.552 developed a one-step carbothermal reduction method for the preparation of hierarchical porous materials. In this study, the mesoporous carbon CMK-3 acts both as a template for casting the hierarchical porous structure and as a reducing agent for carbothermal reaction. Hierarchical porous MoO2 was fabricated using MoO3 as molybdenum source and mesoporous carbon (i.e., CMK-3) as template and reactant via a route illustrated in Fig. 58. After grinding for 20 min, a mixture of MoO3 and CMK-3 was heated at 820 °C, a temperature above the melting point of MoO3 (i.e., 795 °C). Molten MoO3 was dispersed onto the surface of CMK-3 followed by gradual reduction by carbon to form MoO2 nanoparticles with a higher melting point. The consumption of CMK-3 led to nanosized tubules composed of as-formed MoO2 nanoparticles. These tubules, in combination with the voids among the MoO2 nanoparticles, feature a hierarchical structure with a three-dimensional (3D) porous channel system.
 Fig. 58  Schematic illustration of the preparation of MoO2 nanotubes (reproduced with permission from ref. 552). 
More recently, supported tungsten and molybdenum carbides have exhibited high activities for deoxygenation of triglycerides and fatty acids to linear alkanes, while their product selectivity could be further tuned to alkenes and branched alkanes, if desired.553–557 Although these transition metal carbides appear to be versatile deoxygenation catalysts, there are still unresolved issues regarding the activity and stability of such catalysts. Supported molybdenum carbide catalysts could produce alkanes from fatty acids in high yields, but the deoxygenation reaction needed either high reaction temperatures and long reaction times,554 or the use of a supercritical solvent as reaction medium.555,556 Tungsten carbides exhibit high selectivity for the production of alkenes from fatty acid.553,557 However, their catalytic activity was slightly lower than that of supported molybdenum carbides, whereas tungsten carbides additionally suffered from deactivation due to oxidation of the active carbide phase.554 In Stellwagen et al.'s work,558 CNF-supported tungsten and molybdenum carbides of different crystallite sizes could be synthesized to improve their performance in deoxygenation reactions for hydrodeoxygenation of stearic acid to hydrocarbons. To vary the size of carbide particles while maintaining equal metal loadings, the distribution of the metal precursor over the CNF support after impregnation was altered by changing the surface properties of the CNF. Surface oxygen groups were inserted onto the CNF by treatment with nitric acid (i.e., CNF-ox), while non-oxidizing treatment with hydrochloric acid resulted in a largely oxygen-free surface (i.e., CNF-hcl).559 The surface oxygen groups of the CNF-ox material are known to interact with the polymolybdate or polytungstate complexes that are present in the impregnation solutions. Consequently, impregnation of CNF-ox leads to higher metal dispersion than impregnation of the CNF-hcl support.560,561 Conversion of tungsten and molybdenum ammonium salts to carbides can be achieved by a carbothermal reduction process.562,563
With respect to molybdenum carbide (Mo2C), this also exhibits interesting activity in a number of catalytic reactions, including isomerization,564 hydrogenation and hydrogenolysis,565 methanol reforming,566 and hydrotreating reactions.567 It can be formed under relatively mild conditions, making it an ideal carbide material in catalysis. Besides, Mo participates in extensive reactions with oxygen, providing various potential routes to mono- and polymetallic carbides via multi-metal oxide precursors. Carburization of MoO3 in a mixed feed of hydrogen and hydrocarbon is affected by the surface area of the oxide and the chain length of the alkane. Longer-chain hydrocarbons are able to reduce the oxide at lower temperatures than pure H2. Each successive increase in chain length decreases the temperature required to decompose the hydrocarbon and incorporate carbon into the oxide matrix.568–571 In addition, MoOx can be carburized using carbon alone as the reducing–carburizing agent.572–575 Depending on the reaction conditions, reduction–carburization can be directed to form either fcc α-Mo2C or hcp β-Mo2C. Oyama et al.576 concluded that the formation of the metal carbide occurs in two consecutive steps, due to similarities in the reaction profiles and effluent gas analysis in temperature-programmed reduction of MoO3 in hydrogen alone and temperature-programmed reduction–carburization (TPRC) of MoO3 to β-Mo2C under H2/CH4. The first step comprises reduction of MoO3 to MoO2, which is carried out by the action of H2 alone. The second step is reduction–carburization of MoO2 to Mo2C, which is performed by H2 and CH4 acting in concert. The synthesis of binary MoNb carbides occurs similarly in two stages.577 Cotter et al.578 synthesized a series of bimetallic carbides of the form β-(Mo1−xVx)2C (0 < x < 0.12) by the carbothermal reduction of corresponding h-Mo1−xVxO3 precursors. The oxides were synthesized by precipitation and the subsequent development of a carbide phase was monitored. As illustrated in Fig. 59, the reduction proceeded via a complex mechanism involving the initial formation of defective MoO2. Increasing the V content retards the onset of reduction and strongly impacts the kinetics of carburization. The carbides exhibit a trend in growth morphology with V concentration, from a particulate-agglomerate material to packed nanofibrous morphology. The high-aspect-ratio crystallites exhibit pseudomorphism and, in the case of V-containing materials, some preferential crystal orientation of grains is observed. Moreover, an increase in mesoporosity is associated with fibrous morphology, as well as an exceptionally high surface area (i.e., 80–110 m2 g−1). By adapting the heating rate, gas flow, and pretreatment conditions, it was possible to produce carbide materials with comparable physical properties to those obtained on a small scale. As a result, it was possible to synthesize Mo2C materials in multi-gram quantities (5–15 g) with BET surface areas ranging from 50 to 100 m2 g−1.
 Fig. 59  Suggested cascade of reaction steps during carburization of MoO3 (reproduced with permission from ref. 578). 


4.9.2. Antimony (Sb). 
There are various methods for the synthesis of Sb-based alloy materials, including mechanical alloying of metal powders,579,580 hydrothermal methods,581 chemical precipitation,582 and electrochemical deposition,583,584 as well as carbothermal reduction of metal oxides.585–588 Compared to other preparation methods, the carbothermal reduction method is low-cost and much more easily adapted to mass-production. In carbothermal reduction processes, the amount of carbon is a critical parameter, as it directly determines the product purity. If carbon is in excess, there will be a certain amount of carbon remaining; on the other hand, less carbon than required will lead to the incomplete reduction of metal oxides. In this case, many studies have been carried out based on the hypothesis that all carbon is oxidized to CO during the carbothermal process.586,589–593 However, the generation of CO2 cannot be excluded in some particular systems. CO or CO2 is generated mainly depending on the chemical features of different metal oxides. As a matter of fact, in a carbothermal reduction process involving multiple metal oxides, the gas products might be a mixture of CO and CO2 due to various factors. It is of great importance to obtain a further understanding of details of the carbothermal reduction process such as the reduction order and the CO and CO2 composition in the gas product, which can contribute to determining the precise amount of carbon actually required, leading to high-purity products. Furthermore, Yang et al.366 synthesized a high-purity CoSb alloy and clarified the kinetics of the reaction by carbothermal reduction of the Co3O4–Sb2O3–C system. It is noted that Co3O4 was preferentially reduced to CoO at a relatively low temperature, followed by the simultaneous carbothermal reduction of Sb2O3 and CoO. The former is reduced by carbon via the generation of CO2, while the latter is reduced via the formation of CO. The overall reaction for the synthesis of CoSb alloy could be expressed as: 3Sb2O3 + 2Co3O4 + 23/2C → 6CoSb + 6CO + 11/2CO2.
More recently, Wu et al.594 successfully synthesized Sb–C nanofibers via a single-nozzle electrospinning technique and subsequent calcination (as illustrated in Fig. 60A). The morphology of the as-electrospun fibers after carbothermal treatment displayed individual uniform nanofibers with a diameter around 200 nm (Fig. 60B). Moreover, Sb nanoparticles with 15–20 nm diameters are uniformly embedded in the carbon nanofibers (Fig. 60C), appearing as a number of “Sb islands” surrounded by a carbon “ocean”. The high-resolution TEM image in Fig. 60D clearly shows some nanocrystals with diameters around 20 nm. Sb–C nanofibers can be considered a promising anode candidate to construct a viable and low-cost Na-ion battery for energy storage. Furthermore, these types of metal–carbon nanofibers could be widely applied for developing high-performance electrode materials.
 Fig. 60  (A) Schematic illustration of the preparation process and (B–D) SEM & TEM images of the Sb–C nanofibers (reproduced with permission from ref. 594). 
Nanoporous carbon (NC) as a support is highly relevant to these conversions because of its wide commercial availability595 and excellent thermochemical stability.596 Traditional catalysts are synthesized by supporting metal nanoparticles on as-prepared NC via a variety of approaches including electrochemical deposition,597 incipient wetness impregnation,598 sonochemical-assisted deposition,599 precipitation–deposition,600 and chemical vapor deposition.601 With the aim of asserting greater control over synthesis of the material and providing the ability to tailor the physical and catalytic properties of the material, metal–organic frameworks (MOF) were employed as carbon sources/templates where supported nanoparticle catalysts were formed.602–604 It has been indicated that Zn-based MOFs, such as IRMOF-1 (ref. 605) and MOF-2,606 can be converted into highly purified NC materials via carbonization above the boiling point of Zn metal (907 °C) with the formation of mesopores. In addition to acting as a carbon source for NC formation, the pore characteristics as well as macroscopic morphologies of these NC materials can be easily tuned based on the initial structure of the MOF, thus illustrating the role of MOFs as both template and carbon source for the resulting NC material.606,607 It is envisioned that, in addition to serving as a carbon source, MOFs can act as a reactive source to site-isolate transition metal precursors through post-synthetic modification (PSM) prior to thermal treatment. Therefore, MOFs could provide a carbon source for the resulting material support, as well as reducing site-isolated transition metal precursors, potentially leading to the formation of various types of reduced metal phases (elemental metal phases, oxycarbide phases, and carbide phases). Initial results proved the successful preparation of highly dispersed 4 nm titanium nanoparticles on NC with a high specific surface area via carbonization and carbothermal reduction (CCR) of a post-synthetically modified MOF.608 For comparison, using conventional impregnation followed by pyrolysis to synthesize carbon-supported metal nanoparticles from MOFs609–611 can result in significantly reduced surface areas (150 m2 g−1) and extensive metal impurities derived from the metal centers of the MOFs.610,611 However, using IRMOF-3, metal precursors (e.g., Ti(O–iPr)4) can be coordinated with the amine functionalities of IRMOF-3 to form Ti/IRMOF-3 (Fig. 61i).608 When subsequently subjected to CCR conditions, Ti/IRMOF-3 is carbonized to form Ti/NC3, resulting in reduction of the titanium precursor and the formation of dispersed titanium nanoparticles on NC (Fig. 61ii).608 The initial coordination between the MOF amine functionality and the metal precursor served to minimize aggregation of the metal precursor during carbothermal reduction, leading to the isolation of active sites in metal nanoparticles.608 Moreover, metal nanoparticles could be embedded in situ into the NC by consumption of the carbon source resulting from the carbothermal reduction of the metal precursors. This carbothermal reduction can achieve the formation of concave holes in NC where reduced metal nanoparticles could be rigidly immobilized, thereby minimizing aggregation of active metal nanoparticles in catalytic reactions.612 Accordingly, this method could be widely applied to many transition metal precursors to generate catalysts for biomass conversion with enhanced controllable properties: (1) high surface areas, (2) enhanced mesoporosity, and (3) dispersed accessible metal nanoparticles. In addition, this method can tune the resulting phase of the metal, depending on the carbonization temperature during synthesis. For instance, in a previous report on Ti/NC materials, a transition from Ti4+ species to reduced oxidation states of Ti (Ti2+ and Ti3+) was observed during CCR. This demonstrated that this method can be used to control not only the physical and catalytic properties of the materials but also the resulting metal phase in the generation of reduced metals, oxides, carbides, and oxycarbides supported on NC, based on the synthesis conditions and initial MOF structure.
 Fig. 61  Schematic of preparation of M/NC3 and M/NC1 materials (M 1/4 Ru, W, V, and Ti) via the carbonization and carbothermal reduction (CCR) of corresponding M/IRMOF-3 and M/IRMOF-1 materials (taken with permission from ref. 621). 
Using the CCR method as illustrated in Fig. 61, post-synthetically modified IRMOF-3 was used to generate a class of carbon-based catalysts supporting highly dispersed and active transition metal nanoparticles (Fig. 61i and ii). Ruthenium (Ru), tungsten (W), vanadium (V), and titanium (Ti) were studied because all these metals are relevant to biomass conversion via reduction and oxidation (redox) reactions.613–616 The thermal and chemical transitions of these precursors to reduced metals (Ru) and carbide phases (W, V, and Ti)616–618 have also been reported. As controls, IRMOF-1 materials (M/IRMOF-1) without amine coordination sites were simply modified via impregnation (IMP) of metal precursors prior to CCR (Fig. 61iii and iv). Furthermore, the catalytic performance of M/NC3 and M/NC1 materials was studied in various reactions involving biomass model compounds: hydrogenation of levulinic acid (Ru as active metal),613 esterification of levulinic acid (W as active metal),619 and oxidation of benzyl alcohol (V and Ti as active metals).620 Consequently, M/NC3 materials prepared by the CCR method have the potential to be highly active catalysts for the efficient conversion of lignocellulosic biomass.621
Among the different carbon materials, a novel type of mesoporous biocarbon material has displayed unique structures and surface chemistry properties and is especially attractive as a cathode material for lithium-ion batteries from economic and environmentally friendly points of view.622–624 Many functional materials including amorphous and crystal structures are synthesized via biomineralization in biological systems.625–627 Compared with other synthetic routes, the advantage of biomineralization processes is that they not only yield advanced materials in an environmentally benign system, but also enable control of the structure, size, morphology and crystallographic orientation of inorganic crystals. Beer waste brewing yeasts (BWBYs) are a main by-product in the beer industry and most of them are discarded, leaving a small part to add to the next batch of beer fermentation. BWBY cells generally possess spherical or elliptical morphology, a large surface area and abundant surface charge within a broad range of pH and ionic strength.628 Significantly, they accumulate metal ions from aqueous solutions by means of different physicochemical interactions, adsorption and absorption, or a metabolism-dependent mechanism. The most notable feature of BWBY cells is that they have hierarchical structures on different length scales, but use self-assembly to control the mineralization that forms hierarchical nanomaterials.629 He et al.630 synthesized a mesoporous biocarbon-coated Li3V2(PO4)3 (MBC-LVP) cathode material by a biotemplate-assisted sol–gel reaction process using low-cost BWBYs as both structural template and biocarbon source. Moreover, hydrophilic anionic groups in biomacromolecules on a cell wall surface could improve the mineralization ability of yeast cells and regulate crystal nucleation and growth.631Fig. 62a outlines the adsorption, desorption and self-assembly mechanism of Li3V2(PO4)3 nanoparticles in a yeast cell. Afterwards, the proteins and polysaccharides in the yeast cell have hydrophilic anionic groups and negative charges.632 When V3+, Li+ and PO43− are added to a purified BWBY solution, the cations combine with the hydrophilic anionic groups on the biomolecules in the yeast cells by electrostatic interaction. Like most natural cells, yeast cells can induce spontaneous mineralization on their surface, due to their relatively low charge density.633 The sol–gel process with Li3V2(PO4)3 occurs on the yeast cell wall; inside the cell, the yeast cells regulate and control the mineralization of Li3V2(PO4)3 nanoparticles on the molecular level. Therefore, MBC-LVP materials could be synthesized by means of in situ composite carbonization and crystallization via carbothermal reduction (Fig. 62b). MBC-LVP possesses a biocarbon network structure for achieving good electrochemical performances (as illustrated in Fig. 62b–d). In this structure, the Li3V2(PO4)3 nanoparticles can effectively reduce the length of Li+ solid-state transport paths to provide a high diffusion rate. The active mesoporous biocarbon network possesses good electronic conductivity by in situ preparation of a carbon coating during heat treatment of the precursor. This convenient and environmentally friendly synthetic route can be easily scaled up for the industrial production of electroactive materials.
 Fig. 62  (a & b) Schematic illustration of mesoporous biocarbon-coated Li3V2(PO4)3 (MBC-LVP) synthesized via a biotemplate-assisted sol–gel process and a carbothermal reduction method using BWBY cells as both structural template and carbon source. (c) Representative crystal structure of monoclinic Li3V2(PO4)3; the VO6 octahedra are shown in green and PO4 tetrahedra in tan. (d) The distorted crystal structure of Li3V2(PO4)3 nuclei completely coated by biocarbon, lithium and electron pathways; biocarbon-coated atoms are shown in yellow-green and electrons in orange (taken with permission from ref. 630). 

5. Application of sustainable carbon–metal nanostructures
In current society, energy and the environment have become the two most prevalent topics in the fields of science and technology. To address and meet the challenges presented by the global energy demand of an ever-increasing population and the associated environmental consequences of a society still generally based on fossil fuels, very innovative, efficient and increasingly sustainable solutions must be developed.634 In this regard, there are several options by which to generate and store renewable energy from sun, wind, water, geothermal or biomass – all of which are heavily reliant on materials technologies to allow their efficient implementation. Consequently, the development of sustainable materials is extremely important.635 This is not a trivial task as these new materials should ideally be low-cost, scalable, industrially and economically attractive, and based on abundant renewable resources, whilst of course achieving application performances in renewable energy conversion or environmental applications that exceed those of existing technologies. Thus, it is the duty of researchers to develop novel high-performance sustainable materials to solve the challenge of providing renewable energy for all whilst limiting environmental impacts. In the context of sustainable materials development, after oxygen, carbon is the most abundant element existing in the biosphere. Moreover, nature utilizes these elements coupled with hydrogen to provide the basis for renewable energy storage (e.g., carbohydrates). Analogously, carbon-based systems perform a major role in emerging renewable energy conversion technologies in terms of electrocatalysis, photocatalysis, electrodes in energy storage devices, heterogeneous catalysis, biofuels, etc.636
Carbon-based materials have also been extensively used in water purification, gas separation (e.g., CO2 capture), and as a soil additive. Furthermore, the importance and potential of carbon-based materials has been recognized in recent decades by some of the highest scientific awards including the 1996 Nobel Prize in Chemistry (fullerenes), the 2008 Kavli Prize in Nanoscience (carbon nanotubes), and the 2010 Nobel Prize in Physics (graphene). As a consequence, interest in carbon-based materials and their applications is undergoing its most rapid development and represents a very significant topic in modern materials science. With the creation of fossil fuel-based industry since the start of the 20th century, the majority of carbon-based materials are conventionally synthesized using fossil fuel-derived precursors and often require harsh or energy-intensive synthetic conditions. It is vital to emphasize the great importance of optimizing the surface area and porosity of materials for catalytic, separation and energy applications.637
The nanocasting approach is one prevalent route for synthesizing high-surface-area porous carbon materials, but it has its limitations (e.g., multi-step synthesis involving a sacrificial inorganic template).638–640 The synthesis of such ‘‘hard-templated’’ mesoporous carbons can potentially use sustainable precursors (e.g., sucrose in CMK-1 synthesis), but generally carbon-based materials and the aforementioned carbon allotropes (e.g., nanotubes) are not derived from sustainable resources and require complex synthetic approaches that are difficult to scale and usually lead to condensed hydrophobic carbon structures.638–642 Most of these structures are not appropriate for future chemical/energy provision schemes (e.g., aqueous phase-based biorefineries).643–645 So far, renewable resource-derived carbons have only comprised activated carbons (ACs).646–649 This class of predominantly microporous materials is commercially available and has proven applications in water treatment,650,651 CO2 capture,652 energy storage (e.g., supercapacitors653), and, perhaps most typically, heterogeneous catalysis.654,655 However, a top-down approach to the synthesis of ACs derived from natural precursors does not provide the necessary control over material features including porosity, morphology and surface chemistry, whilst limited batch-to-batch reproducibility and final carbonization yield, as well as the use of harsh reagents during synthesis (i.e., strong acids or bases) to generate porosity, are other drawbacks of this process. Conversely, the development of more flexible routes to carbonaceous materials derived from renewable resources has become a significant topic. It is perhaps appropriate to note that researchers could draw inspiration from a variety of natural processes (e.g., coalification, photosynthesis) and structures (e.g., crustacean shells, plant tissues, etc.) to produce the various complex nanostructured materials required to address the energy and chemical challenges of a sustainable society. More importantly, new structures may also be derived from abundant renewable carbon-based precursors that do not compete with food supplies. Examples include lignocellulosic biomass or other bioproducts derived from food/agricultural wastes (i.e., carbohydrates, cellulose, hemicellulose, lignin, chitin, inorganics, proteins, etc.). As biomaterials can act as both template and carbon source, this section will only focus on the production of metal nanostructures during the pyrolysis process, which provides both a high temperature and an inert atmosphere for carbothermal reduction. The simplest approach is one-step pyrolysis of native biomaterials under closed conditions or in an inert atmosphere.656 Alternatively, a two-step heating process could be used to initially strengthen a biopolymer network by crosslinking; for instance, the synthesis of microporous N-doped carbon materials from chicken feather fibers.657 Another two-step strategy is to activate a carbon material after pyrolysis, either chemically or by heating in air to increase its porosity.658,659 Metals can also be used to crosslink and stabilize polymer precursors,660 to increase porosity, or as catalysts (e.g., Fe and Ni) for graphitization of carbon materials at relatively low temperatures.661 Metal precursors can be introduced by infiltration, but many native biomaterials also contain metal species (e.g., potassium in seaweeds) that have been exploited for in situ chemical activation.662 In this area, metal nanostructures synthesized by carbothermal pyrolysis can be widely applied and discussed in the fields of energy and the environment.
5.1. Energy applications
Seaweeds are outstanding precursors of nanotextured carbons, which are promising candidates for carbon-based electrode materials for energy storage applications. Alginate, a high-modulus natural polysaccharide extracted from brown algae, is mainly composed of β-D-mannuronate (M) and α-L-guluronate (G) monomers.663 The negatively charged framework (–COO−) in G-rich blocks of alginate polymers makes it simple to form cobalt alginates (Co-alginates) by absorbing Co2+via an ion-exchange process, and their structures have been described by the egg-box model.664,665 After carbonization of Co-alginates in an inert gas atmosphere, Co3O4 nanoparticles can be encapsulated in the carbonized alginate in the form of Co3O4/C nanocomposites. Furthermore, pyrolysis of abundant sustainable natural alginate resources provides a new opportunity to synthesize Co3O4/C nanocomposites on a large scale via an environmentally friendly pathway. As shown in Fig. 63A, carbonaceous microfibres with a diameter of 5–8 μm embedded with Co3O4 nanoparticles (30–50 nm) were synthesized by pyrolysis of wet-spun Co–alginate fibres (Co–AF). When evaluated as an anode material for LIBs, the synthesized Co3O4–CF exhibits high specific capacity and excellent cycling stability and rate performance. In particular, a Co3O4–CF electrode composite displays a reversible capacity as high as 780 mA h g−1 after 100 cycles at 89 mA g−1 (Fig. 63D). Its charge capacities are 500 and 220 mA h g−1 after 10 cycles at high current rates of 890 and 4450 mA g−1, respectively. The high performance of the Co3O4–CF electrode resulted from its unique hierarchical nano/microstructure, where the synergistic effect of Co3O4 nanoparticles and the micron-sized amorphous carbon matrix greatly improves the reversible capacity, cycling stability and rate performance.666
 Fig. 63  (A) Synthesis process of Co3O4–CF by the pyrolysis of wet-spun cobalt alginate fibres; (B) SEM image of Co3O4–CF; (C) TEM image of Co3O4–CF; and (D) comparison of the cycling performances of Co3O4–CF and CF (reproduced with permission from ref. 666). 
With regard to battery materials in energy applications, e.g., LIBs, advanced materials for cathodes, anodes, and electrolytes have been developed to achieve higher energy and power densities, longer life, and lower cost. For cathode materials, many studies focus on layered LiMO2, olivine-type LiMPO4, and spinel-type LiM2O4, where M is one or more transition metal elements. Moreover, Si, Sn, alloys, and MnXm-type compounds (where M = Fe, Co, Ni, Cu, Sn, etc., and X = O, S, N, etc.) are also used as anode materials. Although all these active materials demonstrate considerable capability and promising prospects, many obstacles restrict their commercialization, including poor electronic conductivity, low Li+ transfer efficiency, volume expansion/contraction during repeated cycling processes, and loss of active materials due to corrosion by electrolytes and/or collapse of the stable structure. To overcome these obstacles, various feasible strategies have been proposed, such as optimizing the crystalline lattice of active materials by doping cations or anions, reducing particles to a suitable scale to provide enhanced electron/Li+ conductivity and reactivity, and combining active materials with other active/inactive materials for complementary strengthening, especially core–shell structures.667 Su et al.668–671 have studied many methods for fabricating LIBs materials with core–shell structures, such as mesoporous Fe3O4@C microcapsules, Fe@Fe3C/C nanocomposites, hierarchical Fe@C microspheres, and MnO@C nanorods. Among these, replacing mature carbon materials with a solution of polysaccharides and subsequent carbonization at high temperatures can improve the quality of coatings. Also, sol–gel chemistry can provide effective coating layers and uniformly distributed particles at the same time. Hydrothermal or solvothermal treatments involve particular conditions of high temperature and pressure and are extensively used to prepare regular particles on the nanometer to micrometer scale. In addition, this route displays advantages for realizing advanced core–shell structures, given that polysaccharides can decompose into carbonaceous materials in a high-temperature solution and simultaneously surround the active cores.667
The biotemplating technique has been an impressive strategy for achieving materials with controllable morphology and structural specialization, complexity, and relevant fascinating functions derived from biomaterials.672 Many state-of-the-art microstructures have been well established by a wide range of biotemplates with multi-scale structures and novel morphologies, enhancing the performance of the prospective materials. It has also been proved that rational utilization of a biotemplating strategy can realize advanced functional materials, including phosphates, oxides, and carbides, via various biotemplates such as bamboo,673–675 pollen grains,676 cotton fibers,677 and microalgae,678,679etc. Nevertheless, conventional biotemplating methods focus only on replication of the morphology and using the biogenic elements of biomaterials (e.g., C, Si, N, Fe, P, S), and the biofunctionality of biological systems was neglected. For example, microalgae, consisting of a large amount of proteins, polysaccharides (alginate, etc.), and lipids, possess various functional groups that can absorb and take up heavy metal ions. Recently, Tao et al.680 took advantage of the unique biosorption behavior of microalgae and synthesized a Sn@C composite with tin nanoparticles embedded in a porous carbon matrix via a facile biosorption and subsequent calcination procedure. Spirulina platensis was employed as a biotemplate, biosorbent, and carbon source. Initially, SnCl4·5H2O was dissolved in absolute ethanol to form a hybrid precursor. Meanwhile, S. platensis was washed several times by formaldehyde and deionized water to remove unwanted materials. Then, S. platensis was added to the precursor. After 1 h at room temperature, the mixture was filtered, dried at 80 °C, and then immersed in 10 wt% polystyrene (PS) dissolved in dimethylformamide. Finally, the sample was calcined at 600 °C for 3 h under a N2 atmosphere to obtain black materials (i.e., sample A3), as illustrated in Fig. 64a. For comparison, pristine S. platensis (A1) and S. platensis with adsorbed tin ions but without a PS coating film (A2) were calcined in the same conditions to obtain control samples. In order to ensure that tin particles could be strongly anchored to the carbon matrix, a PS film was coated onto the surface of S. platensis after biosorption. During subsequent heat treatment, the carbonaceous compounds in S. platensis and PS decomposed to carbon, as well as forming a series of gaseous products (such as CO, H2, CH4, and C2H4)681 to act as a reducing atmosphere, which can transform SnO2 into tin. Combined with carbothermal reduction, tin nanocrystals were dispersed in situ into carbonaceous matrices and constituted the Sn@C composite. A Sn@C electrode exhibits remarkable cycling stability and excellent cycling performance, which demonstrates high potential as an anode material for LIBs (Fig. 64b–c). Furthermore, this green sustainable strategy can not only extend the scope of biotemplating synthesis of advanced functional materials, but also provide an important reference for environmental protection and water purification.
 Fig. 64  (a) Schematic illustration of the synthesis of the Sn@C composite; (b) charge/discharge profiles of sample A3 at different cycles with a current density of 50 mA g−1; (c) cycling performance and coulombic efficiency of samples A1–A3 at a 50 mA g−1 current density; (d) rate performance of samples A2 and A3 (reproduced with permission from ref. 680). 
Fast pyrolysis of lignocellulosic biomass under anoxic conditions at an ultra-high heat-up rate has been heavily studied and commercially used in recent years.682,683 In a pyrolysis process, not only renewable liquid fuel, but also tons of biochar have been produced, which have not found value-added utilization. Besides, various carbonaceous materials, such as nanotubes and graphene, have been mass-produced and found applications in separation, energy storage, drug delivery, and bioimaging.684,685 For instance, carbonaceous materials are the most widely used electrical double-layer capacitors (EDLC).686–688 The more favorable cycle durability and electrical conductivity of different carbonaceous materials have led to their application in almost 100% of commercial supercapacitors since the 1960s.689 Conventional synthetic approaches to carbon nanomaterials, such as chemical vapor deposition (CVD) and arc discharge synthesis, etc., usually require tedious and expensive synthetic routes as well as organic solvents and electrochemical treatment, which limit large-scale production and commercialization.690–692 Although some materials researchers have developed facile methods to prepare carbon nanomaterials by pyrolyzing silk and feathers,684,693 using complex lignocellulosic biomass, which contains lignin, cellulose, hemicellulose, and ash, to prepare carbon nanomaterials has not been carried out to date. Recently, functionalized carbonaceous materials could be formed via pyrolysis of lignocellulosic biomass by adjusting the pyrolysis temperature.329,694 It is envisioned that we can develop a conceptually new method for in situ growth of carbon fibers by catalytically reducing and depositing certain volatile low-molecular-weight organic compounds on carbonaceous skeletons. Moreover, the morphology and properties of as-synthesized carbon composites could be tunable via deliberate adjustment of temperature and selection of catalysts. Furthermore, Liu et al.695 synthesized magnetic nanofiber/mesoporous carbon composites via a one-pot method by catalytic pyrolysis of lignocellulosic biomass with FeCl3 and evaluated their energy storage performance. As illustrated in Fig. 65, the precursor was biomass preloaded with Fe(III), which was prepared by a biosorption process using sawdust as adsorbent at ambient temperature. Subsequently, fast pyrolysis was conducted under N2 flow to obtain magnetic carbon nanofibers. In addition, the coupled catalytic action of both Fe and Cl species can effectively catalyze the growth of carbon nanofibers on mesoporous carbon and form magnetic nanofibers/mesoporous carbon composites, which can be directly used as electrode materials for electrochemical energy storage without further separation, and exhibit favorable energy storage performance with high EDLC capacitance, good retention capability, and excellent stability and durability (>98% capacitance retention after 10000 cycles). Considering that biomass is a naturally abundant and renewable resource, magnetic carbon nanofibers can be easily fabricated via one-step pyrolysis on a large scale and become a sustainable and reliable resource for clean energy storage.
 Fig. 65  Schematic illustration of the synthesis of magnetic carbon nanofibers from lignocellulosic biomass for energy storage applications (reproduced with permission from ref. 695). 
With the depletion of fossil fuels, development of renewable energy has attracted an increasing amount of interest. Acidic materials have been widely used in catalytic production of a variety of renewable fuels (e.g., biodiesels) and fine chemicals (e.g., furfurals and sugars).696–698 Previously, liquid mineral acids (e.g., HCl, H2SO4, and H3PO4) were widely used to catalyze these reactions.696,699 However, their many disadvantages, such as high corrosiveness, costly and inefficient separation from homogeneous reaction mixtures, and creation of a large amount of waste by-products, have limited the applications of liquid mineral acids.700 Alternatively, solid acids, including solid Brønsted acids (e.g., SO3H-functionalized carbon and polymers)701–704 in addition to solid Lewis acids (e.g., transition metal (W, Zr, Nb) oxides),705,706 are easily separated from reaction mixtures and can be reused. Therefore, solid acids have been used to replace liquid mineral acids in catalytic reactions such as esterification, dehydration, and hydrolysis during biofuel production to minimize adverse effects on the environment and to reduce expenses.700,707–711 Porous carbonaceous solid acids are widely used catalysts in acid-catalyzed reactions. Nakajima et al.701 reported that these acids could be synthesized by sulfonation of partially carbonized organic matter including sugars, cellulosic materials, and synthetic polymers and can be selectively used in many acid-catalyzed reactions such as hydrolysis of β-1,4-glucan and biodiesel production. Arancon et al.712 synthesized a porous carbonaceous solid acid derived from corncob residues with good catalytic performance in esterification or transesterification of waste oils. Wu et al.713 prepared sulfonated biochar as a solid acid for hydrolysis of crystalline cellulose.
Compared with conventional solid acids, magnetic solid acids can be separated more easily in the presence of external magnetic fields, particularly in viscous or solid reaction mixtures.714,715 The most common method applied for the synthesis of magnetic solid acid catalysts is sulfonation of a mesoporous structure with a magnetic core, generally Fe3O4. It has been confirmed that a magnetic solid acid with a high acid strength of 2.2–2.5H+ mol−1 was prepared via sulfonation of polystyrene-encapsulated Fe3O4 spheres.716 More recently, a series of magnetic nanosized solid acid catalysts were synthesized by sulfonation of a series of particles containing Fe3O4 cores with various shell materials (e.g., polystyrene, poly(glycidyl methacrylate), and silica).717 All these catalysts exhibit high activity and excellent separability in acid-catalyzed reactions. However, it has been reported that many magnetic solid acid catalysts have been deemed unsatisfactory for acid-catalyzed reactions. For example, SO3H-functionalized polymers often exhibit low thermal stability and are costly.718 In addition, preparation of these magnetic solid acid catalysts involves complex synthetic procedures and usually requires expensive reagents, which impedes large-scale production and commercialization. In the continuing effort toward biomass waste disposal and resource recovery,719–722 Liu et al.723 developed a rapid, low-cost, and readily scalable approach for synthesizing a magnetic porous carbonaceous solid acid catalyst (MPC–SO3H) using biomass waste as a precursor. This method involves fast pyrolysis of biomass preloaded with Fe to produce MPC material; subsequent sulfonation of the obtained material is carried out to prepare a solid acid catalyst (as illustrated in Fig. 66). The primary step is the synthesis of MPC precursor materials including the following steps. Fe preloaded on biomass in the form of FeCl3 is hydrolyzed to Fe hydroxides (FeO(OH)) via a drying process (eqn (37) and (38)). The biomass preloaded with Fe is then subjected to a pyrolysis process in order to be transformed into MPC material. In this process, the biomass preloaded with Fe is thermally decomposed and the volatile components produced are swept out by the carrier gas (N2) and condensed to produce bio-oil. FeO(OH) is reduced to Fe3O4 under mesothermal conditions at 673–873 K by reducing agents, including H2, CO, and amorphous carbon, derived from pyrolysis of biomass (eqn (39) and (41)).724,725 Magnetic solid acids exhibit high acid strength and are readily separable. Therefore, they provide promising replacements for liquid acids in some acid-catalyzed reactions, which can minimize adverse effects on the environment and human health. Therefore, as solid acid catalysts are easily separated and exhibit high catalytic activity, they offer promising applications as low-cost, environmentally friendly, and recyclable acid catalysts in the production of fine chemicals. FeCl3 + 3H2O → Fe(OH)3 + 3HCl↑(37) Fe(OH)3 → FeO(OH) + H2O(38) 6FeO(OH) + 4H2 → 2Fe3O4 + 4H2O↑(39) 6FeO(OH) + 4C → 2Fe3O4 + 4CO↑(40) 6FeO(OH) + 4CO → 2Fe3O4 + 4CO2↑(41)
 Fig. 66  Schematic illustration of the synthesis of a magnetic porous carbonaceous (MPC) solid acid catalyst derived from naturally abundant biomass waste, which was used for three typical acid-catalyzed reactions: esterification of benzyl alcohol and acetic acid (reaction A), dehydration of xylose to produce furfural (reaction B), and hydrolysis of sucrose (reaction C) (reproduced with permission from ref. 723). 
Pyrolysis of biomass or its derivatives is recognized as an efficient and feasible process to selectively transform lignocellulose into a liquid biofuel. However, bio-oil derived from fast pyrolysis of biomass normally contains a large amount of oxygenated compounds. These oxygenated compounds could possess many negative properties such as low heating value, high corrosiveness, high viscosity, and instability; they also greatly limit the application of bio-oil, particularly as a transportation fuel. Hydrocarbons derived from biomass are most attractive because of their high energy density and compatibility with existing infrastructure. Converting lignocellulose into transportation fuels via catalytic fast pyrolysis has attracted much attention.726 The main challenge of this process is the development of active and stable catalysts that can deal with a large variety of decomposition intermediates from lignocellulose.
Close contact between a catalyst and biomass can be achieved by physically mixing a solid catalyst with biomass reduced to fine particles or by impregnating biomass particles with an aqueous solution of metallic salts. In the first case, nanostructured catalysts and nanoparticles prove to be efficient for close contact between the active sites of catalysts and organic macromolecules in biomass, as well as primary molecules generated in the early stages of pyrolysis. High catalytic efficiency is typically expected with metal or metal oxides nanoparticles.727 A great deal of fundamental work has been carried out on the addition of metal species to biomass by impregnation with metal salts solutions, which can explain the role of minerals during pyrolysis of biomass,728–732 modify primary pyrolysis and increase selectivity towards one or more specific products,729–737 or study the effect of fire retardants.737 It is well known that the presence of inorganic species dispersed in biomass can influence the behavior of pyrolysis and thereby the distribution of pyrolysis products. Recent research revealed the merits of the impregnation of wood with Fe or Ni salt solutions in reducing production of tar and increasing production of H2 during pyrolysis at 700 °C.738 With a nickel precursor, an increase of 260% in H2 production was observed compared to a pyrolysis test without a catalyst. This is attributed to in situ generation of crystalline metallic Ni nanoparticles dispersed in the solid fuel during pyrolysis of wood.739 During the wood impregnation step, numerous oxygenated groups in biomacromolecules act as adsorption sites for metal cations in an aqueous medium, providing very high dispersion of metal precursors into the carbon matrix of wood. The surface of the lignocellulosic matrix behaves as a solid polyelectrolyte in the same way as a metal oxide surface, but involving more complex interfacial chemistry in association with the acidity of various types of hydroxyl groups in the biomacromolecules. During the stage of pyrolysis of wood, an amorphous NixOyHz phase is formed, which is easily reduced to metallic nickel (Ni0) via reducing reactions, such as carbothermal reduction, leading to the formation of quasi-monocrystalline Ni0 nanoparticles with a size of 2–4 nm, which mainly depends on the nickel load and the pyrolysis temperature. The formed Ni0 nanocrystallites can also act as catalytic active sites, which enhance production of H2 and conversion of tar during the biomass pyrolysis process.246 Furthermore, an integrated concept of thermochemical conversion of biomass comprises different key reaction steps as shown in Fig. 67, including: (step 1) insertion of a catalytic precursor into solid biomass; (step 2) catalytic pyrolysis of biomass and its main macromolecular constituents; (step 3) catalytically active nanoparticles are generated in situ and well dispersed inside the solid fuel; (step 4) catalytic conversion of nascent tar over the freshly formed nanoparticles; (step 5) catalytic gasification of the nanocomposite char residue; and (step 6) recycling and reuse of the metal catalyst species in the ashes. Each step requires a fundamental understanding of thermochemical conversion of biomass on the nanoscale.740
 Fig. 67  Concept of integrated catalytic biomass gasification via a catalyst/biomass integration strategy (reproduced with permission from ref. 740). 
5.2. Environmental applications
Porous carbon is a commonly used adsorbent material in environmental protection, such as water treatment, owing to its highly developed porosity, excellent adsorption capacity, and high degree of surface reactivity.741 It can effectively adsorb contaminants due to van der Waals forces, electrostatic attraction, or chemical bonds between its surface functional groups and the adsorbate.742,743 However, the adsorption process primarily achieves phase transfer and not transformation of toxic pollutants into non-toxic or low-toxicity substances.744 Therefore, many efforts have been made to synthesize functionalized porous carbons which combine adsorption with detoxification for use in environmental remediation.744,745 In addition, nanotechnology for environmental remediation has been a highly anticipated breakthrough, as growing demand for the limited supply of clean water creates an urgent requirement for technologies that can deliver faster, cleaner, and more affordable water treatment measures.
The application of engineered nanomaterials for environmental remediation came about when iron nanoparticles were observed to rapidly transform a group of persistent groundwater contaminants including trichloroethylene (TCE) and polychlorinated biphenyls (PCBs).746 In recent years, nanoscale zero-valent iron technology has evolved into the most active sub-field of environmental nanotechnology, with remarkable advances seen in the fundamental understanding of the reactivity of nanoscale zero-valent iron with environmental contaminants, surface engineering to enhance transport, and pilot and full-scale field implementation.747 In addition to chlorinated solvents, the contaminants suitable for treatment with nanoscale zero-valent iron include a variety of deleterious organic pollutants such as pesticides, azo dyes, flame retardants, and antibiotics. Moreover, inorganic contaminants are significant, such as nitrates, arsenic, hexavalent chromium, heavy metals, and radionuclides. With an increase in field applications conducted with nanoscale zero-valent iron for in situ groundwater and soil treatment, engineering aspects in association with the stability, mobility, and long-term ecotoxicological impacts of nanoscale zero-valent iron have received increasing attention as well.748 Zhan et al.749 developed a high-throughput method for producing carbonaceous materials embedded with zero-valent iron nanoparticles for environmental remediation of chlorinated hydrocarbons (e.g., TEC) by reductive dechlorination following a simplified redox mechanism (eqn. (42)). While this showed the feasibility of an aerosol process, the reactivity of zero-valent iron is achieved by reduction with NaBH4. In addition, hydrolysis of NaBH4 generates large amounts of hydrogen, impeding the scale-up of this process.750 To retain the merits of using aerosol-based technology to produce iron–carbon composites, an attempt was made to distribute nanoscale zero-valent iron particles throughout a carbon matrix via a facile carbothermal method (as illustrated in Fig. 68A). The iron oxide particles generated by the aerosol process are essentially reduced by the carbon matrix via pyrolysis in an inert atmosphere. The potential distinct advantages of this process include the following: (1) nanoscale zero-valent iron nanoparticles that are highly distributed in the carbon support; (2) reduction of the iron precursor to zero-valent iron via a carbothermal step, instead of adding NaBH4; and (3) the fact that carbothermal treatment introduces high porosity into iron–carbon composites, as part of the carbon is converted into CO and CO2 during reduction of the iron oxide, thus allowing the entry of contaminants to reactive sites. Additional factors include the fact that the starting materials are environmentally benign and economical. The whole process is operated in a semi-continuous method, facilitating large-scale synthesis for eventual in situ application. For instance, Fig. 68C compares the adsorptive capacity of aerosol-based Fe–C composites with those of humic acid (the major natural organic matter in soil) and commercial activated carbon. The adsorption of TCE on Fe–C (∼94.5%) is much higher than that of humic acid (∼30%) and comparable to that of activated carbons (∼95%). The conceptual basis of this technology involves injecting these carbon materials into contaminated sediments saturated with groundwater containing chlorinated hydrocarbons traveling as a plume in the groundwater. As the Fe–C nanoparticles enter the contaminated site, the carbon component acts as an adsorbent for dissolved TCE and concentrates TCE on the particles. Consequently, reaction with TCE can proceed via catalysis by zero-valent iron and Pd, resulting in destruction of the chlorinated compound (Fig. 68B). It is noteworthy that the hydrophobic nature of carbon allows colloidally stabilized particles to be partitioned into interfacial regions when TCE as a bulk phase (in pools within bedrock) is in contact with water.751,752 Anchoring of the particles at such interfaces allows the reaction to continue. The carbothermal process could become an efficient way to modify the internal porosities of particles in carbon-supported catalysts. The aerosol method for generating metal-supported carbons in a facile process therefore has significant applicability for the design of new catalysts. C2HCl3 + 4Fe0 + 5H+ → C2H6 + 4Fe2+ + 3Cl−(42)
 Fig. 68  (A) Schematic of the synthesis of nanoscale zero-valent iron–carbon composites; (B) schematic illustration of Fe–C composites for the adsorption and simultaneous dechlorination of chlorinated hydrocarbon contaminants (TCE); (C) comparison of adsorption capacities of humic acid, Fe–C, and commercially activated carbon (in all experiments, 20 mL of a 20 ppm TCE solution and 0.2 g particles were used) (reproduced with permission from ref. 749). 
It has been mentioned before that iron–carbon composites can be employed for the treatment of inorganic contaminants, especially heavy metal wastewaters. For example, Zhuang et al.753 successfully prepared a porous carbon-encapsulated iron (Fe@PC) composite by carbothermal reduction of Fe(NO3)3 and starch (as illustrated in Fig. 69A). It should be noted that, after being cooled in flowing N2, the sample was washed with dilute HCl to thoroughly remove external Fe and iron oxide. In addition, NaCl served as a solid spacer to prevent the particles from agglomerating. After carbothermal reduction, iron oxide was reduced to metallic iron and covered with a carbonaceous layer. Fe@PC prepared at 750 °C (denoted as Fe@PC-750) had an irregular porous surface (Fig. 69B) with a specific surface area up to 378.71 m2 g−1 and a pore volume up to 0.17 cm3 g−1. As external Fe species had been completely removed by the HCl solution, the residual Fe was mainly distributed in the interior of the bulk particles, instead of being attached to the extra carbon shell, and was not accessible from the outside (EDS mapping in Fig. 69B). It was also confirmed that the carbon layer could protect the Fe particles in harsh conditions. The maximum removal capacity for Cr(VI) of Fe@PC-750 and PC-750 at 25 °C can reach 10.07 mg g−1 and 2.50 mg g−1, respectively (Fig. 69C). This is attributed to the fact that Fe(NO3)3 serves not as a Fe precursor, but as an activating agent at higher temperatures and produces pores on carbon. Compared with PC-750, Fe@PC-750 possessed a much higher specific surface area, and the surface reducing groups on its carbon shell had more opportunity to contact Cr(VI). Moreover, the removal rate first increased sharply with time and reached 70% in 3 min. The removal rate of Cr(VI) also increased with the adsorption temperature (Fig. 69D). Significantly, almost no hysteresis loop was observed in the magnetization curve (Fig. 69E), which indicates that the composite is a superparamagnetic material. Furthermore, its saturation magnetization reached 72.5 emu g−1, which was high enough for magnetic separation. Therefore, the prepared material can be used as an easily recoverable adsorbent to remove heavy metals from wastewater.
 Fig. 69  (A) Schematic of the preparation of Fe@PC composite; (B) SEM image and EDS mapping of Fe@PC; (C) adsorption isotherm of Fe@PC-750 and PC-750 (T = 25 °C, pH = 7.0, m = 0.1 g); (D) effect of adsorption temperature on the removal of Cr(VI) by Fe@PC (pH = 7.0, m = 0.1 g); (E): (a): magnetization curve of Fe@PC-750, (b): I. Attraction of the particles to a permanent magnet; II. Particles dispersed in water (reproduced with permission from ref. 753). 
As one of the two stable forms of chromium (Cr) in the natural environment, Cr(VI) is commonly present as soluble, mobile, and oxidizing oxyanions (CrO42−, HCrO4−, and Cr2O72−) that exhibit high toxicity, causing carcinogenic and mutagenic effects and liver damage.754–757 In contrast, trivalent chromium, (Cr(III)), usually found in the form of Cr(OH)3 or Cr(III) complexes with organic ligands, is less soluble, less mobile, and less toxic.758,759 It is even considered to be a non-toxic essential trace metal in human nutrition (especially in glucose metabolism).760,761 Therefore, it is of great importance to transform Cr(VI) into Cr(III) and fundamentally reduce its toxicity when porous carbons are used to adsorb Cr(VI) from contaminated water.762 Actually, porous carbon can itself weakly reduce Cr(VI) to Cr(III) by redox reactions of its surface functional groups.763,764 In order to promote reduction, some reducing agents such as zero-valent iron, ferrous iron, and iron sulfide765–767 could be introduced onto porous carbon. More recently, several elegant studies associated with an activated carbon/nZVI composite, ordered mesoporous carbon doped with iron, and nanosized magnetite-coated activated carbon have been reported,768–770 whereas limited research has been carried out to integrate ferrous sulfide with porous carbon. Due to the high reducibility provided by both Fe(II) and S(−II), it is far more efficient to reduce Cr(VI) than iron-bearing oxide minerals, and the products generated during redox reactions could greatly decrease the chance of reoxidation of chromium.766 A type of carbon/pyrite composite showing potential for environmental remediation via the removal of TCE has been synthesized by sequential processes, including incipient wetness impregnation of iron, transformation into iron oxides by calcination, and sulfurization by further heat treatment, in addition to the preparation of bituminous-coal-based carbon.771 However, the multi-step synthesis and high-cost raw materials involved still compel us to explore more facile and more economical preparation routes.
Yang et al.772 proposed an in situ carbothermal reduction method for synthesizing a new C/FeS/Fe composite by co-pyrolysis of hemp stems and iron(II) sulfate in flowing N2. Iron(II) sulfate could be directly decomposed and reduced to FeS/Fe nanoparticles embedded in a carbon matrix under a reducing atmosphere, which is generated via carbonization of hemp stems. Furthermore, it acts as an activating agent to develop pores in the carbon matrix. This synthetic process saves time and, more importantly, the porous C/FeS/Fe composite displays good simultaneous adsorption and reduction performance in the removal of Cr(VI) (as illustrated in Fig. 70), which makes it a potential material for the fields of chemical industry and environmental protection. Furthermore, using functionalized carbon composites produced from biomass wastes for wastewater treatment should become a promising sustainable way to achieve environmental treatment using waste.
 Fig. 70  Schematic of C/FeS/Fe composite synthesized by in situ carbothermal reduction method from hemp (Cannabis sativa L.) stems for removal of Cr(VI) (reproduced with permission from ref. 772). 
Magnetic porous carbons with different structures have been widely synthesized. Magnetic carbon nanoparticles with metallic cores (Fe, Co, Ni, or alloys) and graphitic shells (carbon onions, etc.) have been developed by arc discharge, laser ablation, electron irradiation, and pyrolysis processes.773–781 However, their non-porous graphitic shells result in extremely low specific surface areas, which thereby limits the amount of chemically active sites. To overcome this disadvantage, magnetic carbons with high specific surface areas and porosity were generated by impregnation of iron, cobalt, or nickel salts in activated carbon slurry with subsequent calcination.782–784 In spite of the highly enhanced porous features, condensation of metal salts leads to substantial blocking of pore space and reduces the pore dimensions, which usually increases mass transfer resistance. To eliminate pore blocking and improve mass transfer performance, Lu et al.785 reported the synthesis of mesoporous magnetic carbon by a combination of nanocasting and spatially selective deposition of magnetic cobalt nanoparticles. Later, Lee et al.786 and Park et al.787 independently developed a new route for carbonizing carbon sources and metal salts in a mesoporous silica template to produce mesoporous magnetic carbons. In these cases, pore spaces were utilized as nanoreactors for the formation of magnetic carbon composites and pore walls to guarantee the formation of open mesopores. In addition, Sun et al.788 prepared magnetic carbon spheres by direct pyrolysis of an anion exchange resin. The magnetic carbon spheres are sub-millimetre-sized with rough exterior surfaces and microporous bulk regions. The exterior roughness of these large spheres contributes to the formation of macropores, which are beneficial for transportation of guest species to the core of the sphere. Therefore, intensive studies have been conducted in the development of magnetic porous carbon materials with mesopores or macropores.
