Wang et al.789 synthesized a ferromagnetic hierarchical porous carbon (FHPC) for separation of dyes. This FHPC material exhibits a macroporous skeleton consisting of smaller macropores, mesopores, micropores and nickel particles. Moreover, the hierarchical porous texture was formed using Na2CO3 as a salt template and the nickel particles were carbothermally reduced from nickel oxide.
Except for zero-valent iron (Fe0) nanoparticles, Fe3O4 is also non-toxic and can be easily separated by external magnetic forces. When Fe3O4 is encapsulated in carbon, the magnetic Fe3O4 core is protected from attack by dissolved aqueous species. Fe3O4 exhibits good electrochemical performance in reversible ion storage, whereas it displays poor cyclability due to pulverization of the electrode and poor conductivity.790–794 In general, carbon-coated Fe3O4 nanoparticles have become accepted in the field of lithium-ion batteries to overcome these issues. It is an interesting but challenging task to design a facile and scalable procedure for the manufacture of multifunctionalized materials used in energy and environmental applications. More recently, Suma and Deng795 developed a facile one-step hydrothermal preparation of Fe2O3@a-C nanorods (disordered-carbon-coated Fe2O3 nanorods). By heating Fe2O3@a-C nanorods in argon, they could be easily converted into graphitic carbon (g-C)-coated magnetic Fe3O4 (Fe3O4@g-C) nanorods (as illustrated in Fig. 71A). Magnetic Fe3O4@g-C nanorods can reversibly store molecules, using a dye as a model. Consequently, one application of them is as a recyclable and reusable adsorbent for remediation of water (e.g., adsorption of methylene blue in Fig. 71D). Moreover, magnetic Fe3O4@g-C nanorods can be applied as vehicles for controlled drug release in an aqueous environment; in particular, for targeted treatment of infected regions via guided external magnetic forces. The thin graphitic carbon sheaths which encapsulate the high-capacity Fe3O4 cores could actually prevent the cores from having full access to sodium ions. Therefore, a carbon coating commonly used in electrode materials for lithium-ion batteries may not be generally appropriate for electrode materials for future sodium-ion batteries. Further study would be helpful on the utilization and selection of carbon coatings, which is a common strategy to overcome pulverization of electrodes in lithium-ion batteries, for electrodes in future sodium-ion batteries.
 Fig. 71  (A) Schematic of one-step synthesis of Fe2O3@a-C nanorods and their subsequent conversion to Fe3O4@g-C nanorods by calcination in Ar. (B) SEM and TEM images of Fe2O3@a-C nanorods and Fe3O4@g-C nanorods, respectively. (C) XRD patterns of Fe2O3@a-C nanorods and Fe3O4@g-C nanorods after calcination. (D) Magnetic Fe3O4@g-C nanorods as adsorbents: (a) UV-vis spectra of methylene blue solution with increasing adsorption times; inset of (a) shows a magnet used to recover the nanorods in a beaker; (b) normalized methylene blue concentration for increasing adsorption times; inset of (b) shows optical images of the decay in color of the solution over time (reproduced with permission from ref. 795). 
Magnetic transition metal nanocrystals have been highly favored as they are low-cost and make the separation process by means of an external magnetic field easy, thus avoiding conventional filtration or centrifugation procedures, which is a key obstacle preventing industrial-scale application. In spite of their significant virtues, magnetic nanocrystals, especially when small in size, are prone to agglomeration during usage owing to their high surface energy, which significantly lowers their catalytic activity and reduces their lifetime. Therefore, creating large nanocrystals and dispersing them on porous supports or adsorbents is vital for the construction of economical and durable nanocatalysts for wastewater treatment. Furthermore, porous carbonaceous supports have been proved to be superior to oxide carriers, due to their chemical inertness and excellent stability in both acidic and basic conditions. Activated carbons with large surface areas and high pore volumes have been employed as supports/adsorbents. However, the size of micropores restricts their further application owing to the difficulty in transportation of large molecules.796 Therefore, mesoporous carbons with uniform large pores and high surface areas are principally required. Recent advances in the synthesis of nanocrystals on mesoporous carbons mainly focus on the hard-templating approach to infiltrate an appropriate carbon precursor (e.g., furfuryl alcohol or sucrose) and a metal source (e.g., Fe, Co and Ni) into the mesopores of a silica template, accompanied by thermal polymerization and carbonization with dissolution of silica.797–800 Mesoporous carbons with dispersed Ni nanocrystals can be synthesized using SBA-15 coated with 2,3-dihydroxynaphthalene and impregnated with a nickel salt in combination with a heating process.801 However, this multi-step synthetic route involves repeated impregnation with carbon and/or a metal precursor, which greatly restricts widespread application of these composite materials. With respect to soft-templated carbons, incorporation of inorganic species into the carbon framework can be carried out directly via an extra self-assembly process. The addition of metal salts to a reaction mixture containing polymerizable precursors and block copolymer templates can facilitate its transformation into metal nanocrystals embedded in the carbon matrix during thermal treatment.
Nitrophenols are among the most common organic pollutants in industrial and agricultural wastewaters. In particular, 4-nitrophenol (4-NP) is a notorious industrial pollutant exhibiting high solubility and stability in water.802,803 Catalytic reduction of 4-NP to 4-aminophenol (4-AP) using sodium borohydride, which is widely used in sewage treatment, is considered to be the most efficient, green and economical approach804–807 and the reduction product (i.e., 4-AP) has great industrial relevance for the production of aniline and paracetamol.808 Recently, Yang et al.220 synthesized large Ni nanocrystals (30–75 nm, ca. 20 wt%) integrated in situ onto mesoporous carbons via self-assembly of nickel acetate and 8-quinolinol-modified chitosan directed by Pluronic P123 under neutral conditions assisted by tetraethyl orthosilicate before pyrolysis and removal of silica (as illustrated in Fig. 31), which were subsequently used for catalytic reduction of 4-NP. The mechanism of the reduction of 4-NP is illustrated in Fig. 72. (i) The external nickel (tri)oxide layer was rapidly reduced by NaBH4 to give Ni0 species upon mixing. (ii) Because of chemical interaction between the particle surface and the substrate, phenolate ions are adsorbed onto the Ni NC surface when present in the aqueous medium, forming a surface complex, and the strong nucleophile NaBH4, due to its diffusive nature and high electron injection ability, transfers electrons to the substrate with metal particles or a carbonaceous support. This contributes to overcoming the kinetic barrier of the reaction. (iii) Reduction of 4-NP by H2 generated in situ to form the adsorbed product 4-AP, and finally (iv) desorption of the product. The surface of the Ni0 species was reoxidized by air to nickel (tri)oxide when NaBH4 was fully consumed. The rate-determining step is governed by the reaction of the adsorbed species. Adsorption/desorption equilibrium is reached much faster, modeled in terms of a Langmuir isotherm. Furthermore, nickel catalysts can easily be magnetically separated and the size and microstructure of Ni nanocrystals and the morphology of the carbonaceous support are well adjusted via the pyrolysis temperature, which influences the catalytic performances of Ni nanocrystals in reduction of 4-nitrophenol. Ni nanocrystals embedded in mesoporous carbon and pyrolyzed at 750 °C, possessing both twinned microstructure and carbon nanotube morphology, exhibit the highest catalytic efficiency.
 Fig. 72  Langmuir–Hinshelwood mechanistic model for reduction of 4-nitrophenol (4-NP) to 4-aminophenol (4-AP) by NaBH4 using Ni nanocrystals (taken with permission from ref. 680). 
5.3. Solar carbothermal reduction applications in energy
The significant obstacle in carbothermal reduction is energy consumption caused by reduction of oxides to a pure metal/metalloid. Aluminum (Al) and magnesium (Mg) are light metallic elements with stronger metal–oxygen bonds compared with some transition metals such as iron (Fe) and nickel (Ni). Therefore, high electrical energy consumption and potential for global warming are still present in industrial processes. Solar carbothermic reduction can be considered an alternative route, where a significant part of the energy input is provided by concentrated solar radiation, thereby decreasing the CO2 emissions associated with traditional industrial electrolytic and thermal processes.809–815 The main sources of CO2 emissions are twofold: one is associated with electric power generation based on combustion of fossil fuel, the other stems from oxidation of graphite electrodes in electrolytic processes or, alternatively, during generation of the heat supplied in thermal processes. Consequently, renewable electricity can reduce the CO2 footprint only partially in the production of these metals. In addition, solar carbothermic reduction can be considered the endothermic step in redox cycles for solar energy storage combined with gasification of a carbon source. CO released in these processes can be used as a valuable gaseous fuel and reducing agent and its combustion provides additional thermal energy. CO2 finally released during CO combustion does not have an impact on the potential for global warming when biomass charcoal is used as a desirable source of reducing carbon.816–818 Carbothermic reduction of oxides of these metals under vacuum not only reduces the reaction temperatures, but also minimizes the formation of carbides and oxycarbides intermediate compounds.819,820Fig. 73 shows one of the WIS's solar facilities capable of concentrating solar radiation. Its principal optical set-up is shown in Fig. 73a: a heliostat reflects solar energy onto a stationary parabolic dish through an intermediate flat deflecting mirror. Concentrated solar light enters the reactor (Fig. 73b) from above through a spherically shaped quartz window that can withstand the internal vacuum conditions and temperature (Fig. 73d) and then passes through a zirconia aperture placed in the focal plane of the dish.820
 Fig. 73  Solar facility capable of concentrating solar radiation: (a) concentrating optics; (b) photo of the batch solar reactor during cooling after testing; (c) theoretical estimation of the temperature distribution inside the reactor; (d) IR image of the reactor window during testing (taken with permission from ref. 819 and 820). 
With respect to thermal reduction of metal oxides to their elements without the application of electrical energy, a chemical reducing agent is necessary to lower the dissociation temperature. Coal (i.e., coke) and natural gas (i.e., methane) are normally preferred as reducing agents in blast furnace processes, because of their availability and relatively low price. In the presence of carbon, uptake of O2 with the formation of CO brings about reduction of oxides at much lower temperatures; for example, zinc production by solar carbothermic reduction of zinc oxide via the conventional fossil-fuel-based electrolytic or Imperial Smelting processes. Furthermore, the use of solar energy for supplying the enthalpy of the reaction increases the calorific value of the initial reactants by 39%. Solar thermochemical cycles based on metal oxide redox reactions can split H2O and CO2 to produce H2 and CO for catalytic synthesis of liquid fuels for transportation.821 When coupled to direct capture of CO2 from atmospheric air, the solar cycle becomes a carbon-neutral source of hydrocarbon fuels. Among a variety of redox materials investigated for use in thermochemical cycles, the Zn–ZnO redox cycle (Fig. 74A) has been identified as a promising route due to its potential to reach a high solar-to-fuel energy conversion efficiency.822 In the endothermic reduction step, ZnO is thermally dissociated into Zn and O2 at above 2000 K by concentrated solar energy. After being separated from O2, the produced Zn is exothermally reacted with H2O and CO2 in a lower-temperature oxidation step to form syngas (H2 and CO) and metal oxide; the latter is recycled to the solar reactor. As the two steps are decoupled, the production of syngas can be carried out on demand at convenient sites. Thermodynamic analysis can indicate that the solar-to-fuel energy conversion efficiency, defined as the heating value of the produced syngas divided by the total energy input into the cycle, can attain values exceeding 30%. As shown in Fig. 74B, the Solzinc project uses concentrated solar radiation for carbothermal production of zinc.823 Solar-produced zinc (or other metals) can be used as a commodity. Alternatively, zinc is used for flexible (chemical) storage of a large fraction of the solar energy used in its production. Options for solar-produced zinc include: (1) production of hydrogen by reaction with steam in a hydrolysis reactor; (2) production of syngas (H2 and CO) by reaction with steam and CO2. Syngas could be further applied for the synthesis of liquid fuels; (3) generation of electricity in mechanically rechargeable Zn–air batteries or fuel cells with Zn anodes produced from solar-produced Zn. The produced ZnO can again be reduced to Zn in the zinc solar reactor. In summary, zinc serves as a flexible chemical storage material in a cyclic process.
 Fig. 74  (A) Schematic of two-step solar thermochemical cycle based on Zn/ZnO redox reactions to produce H2 and/or CO from H2O and/or CO2; (B) sketch of concepts for use of solar-produced zinc for flexible storage, shown for carbothermic reduction of ZnO (taken with permission from ref. 822 and 823). 
Currently, biomass offers an economically feasible means of replacing fossil-based liquid and gaseous fuels, which are necessary for transportation and industry. Solar energy, on the other hand, is practically limitless but has applications only in the electricity and heating markets. Solar and biomass energy can be combined to produce a much greater amount of fuel from a given quantity of biomass using a reduced metal oxide produced in a solar thermal process to deoxygenate the biomass. Hargus et al.824 proposed a novel route for augmented biofuel production, which indirectly harnesses solar thermal energy to drive deoxygenation of bio-oil. This process, which is termed “looped-oxide catalysis” (LOC), is envisioned as a two-step thermochemical cycle, illustrated in Fig. 75. In one step of the cycle, bio-oil is catalytically upgraded by reaction with a reduced metal oxide, forming a higher-valence oxide and yielding a deoxygenated fuel product. During this upgrading step, the reduced metal oxide offers a bulk source of reducing potential to drive the deoxygenation reaction and a catalytic surface, thereby promoting both the thermodynamics and the kinetics of the reaction. In the opposite step of the cycle, the metal oxide is heated in a solar furnace to the point at which it dissociates into its metal or into a lower-valence metal oxide and oxygen gas, thereby completing the cycle. By the input of solar thermal energy, LOC has the potential to achieve significantly increased fuel yields compared to stand-alone biomass-to-fuels pathways. Notably, other processes can be envisioned for regenerating reduced metal oxides, e.g., carbothermal reduction. Fig. 75B illustrates six exergonic reaction cycles with metal–oxygen bond strengths intermediate between those of the Cd/CdO and SnO/SnO2 pairs in order to increase the metal–oxygen bond strength: Fe/FeO, Fe/Fe3O4, Sn/SnO, Ge/GeO2, Sn/SnO2 and Mo/MoO2. Whereas zero-valent metals allow exergonic deoxygenation, reducing metal oxides may be limited kinetically, due to diffusion of oxygen through a solid metallic phase (Fe and Mo), or complicated due to mixing of liquid metal with the electrolyte (Sn and Ge). Regarding LOC upgrading of acetic acid (as shown in Fig. 75C and D), the formation of acetaldehyde with comparable selectivity via LOC with zinc metal to that via HDO with zinc oxide suggests that surface oxygen vacancies are the catalytically relevant sites in both processes. Although LOC would face various challenges in development to an industrial-scale process, especially in materials handling and thermal management, it has inherent advantages over catalytic HDO of bio-oils that makes LOC a strong candidate as a complementary or alternative process. After decades of research, solar thermal processing is mature to the extent that use of solar thermochemical cycles integrated with carbothermal reduction for upgrading bio-oil is technologically feasible and merits exploration.
 Fig. 75  (A) Overview of the looped-oxide catalysis (LOC) thermochemical cycle for deoxygenation of bio-oil feedstocks (MO: a metal oxide, M: the corresponding zero-valent metal or a lower-valence metal oxide). (B) Volcano-type plot of LOC cycles, with five candidate cycles annotated. The inset figure shows the volcano-type plot over its entire range of 80 cycles included in this analysis. (C) LOC experiment. (D) HDO control experiment (reproduced with permission from ref. 824). 
6. Summary and outlook
This review aims to present recent progress in carbothermal synthesis of commonly used transition-metal-functionalized nanostructures ranging from one-dimensional nanostructures to three-dimensional nanoparticles, then highlight their applications in the fields of energy and the environment. A significant challenge in materials science at the moment is to quantify sustainability. In the future, researchers will need to be creative and realistic and consider all contributions when it comes to designing a truly sustainable material, from precursors to processing (catalysts, number of steps, energy requirements, solvents), CO2 emissions, harmful releases as well as end of life. Using renewable resources that do not compete with the food chain is clearly important, but it is not the only condition for making a sustainable material. Carbothermal reduction can be used as a facile route for the synthesis of various novel materials, especially in terms of transition-metal-functionalized materials. In particular, energy and electronics materials such as ZnO, MnO2, and LiFePO4 with different carbon–metal nanostructures have been extensively synthesized by means of various carbothermal reduction methods integrated with other auxiliary technologies (e.g., sol–gel, hydrothermal process), which can be well established industrially because of their low-cost starting materials. In addition, a variety of carbon sources could be considered comparatively low-cost carbon precursors for the synthesis of carbonaceous functional materials, such as carbon-coated magnetic nanoparticles (e.g., Co3O4@C, Fe3O4@C, FeC3–C). These materials have great potential for use in energy and environmental applications. Carbothermal reduction methods possess some incomparable advantages such as convenience, relatively low cost, and good repeatability for commercial applications. However, they often require a relatively high temperature for sustaining the carbothermic reaction. Consequently, novel structures can also be derived from abundant renewable carbon precursors. Examples include lignocellulosic biomass or other components in food or agricultural wastes (i.e., carbohydrates, cellulose, hemicellulose, lignin, chitin, inorganics, proteins, etc.). Thermochemical conversion of biomass including pyrolysis in an inert environment has been developed for production of energy and carbon materials. Furthermore, it is still a potential challenge to simultaneously produce high-quality biofuel products and synthesize value-added functionalized materials via in situ carbothermal reduction. This review will be a powerful resource to stimulate the rapid development of sustainable transition-metal-functionalized nanostructures via carbothermal reduction, particularly for strengthening efforts towards novel nanostructured materials for clean energy and environmental applications in the future sustainable society.

Table Content:
Yafei Shen	Yafei Shen received his MSc degree (2012) from Shanghai Jiaotong University (SJTU), PR China. In this period, his research interests include energy & chemical engineering. He was awarded the excellent master dissertation in Shanghai, PR China. He received his PhD (2015) at Tokyo Institute of Technology (TokyoTech), Japan, supported by China Scholarship Council (CSC). After that, he worked as a lecturer in SJTU. Currently, he is hired as a professor in Nanjing University of Information Science & Technology (NUIST), PR China. His research interests mainly include tar and fuel gas cleaning, thermochemical conversion of wastes, and carbonaceous materials for energy and environmental application. He has authored more than 30 academic publications in the fields of energy and environment.
